Al-Husayn's Multiple Reasons for Creation
Al-Husayn enumerates multiple reasons for creation and argues against a single-purpose view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Husayn al-Najjar, God created the world for a multiplicity of reasons, not just a single benefit.

Core Idea
Creation serves many purposes, sometimes even opposite ones.

Al-Husayn al-Najjar responds to the question of why God created the world. He proposes that creation has multiple, layered reasons, not just one. This passage lists those reasons and argues against limiting creation to immediate benefits. Understanding this helps us see divine wisdom in the variety and complexity of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full argument. Notice the list of reasons and the conditional argument that follows.
2
Identify the multiple reasons
The passage begins with a list of reasons for creation. Make a mental list: indication, proof, lesson, admonition, blessing, mercy, sustenance, support, and being an agent for needs. Note that some things are a blessing for one and a trial for another.
These are not exhaustive but represent categories.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Al-Husayn then argues: if creation were solely for interests and benefits, no change, delay, or variation would be possible. But since God created things beyond human imagination and hidden from sight, it shows the matter is not that simple. Instead, God places things in their proper context and can turn benefit into harm and vice versa.
This rebuts the idea of a single purpose.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
The lesson ends with the affirmation that God's wisdom encompasses multiple purposes, some known, some hidden. This teaches humility in understanding divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الحسين في جواب هذا السؤال: إنه خلق لأسباب تكثر، منها دلالة وحجة، ثم عبرة وعظة، ثم نعمة ورحمة، ثم غذاء وقوام، ومتصرف في الحوائج؛ ومنه ما خلق نعمة لأحد بلية على آخر. قال: ولو خلق ابتداء الخلق للمصالح والمنافع لاغير لم يكن يجوز تقديم شيء ولا تأخيره ولا خلق شيء قبل خلق الممتحن، ولا قلب أمر من حال إلى حال، ولا زيادة ولا نقصان. وإذ خلق الله من الخلائق مالا يحيط به الأوهام واستتر عن نظرة الأنام، ثبت أن الأمر ليس على ذلك، لكنه في وضع الأشياء مواضعها، وصرف الأمور من النفع إلى الضرر، والضرر إلى النفع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And al-Husayn said in answer to this question: He created for many reasons, among them: indication and proof, then lesson and admonition, then blessing and mercy, then sustenance and support, and an agent in needs; and among it is what was created as a blessing for one is a trial for another. He said: And if the beginning of creation was solely for interests and benefits, it would not be permissible to advance anything or delay it, nor to create anything before the creation of the tested one, nor to turn a matter from one state to another, nor increase nor decrease. And since God created from the creatures what imaginations cannot encompass and is hidden from the sight of mankind, it is established that the matter is not like that, but rather He places things in their places, and turns matters from benefit to harm, and from harm to benefit. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage records al-Husayn al-Najjar's response to the question of why God created. He lists multiple reasons: for indication (dalala) and proof (hujja), for lesson ('ibra) and admonition ('idha), for blessing (ni'ma) and mercy (rahma), for sustenance (ghidha') and support (qiwam), and as an agent in needs. Moreover, what is a blessing for one person may be a trial (baliyya) for another. He then argues that if creation were solely for interests and benefits, no change, delay, or variation would be permissible. But since God created things beyond human imagination and hidden from sight, the matter is not limited to immediate benefits. Instead, God places everything in its proper context and turns matters from benefit to harm and vice versa, demonstrating a broader wisdom.

Key Terms
دلالة (indication)
حجة (proof)
عبرة (lesson)
عظة (admonition)
نعمة (blessing)
بلية (trial)
مصالح (interests)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation has only one purpose, such as benefit or test.
Correction: Al-Husayn shows that creation has multiple, sometimes opposite purposes (e.g., what is a blessing for one is a trial for another).

Checkpoint
What does al-Husayn say about something created as a blessing for one person?
A) It is always a blessing for everyone
B) It may be a trial for another
C) It cannot be a trial
D) It is only for the tested ones

Key Takeaway
God's creation serves multiple purposes, some known and some hidden, and what is a blessing for one may be a trial for another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you experienced something that felt like a trial. Can you see how, according to al-Husayn's argument, that trial might also be a blessing or lesson for you? What one attitude shift can you make today to view it as part of God's broader wisdom?
?
The passage says God turns matters from benefit to harm and from harm to benefit. Is there an area of your life where you are clinging to a perceived benefit that may actually be harming you spiritually? What step will you take to surrender that to God?
?
Al-Husayn lists multiple reasons for creation, including that some things are signs and proofs. When was the last time you looked at a creature or event as a sign pointing to God, rather than just a utility for yourself? What practical change can you make this week to see more of God's signs in your daily life?

The First Wisdom: Trial Through Present Harm and Benefit
Maturidi presents the first wisdom: harm and benefit as a didactic trial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the creation of both harmful and beneficial things serves as a visible trial to teach humans the pleasure of reward and pain of punishment.

Core Idea
Visible examples of harm and benefit train our minds to value the unseen.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by acknowledging that human minds cannot fully grasp divine wisdom, yet he identifies clear aspects. The first wisdom is that harmful and beneficial things in the world serve as a trial, making the consequences of obedience and disobedience tangible. This lesson explores that foundational idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage about the first wisdom. Notice how Maturidi connects present harm and benefit to reward and punishment.
2
Understand the purpose of the trial
Reflect on why a visible example is needed. Humans naturally aim at consequences, so Allah provides tangible examples to help imagine the promised rewards and punishments.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Consider how this first wisdom sets the stage for the other wisdoms. It shows that everything in creation has a purpose related to guidance and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : الحكمة في خلق الحيّات والجواهر الضارة - وإن كانت العقول تقصر عن بلوغ كنه حكمة الربوبية، على ما سبق القول في لزوم الحكمة لكل شيء من الوجه الذي خلقه الله، وإن لم يعرف مائيتها - يكون من وجوه. الأول، المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين، ليُعْلَم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية. إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال. فجعل لها مثالاً من العيان ليتصور الموعود في الأوهام، فيسهل به السبيل. والله الموفق. والثاني، أن المحنة هي تحمّل المؤنة التي تسهل وتصعب على البدن بالنظر والفكر. والناس في تكلّف النظر والفكر يختلفون؛ لأنه ليست لهما منفعة حاضرة، وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات. وتحمّل مثله على البدن عسير، وفي التقصير فيهما اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يُعقِب المعاداة والمجاوبة، وفي الموافقة موالاة ومسالمة. فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيما خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشرى زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء؛ حتى إذا ابْتُلُوا بمثله في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم العبادات والأخلاق المحمودة للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر. والله أعلم. وأيضا إن الخلق ـ على اختلاف جوهرهم في المضار والمنافع ـ جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدبّر لهم حكيم عليم وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فيكون في ذلك بيان عجيب حكمته: أن جمع جمع بين الضار والنافع والخير والشر، على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحداً. وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذلّل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشي والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله؛ بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته: تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وليُعْلَم من تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن أن تمسته الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر. وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء. مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها. ما فيها من الإحراق، ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية. والماء يجوز أن يكون من ذلك النار، مع به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه. وكذلك لا يوجد جوهر مر أو سام إلا وفيه دواء للداء المعضل؛ ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر أو الخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. ما فيه وجهان، أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليُرْجَى ويُخاف. ومن لا يكون كذلك لا يتم الأمر به؛ لأنه لا يُرْهَب منه ولا يُرْغَب فيه عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضاً. والثاني ليتم العبر وليصح الأمر والنهي، فيكون للنظر والفكر مجال في الأمرين؛ ولأنهما عظة بهما وعبرة ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances — even though minds fall short of attaining the reality of the wisdom of lordship, as previously stated about the necessity of wisdom for everything in the aspect in which Allah created it, even if its essence is not known — is from among aspects. First: the trial by means of present harm and benefit, so that by them the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience may be known. For creatures are disposed by nature to aim at consequences in actions. So He made for them a visible example, that the promised may be visualized in minds, thus facilitating the path. And Allah is the granter of success. Second: the trial is the bearing of hardship which becomes easy or difficult for the body through reflection and thought. People differ in undertaking reflection and thought because they have no immediate benefit, and they are an occupation from pleasures and desires. Bearing the likes of them upon the body is difficult, and in negligence of them there is difference and separation, and that leads to enmity and disputation, while in agreement there is friendship and peace. So Allah made for them, in what He created for them, a likeness of enemies in the harms contained within them, and an example of friends in the benefits, so that it might be a glad tiding and a deterrent for them to become accustomed to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends; until if they are tested with the like of it in their essence, they know its manner of caution, readiness, assistance, and victory. And similarly, children are commanded when they bear the capacity of their worship and praiseworthy morals to become accustomed, so that the path of that becomes easy for them at the time of obligation. So the like of that is in the creation of what was mentioned. And Allah knows best. Also: the creation — despite the difference of their essence in harms and benefits — Allah has made them, in indicating a wise, knowing Creator and His oneness, like a single essence in agreement in respect of indication and witness. And there is no power except with Allah. So in that there is a wondrous explanation of His wisdom: that He gathered together harm and benefit, good and evil, despite their contradiction, in indicating His oneness and witnessing to His lordship as one. Also: He created that to humble the tyrants and kings, so that they may know thereby their weakness, and that they may not be deluded by the abundance of retainers and soldiers and thus transgress the limits of Allah; by what they see of sovereignty in His power: giving authority to whom He wills over whom He wills. And there is no power except with Allah. And that it may be known by one who contemplates His creation upon the essence of harm and benefit regarding His independence and exaltedness from being touched by needs; because describing Him from that is that His action only emerges in ways that benefit and do not harm. And that His power over whatever He wills may be known. Along with that, there is no harmful substance but that there are in it benefits that creatures are incapable of encompassing its reality. What it contains of burning, there is in it the correction of foods. And water may be from that, fire, with it the life of every living soul and its destruction. And similarly, there is no bitter or poisonous substance except that there is in it a cure for the stubborn disease; so that the observer may know that the statement that evil is in the substance or good is a false error. Rather, every substance contains both harm and benefit, and in that is the greatest signs of tawhid. Within it there are two aspects: one is the complete power over possessing what harms and benefits, so that He may be hoped and feared. And whoever is not like that, affairs are not accomplished through him; because neither fear nor desire is directed towards him, and one who possesses both aspects may overcome him. The second is that lessons may be completed and command and prohibition may be sound, so that there may be a field for reflection and thought concerning both matters; and because they are a warning and a lesson. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of multiple wisdoms (wujūh) behind the creation of harmful substances such as snakes and poisons. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins with a preamble acknowledging that human minds cannot fully comprehend the depth of divine wisdom, but there are still discernible aspects. He then lists them in sequence.

First wisdom: The creation of both harmful and beneficial things serves as a trial (miḥna) to make people taste the pleasure of reward and the pain of punishment. Humans naturally seek consequences, so visible examples help them imagine the promised rewards and punishments, facilitating the path of obedience.

Second wisdom: Life is a burden that requires reflection and thought, which are difficult because they have no immediate benefit and distract from pleasures. People vary in undertaking this effort, leading to enmity or friendship. By creating harmful and beneficial entities, Allah provides a training ground for dealing with enemies and friends. Just as children are gradually trained in worship, these created things accustom people to the proper conduct before the time of full obligation.

Third wisdom: Despite their differences, all created things unanimously point to a single wise Creator. The coexistence of harm and benefit, contradictory as they are, yet both pointing to the same lordship, is a wondrous proof of tawhid.

Fourth wisdom: Harmful things humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness and preventing them from transgressing Allah's limits due to their power, as they see that Allah can empower anyone over anyone.

Fifth wisdom: Contemplating the nature of harm and benefit leads to knowledge of Allah's independence and transcendence above being touched by needs. His actions always benefit and never harm in the ultimate sense.

Sixth wisdom: This shows His complete power; even harmful substances contain hidden benefits (e.g., fire burns but also cooks food; water gives life and causes death; poisons can be medicines). This refutes the false notion that evil or good inheres in substances absolutely—rather, each substance has both aspects, which is the greatest sign of tawhid.

Finally, in all this there are two further aspects: (1) It demonstrates Allah's complete power to grant benefit or harm, making Him alone worthy of hope and fear. Without both, His authority would be incomplete. (2) It provides lessons and establishes the validity of command and prohibition, giving room for reflection and warning.

Key Terms
محنة
الضار والنافع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that harmful things have no purpose and are merely evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows they serve a pedagogical purpose: they teach about reward and punishment through tangible experience.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did Allah create present harm and benefit?
A) To test the patience of the prophets only
B) To provide visible examples of reward and punishment
C) To cause suffering for no reason
D) To show that matter is inherently evil

Key Takeaway
The presence of both harm and benefit in the world is a deliberate trial that makes the consequences of obedience and disobedience tangible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a harmful situation, do you immediately recognize it as a trial designed to teach you about the pain of disobedience? Or do you react with complaint? How can you train your heart to see divine wisdom in every difficulty?
?
If your life were filled only with benefit and never harm, would you still be motivated to obey Allah? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to worldly comfort rather than to Allah Himself?
?
The passage says visible examples facilitate the path. What visible example in your life—benefit or harm—has most powerfully reminded you of the afterlife? Have you allowed that reminder to change your daily actions? If not, why not?

Maturidi presents the second wisdom: training through hardship and examples of enemies and friends.

مائيتها - يكون من وجوه. الأول، المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين، ليُعْلَم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية. إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال. فجعل لها مثالاً من العيان ليتصور الموعود في الأوهام، فيسهل به السبيل. والله الموفق. والثاني، أن المحنة هي تحمّل المؤنة التي تسهل وتصعب على البدن بالنظر والفكر. والناس في تكلّف النظر والفكر يختلفون؛ لأنه ليست لهما منفعة حاضرة، وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات. وتحمّل مثله على البدن عسير، وفي التقصير فيهما اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يُعقِب المعاداة والمجاوبة، وفي الموافقة موالاة ومسالمة. فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيما خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشرى زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء؛ حتى إذا ابْتُلُوا بمثله في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم العبادات والأخلاق المحمودة للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر. والله أعلم. وأيضا إن الخلق ـ على اختلاف جوهرهم في المضار والمنافع ـ جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدبّر لهم حكيم عليم وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فيكون في ذلك بيان عجيب حكمته: أن جمع جمع بين الضار والنافع والخير والشر، على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحداً. وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذلّل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشي والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله؛ بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته: تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وليُعْلَم من تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن أن تمسته الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر. وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء. مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها. ما فيها من الإحراق، ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية. والماء يجوز أن يكون من ذلك النار، مع به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه. وكذلك لا يوجد جوهر مر أو سام إلا وفيه دواء للداء المعضل؛ ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر أو الخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. ما فيه وجهان، أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليُرْجَى ويُخاف. ومن لا يكون كذلك لا يتم الأمر به؛ لأنه لا يُرْهَب منه ولا يُرْغَب فيه عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضاً. والثاني ليتم العبر وليصح الأمر والنهي، فيكون للنظر والفكر مجال في الأمرين؛ ولأنهما عظة بهما وعبرة ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances — even though minds fall short of attaining the reality of the wisdom of lordship, as previously stated about the necessity of wisdom for everything in the aspect in which Allah created it, even if its essence is not known — is from among aspects. First: the trial by means of present harm and benefit, so that by them the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience may be known. For creatures are disposed by nature to aim at consequences in actions. So He made for them a visible example, that the promised may be visualized in minds, thus facilitating the path. And Allah is the granter of success. Second: the trial is the bearing of hardship which becomes easy or difficult for the body through reflection and thought. People differ in undertaking reflection and thought because they have no immediate benefit, and they are an occupation from pleasures and desires. Bearing the likes of them upon the body is difficult, and in negligence of them there is difference and separation, and that leads to enmity and disputation, while in agreement there is friendship and peace. So Allah made for them, in what He created for them, a likeness of enemies in the harms contained within them, and an example of friends in the benefits, so that it might be a glad tiding and a deterrent for them to become accustomed to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends; until if they are tested with the like of it in their essence, they know its manner of caution, readiness, assistance, and victory. And similarly, children are commanded when they bear the capacity of their worship and praiseworthy morals to become accustomed, so that the path of that becomes easy for them at the time of obligation. So the like of that is in the creation of what was mentioned. And Allah knows best. Also: the creation — despite the difference of their essence in harms and benefits — Allah has made them, in indicating a wise, knowing Creator and His oneness, like a single essence in agreement in respect of indication and witness. And there is no power except with Allah. So in that there is a wondrous explanation of His wisdom: that He gathered together harm and benefit, good and evil, despite their contradiction, in indicating His oneness and witnessing to His lordship as one. Also: He created that to humble the tyrants and kings, so that they may know thereby their weakness, and that they may not be deluded by the abundance of retainers and soldiers and thus transgress the limits of Allah; by what they see of sovereignty in His power: giving authority to whom He wills over whom He wills. And there is no power except with Allah. And that it may be known by one who contemplates His creation upon the essence of harm and benefit regarding His independence and exaltedness from being touched by needs; because describing Him from that is that His action only emerges in ways that benefit and do not harm. And that His power over whatever He wills may be known. Along with that, there is no harmful substance but that there are in it benefits that creatures are incapable of encompassing its reality. What it contains of burning, there is in it the correction of foods. And water may be from that, fire, with it the life of every living soul and its destruction. And similarly, there is no bitter or poisonous substance except that there is in it a cure for the stubborn disease; so that the observer may know that the statement that evil is in the substance or good is a false error. Rather, every substance contains both harm and benefit, and in that is the greatest signs of tawhid. Within it there are two aspects: one is the complete power over possessing what harms and benefits, so that He may be hoped and feared. And whoever is not like that, affairs are not accomplished through him; because neither fear nor desire is directed towards him, and one who possesses both aspects may overcome him. The second is that lessons may be completed and command and prohibition may be sound, so that there may be a field for reflection and thought concerning both matters; and because they are a warning and a lesson. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of multiple wisdoms (wujūh) behind the creation of harmful substances such as snakes and poisons. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins with a preamble acknowledging that human minds cannot fully comprehend the depth of divine wisdom, but there are still discernible aspects. He then lists them in sequence.

First wisdom: The creation of both harmful and beneficial things serves as a trial (miḥna) to make people taste the pleasure of reward and the pain of punishment. Humans naturally seek consequences, so visible examples help them imagine the promised rewards and punishments, facilitating the path of obedience.

Second wisdom: Life is a burden that requires reflection and thought, which are difficult because they have no immediate benefit and distract from pleasures. People vary in undertaking this effort, leading to enmity or friendship. By creating harmful and beneficial entities, Allah provides a training ground for dealing with enemies and friends. Just as children are gradually trained in worship, these created things accustom people to the proper conduct before the time of full obligation.

Third wisdom: Despite their differences, all created things unanimously point to a single wise Creator. The coexistence of harm and benefit, contradictory as they are, yet both pointing to the same lordship, is a wondrous proof of tawhid.

Fourth wisdom: Harmful things humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness and preventing them from transgressing Allah's limits due to their power, as they see that Allah can empower anyone over anyone.

Fifth wisdom: Contemplating the nature of harm and benefit leads to knowledge of Allah's independence and transcendence above being touched by needs. His actions always benefit and never harm in the ultimate sense.

Sixth wisdom: This shows His complete power; even harmful substances contain hidden benefits (e.g., fire burns but also cooks food; water gives life and causes death; poisons can be medicines). This refutes the false notion that evil or good inheres in substances absolutely—rather, each substance has both aspects, which is the greatest sign of tawhid.

Finally, in all this there are two further aspects: (1) It demonstrates Allah's complete power to grant benefit or harm, making Him alone worthy of hope and fear. Without both, His authority would be incomplete. (2) It provides lessons and establishes the validity of command and prohibition, giving room for reflection and warning.

Key Terms
محنة
المؤنة
النظر والفكر
الأعداء والأولياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think harmful things are only for punishment.
Correction: Maturidi shows they serve as training tools for dealing with enemies and developing virtues.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi compare the gradual training from harmful/beneficial things to?
A) The training of soldiers
B) The training of children in worship and morals
C) The growth of plants
D) The education of scholars

Key Takeaway
Harmful and beneficial things serve as practical training for humans to learn how to interact with enemies and friends, just as children are trained in worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you see it as a random punishment or as a training from Allah to teach you how to deal with enemies? How will this understanding change your reaction next time you encounter a difficulty?
?
Think of a recent argument. Did you treat the other person as an enemy? Maturidi says harmful things are likenesses of enemies to train you. What does that tell you about your actual enemy? How can this insight transform your approach to conflict?

The Unifying Wisdom: Pointing to Tawhid and Other Wisdoms
Maturidi presents additional wisdoms: unanimous indication of tawhid, humbling tyrants, divine independence, hidden benefits, and two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand multiple wisdoms: how diverse creations point to one Creator, humble tyrants, show Allah's independence, contain hidden benefits, and demonstrate complete power for hope and fear.

Core Idea
Diverse, contradictory creations all point to the same wise Creator.

Maturidi concludes with several more wisdoms. Despite differences in harmful and beneficial essences, all created things unanimously indicate a single wise Creator. Furthermore, harmful things humble tyrants, show Allah's transcendence, contain hidden benefits (like poisons as medicines), and demonstrate His complete power over harm and benefit, making Him alone worthy of hope and fear. This passage also affirms that no substance is purely good or evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأيضا إن الخلق' to the end. Notice the multiple points Maturidi makes.
2
Identify the unifying indication
See how Maturidi argues that despite contradictions, all things agree in pointing to one Creator. This is a profound sign of tawhid.
3
Reflect on hidden benefits
Consider the example of fire and water, and how poisons can be medicines. This shows that harm and benefit are relative.
4
Understand the two aspects
The passage ends with two aspects: Allah's complete power to give harm and benefit, and the completion of lessons for command and prohibition.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : الحكمة في خلق الحيّات والجواهر الضارة - وإن كانت العقول تقصر عن بلوغ كنه حكمة الربوبية، على ما سبق القول في لزوم الحكمة لكل شيء من الوجه الذي خلقه الله، وإن لم يعرف مائيتها - يكون من وجوه. الأول، المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين، ليُعْلَم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية. إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال. فجعل لها مثالاً من العيان ليتصور The faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances — even though minds fall short of attaining the reality of the wisdom of lordship, as previously stated about the necessity of wisdom for everything in the aspect in which Allah created it, even if its essence is not known — is from among aspects. And Allah is the granter of success. Second: the trial is the bearing of hardship which becomes easy or difficult for the body through reflection and thought. People differ in undertaking reflection and thought because they have no immediate benefit, and they are an occupation from pleasures and desires. Bearing the likes of them upon the body is difficult, and in negligence of them there is difference and separation, and that leads to enmity and disputation, while in agreement there is friendship and peace. So Allah made for them, in what He created for them, a likeness of enemies in the harms contained within them, and an example of friends in the benefits, so that it might be a glad tiding and a deterrent for them to become accustomed to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends; until if they are tested with the like of it in their essence, they know its manner of caution, readiness, assistance, and victory. And that His power over whatever He wills may be known. Along with that, there is no harmful substance but that there are in it benefits that creatures are incapable of encompassing its reality. Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of multiple wisdoms (wujūh) behind the creation of harmful substances such as snakes and poisons. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins with a preamble acknowledging that human minds cannot fully comprehend the depth of divine wisdom, but there are still discernible aspects. People vary in undertaking this effort, leading to enmity or friendship. By creating harmful and beneficial entities, Allah provides a training ground for dealing with enemies and friends. Just as children are gradually trained in worship, these created things accustom people to the proper conduct before the time of full obligation.

Third wisdom: Despite their differences, all created things unanimously point to a single wise Creator. The coexistence of harm and benefit, contradictory as they are, yet both pointing to the same lordship, is a wondrous proof of tawhid.

Fourth wisdom: Harmful things humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness and preventing them from transgressing Allah's limits due to their power, as they see that Allah can empower anyone over anyone.

Fifth wisdom: Contemplating the nature of harm and benefit leads to knowledge of Allah's independence and transcendence above being touched by needs. His actions always benefit and never harm in the ultimate sense.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that evil is inherent in certain substances (e.g., poisons are purely evil).
Correction: Maturidi argues that every substance contains both harm and benefit; calling something purely evil or good is false. The greatest sign of tawhid is that contradictions coexist and point to one Creator.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the coexistence of harm and benefit in various substances indicate?
A) That matter is inherently contradictory
B) That there is no absolute good or evil in substances
C) That evil is more powerful than good
D) That humans should avoid all harmful things

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of harm and benefit in creation is a profound sign of tawhid, showing that no substance is purely good or evil and that all points to Allah's wisdom and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a harmful situation or a 'bad' event in your life, do you immediately question Allah's wisdom, or do you pause to look for the hidden benefit and the lesson it carries? What would change if you consistently sought the wisdom behind every trial?
?
The passage says that harmful things humble tyrants. Is there any area of your life where you feel 'powerful' or in control—perhaps over others, over your health, or over your wealth? How does this lesson expose the weakness in that feeling and call you to rely only on Allah?
?
This lesson teaches that no substance is purely evil and every harm contains a hidden good. Can you think of a recent 'harm' you experienced that, upon reflection, contained a benefit you had not seen? How might remembering this reshape your reaction to future difficulties?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The First Source of Doubt: Blind Following
Maturidi enumerates the first source of doubt: blind following (taqlid).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi identifies taqlid (blind following) as a primary obstacle to recognizing the Creator.

Core Idea
Blind following prevents reflection on clear proofs.

Maturidi has already laid out clear proofs for the Creator. Yet humans still fall into doubt. The first source is taqlid: the habit of following others without thought. This lesson explains how taqlid works and why it is dangerous.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text describing the first source of doubt. Notice how Maturidi details the psychological attachment to taqlid.
The passage uses phrases like 'ألفت نفسه به' (the soul is habituated to it).
2
Identify the consequences of taqlid
Look for the outcomes Maturidi mentions: leaving reflection on proofs, turning to desires, trusting others, etc.
3
Reflect on the depth of taqlid
Consider that taqlid is not just intellectual laziness; it involves desires, habits, and social bonds. Why does Maturidi emphasize this?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text

فرأيت الشبهة اعترضت البشر من بعد ما بينا مما يجب أن يكون به دفع الشبهة لمن تصح نفسه، من أوجه ثلاثة. أحدها التقليد بمن ألفت نفسه به ومالت إليه؛ فترك التفكر في الأدلة وأقبل على أماني النفس ثقة بهم أو رغبة في صحبتهم والوصول بهم إلى شهوات النفس، أو إنهاما لآرائهم أن تهيئ بهم إلى رشد، أو إنهاما لإنعامهم وغيره من أسباب السعادة؛ حتى يبلغ به العياد بشر نفسه وسوء عاداتها. والثاني نظرهم إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس، فوجدوه يتقلب من حال إلى حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء لا عن شيء والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، والشاهد عندهم هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا. فمنهم من يقول : على هذا أمر العالم في الأزل؛ لكنهم اختلفوا فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا، من غير أن يكون له صنع. وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع : إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاضلها؛ وسماها قوم «هيولى».
Then I saw that doubt (shubha) confronted humans after what we have explained, by which doubt must be repelled for one whose soul is sound, from three aspects. One of them is taqlid (blind following) of those to whom the soul is habituated and inclined; so he leaves reflection on the proofs and turns to the desires of the soul, trusting them or desiring their company and attaining through them the pleasures of the soul, or suspecting their opinions to prepare through them to guidance, or suspecting their favors and other causes of happiness; until habituation reaches him with the evil of his soul and the badness of his habits. The second is their looking at existence which falls under the senses, and they found it changing from state to state by materials and nourishment, and one thing generating from another, and they thought that things coming from nothing and branches from no origin is impossible in existence; because they did not witness that, and the witnessed is for them the evidence of the unseen. Then they split. Some of them say: on this basis the matter of the world is eternal; but they disagreed, some making it so as we have explained, without it having a maker. And on this emerges the school of the Naturalists (ashab al-taba'i'): that disparity and difference is according to the difference of natures and their superiority; and some call it 'hyle' (hayula).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage classifies the three sources of doubt (shubuhat) that prevent people from accepting the truth of a single Creator. Maturidi has already presented clear proofs; now he diagnoses why people still err. The first source is taqlid (blind following): people become attached to their mentors or traditions and stop reflecting on evidence, instead following their desires. The second source is faulty empirical reasoning: people look at the physical world and see constant change through materials and generation, so they assume that everything must come from a prior material cause. They argue that creation from nothing (ex nihilo) is impossible because they have never observed it, and they treat the observed (al-shahid) as the sole guide for the unseen (al-gha'ib). This leads them to believe the world is eternal and uncreated. This error leads to the Naturalist school which attributes all variation to the inherent natures (taba'i') or to a primordial matter called 'hyle' (hayula). Maturidi does not specify the third source here; the passage cuts off, but the two given are clearly delineated. The commentary follows the author's order: identifying the three, then explaining the first (taqlid) in detail, then the second (empirical reasoning) and its consequences.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
شبهة (shubha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid is simply intellectual laziness or lack of education.
Correction: Maturidi shows that taqlid involves deep psychological attachment and desire, making it a willful turning away from evidence, not just passivity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does taqlid cause a person to abandon?
A) Their community
B) Reflection on proofs
C) Their desires
D) Their habits

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is a psychological barrier rooted in habituation and desire that prevents engagement with rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Who are the people or groups you are habituated to trust so deeply that you've never seriously questioned their teachings using proofs? What would it cost you to examine that trust today?
?
When you find yourself defending a belief emotionally rather than rationally, can you identify which 'desire of the soul' (company, status, comfort) is driving that reaction? Will you surrender it to seek truth?
?
If you honestly tracked your habits this week, where did you choose comfort and familiarity over reflection on evidence—even in small matters? What would it take to break that habit starting tomorrow?

The Second Source of Doubt: Faulty Empirical Reasoning
Maturidi explains the second source of doubt: faulty reasoning from sensory observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reliance on sensory observation alone can lead to the denial of creation ex nihilo and belief in the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
The fallacy of equating 'unseen' with 'impossible' leads to naturalism.

The second source of doubt is a flawed method of reasoning. People observe constant change in the physical world and conclude that everything must come from prior matter. They reject the possibility of creation from nothing because they have never seen it. Maturidi explains how this leads to the doctrine of the eternity of matter and the naturalist school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text describing the second source. Note how Maturidi describes the observation and its misinterpretation.
Focus on the phrase 'الشاهد عندهم هو دليل الغائب'.
2
Identify the logical fallacy
The people assume that because they never see something from nothing, it is impossible. Why is this flawed according to Maturidi?
They use the observed (shahid) to judge the unseen (gha'ib) absolutely.
3
Trace the consequences
See how this assumption leads to belief in the world's eternity and then to naturalist and hylist theories.
Maturidi links this to the schools of the Naturalists (ashab al-taba'i') and those who posit hyle (hayula).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Empirical science can prove or disprove the existence of a creator.

Checkpoint
What do the Naturalists (ashab al-taba'i') believe about the variation in the world?
A) It is caused by a creator
B) It results from different inherent natures
C) It is an illusion

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as impossible simply because you have never experienced it? In what area of your life has this faulty reasoning kept you from acknowledging a deeper truth? Be honest: will you actively challenge this assumption by seeking evidence from revelation rather than just your senses?
?
You believe in a Creator you have never seen. But do you secretly rely on your sensory experience as the ultimate judge of reality, like the naturalists described in this lesson? Pause and identify one unseen belief you hold weakly because it contradicts your experience—are you willing to submit to the unseen even when it feels 'impossible'?
?
The naturalists used the principle 'the witnessed is evidence for the unseen' to deny God. At some point, you may have used a similar logic to reject a command of Allah because you could not see its wisdom. When you next face a command that seems unreasonable, will you remember that the seen is not the measure of the unseen? What concrete step will you take to surrender your reasoning to Allah's Word?

Materialist Theories of the World's Origin
Maturidi reports materialist theories of creation from clay, stars, and prime matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist theories of creation from clay, stars, and prime matter as reported by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Materialists sought an eternal origin for the world, but differed on what it was.

Imam Maturidi begins by presenting various materialist theories that deny a single Creator. These theories posit an eternal origin—clay, stars, or prime matter—from which the world emerged. Understanding these views is essential to appreciate the Islamic refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on materialist origins
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three different origins mentioned: clay, stars, and prime matter.
The passage uses technical terms like 'hayula' for prime matter.
2
Identify the three theories
List the three materialist theories: (1) clay made by the Creator, (2) stars, sun, and moon as eternal movers, (3) prime matter (hayula) that receives accidents.
The third theory is described as being invalidated because matter can change.
3
Note the internal contradictions
Observe how Maturidi points out that the hayula theory is self-defeating: they describe it like the Creator but then admit it changes.
This prepares for the Islamic critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول : هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه . لكنهم اختلفوا؛ فمنهم من يجعل أصله طينة أحدث الباري منها هذا العالم والباري يجعله قوم واحداً ، وقوم يجعلونه النجوم والشمس والقمر ، بما كُن يجرين دائبات، وبجريهن نشوء العالم. ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له . ومنهم من يجعلها تعترض فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولّد العالم؛ يسمونه من قبل «هيولى»، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع ، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيّره من حال إلى حال. ومنهم من يقول : أصله اثنان نور وظلمة؛ من النور كل خير ونفع، ومن الظلمة كل شر وضار. لكن منهم من يقول : كانا متباينين فامتزجا على ما مربيانه. وعلى قول أصحاب الهيولى والطينة يجيئ أن يكون كانا واحداً فتفرقا؛ إذ هو الأصل، فصار أصلاً للشر والخير ، فبالتفرق عمل كل عمله . على أن عامة هؤلاء يجعلون كون العالم بالطبيعة لا بالفعل والثالث الاعتبار بالمعاني ، فقالوا: إنا نجد العالم اشتمل على نفع وضرر، وعلى خير وشر . ثم في العرف أن فاعل الخير محمود، ومن ينفع غيره [ فهو ] رحيم حكيم؛ وأن فاعل الشر مذموم ، ومن يضر غيره [ فهو] قاس سفيه. لم يجز أن يجيئ من الله الذي هو حكيم رحيم فعل الشر أو الضرر بأحد، ومثله في الشاهد، ولا له السفه والقساوة. وهذا مما ينتفع به أو يدفع الضرر عن نفسه، فكيف لمن لا ينتفع بشيء ولا يضره شيء ؟ على قولهم: إن الحكيم في من يجرّ بفعله النفع له لا الضرر . فأما من يضر غيره بلا نفع له فليس هو بحكيم. فقالوا : هذا باختلاف الأصل الذي منه العالم ليرجع كل موجود فيه إلى أصله من خير أو شر؛ أو كان واحدا فيه الجوهران فتفرقا، فكان من كل ما يكون من مثله؛ أو بما اعترضت فيه الأعراض اختلف . الشاهد فرجع إلى هذا قول الدهرية المنكرة للصانع والمثبتة جميعا لعدد، فسمت الثنوية الخير بجوهره نورا والشر ظلمة، والمجوس سمّوا الخير «الله»، والشر «الشيطان».
And among them is one who says: This world came from an origin in which making occurred. But they differed; among them is one who makes its origin to be a clay from which the Creator made this world, and the Creator — some make it one, and some make it the stars, the sun, and the moon, because they were running continuously, and by their running the world arises. And they make for the running a beginning by making the existence of one thing from another go back to what has no first. And among them is one who makes it — it is subject to accidents, and from that the world is generated; they call it before that 'hayula', and describe it as the people of tawhid describe the Maker, then they invalidated that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state. And among them is one who says: Its origin is two, light and darkness; from light comes every good and benefit, and from darkness every evil and harm. But among them is one who says: They were distinct then mixed as they claim. And according to the saying of the adherents of hayula and clay, it is possible that it was one then split; since it is the origin, it became an origin for evil and good, so by the splitting each did its work. But the majority of these make the existence of the world by nature not by action, and the third consideration is by meanings. They said: We find that the world contains benefit and harm, good and evil. Then customarily, the doer of good is praised, and one who benefits others is merciful, wise; and the doer of evil is blamed, and one who harms others is harsh, foolish. It is not permissible that from Allah, who is wise and merciful, should come an act of evil or harm to anyone, and similarly in the witnessed world, nor is foolishness and harshness attributed to Him. And this is something by which one benefits or repels harm from oneself, so how about one who does not benefit from anything nor is harmed by anything? According to their saying: The wise one is he who by his action brings benefit to himself, not harm. As for one who harms others without benefit to himself, he is not wise. So they said: This is due to the difference of the origin from which the world comes, so that every existent in it returns to its origin of good or evil; or it was one containing two essences then they split, so from each came what is like it; or by what accidents intervened in it, it differed. The witnessed returns to this saying: the materialists who deny the Maker and those who affirm [Him] all [come] to a number, so the dualists called good by its essence light and evil darkness, and the Magians called good 'Allah' and evil 'Satan'.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents a series of materialist and dualistic theories about the origin of the world, followed by the argument from the presence of good and evil that leads to dualism. The author reports that some say the world originated from a clay that the Creator made, while others say the origin is the stars, sun, and moon whose continuous motion generates the world. Others posit prime matter (hayula) that receives accidents, but they invalidate this because matter changes. Another group posits two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). Some within this group say these were distinct and then mixed, while others (adherents of hayula and clay) say the origin was one and then split into good and evil. The majority hold that the world proceeds by nature, not by action, and they consider meanings (moral qualities). Their argument is: since the world contains benefit and harm, good and evil, and since custom dictates that a doer of good is praised and wise, while a doer of evil is blamed and foolish, it cannot be that Allah, who is wise and merciful, commits evil or harm. Even in human experience, one who harms others without benefit is not wise. Therefore, they conclude that the world must have two origins (or a split origin) to explain the coexistence of good and evil. This leads to the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who name good as light and evil as darkness, and the Magians who name good as Allah and evil as Satan. The author is reporting these views, setting them up for refutation.

Key Terms
هيولى
طينة
دهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The materialists believe the world has an eternal material origin, such as clay or stars, that does not require a single Creator.
Correction: Maturidi will later show that these theories are self-contradictory and fail to account for the world's existence, pointing to the necessity of a single Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the materialists who posit prime matter (hayula) do that contradicts their own description?
A) They say it is eternal like the Creator
B) They say it receives accidents and changes, which invalidates its being like the Creator
C) They say it is subject to motion
D) They say it is composed of clay

Key Takeaway
Materialist theories of the world's origin are diverse and internally inconsistent, paving the way for the Islamic argument for a single Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the origin of the universe, do you ever find yourself leaning on an explanation that fails to account for change and contingency—just like the materialists' prime matter that contradicts itself? What would it cost you to abandon such a flawed foundation?
?
The materialists sought an eternal origin but could not avoid admitting change. Is there any foundation in your life that you treat as eternal and unchanging, yet it has proven itself to be subject to change? How does that inconsistency affect your certainty?
?
Maturidi reports these theories not as curiosities but as errors to be refuted. What error about the world's origin do you subtly hold that you need to expose and reject? Will you take a step today to align your belief with the truth of a single Creator?

Dualist Theory: Light and Darkness as Origins
Maturidi reports dualist theories of light and darkness and their moral argument for two principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist theory of two eternal principles (light and darkness) and the moral argument used to support it.

Core Idea
Dualists argue that good and evil must come from separate eternal sources.

After presenting materialist theories, Maturidi now turns to dualist beliefs. These groups posit two eternal principles—light (good) and darkness (evil)—and argue that the coexistence of good and evil in the world requires two distinct origins. This lesson covers their theory and their moral reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualist passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note the two principles: light and darkness, and their attributes.
Some dualists say they were distinct then mixed; others say they were one and split.
2
Understand the moral argument
The dualists argue that since a wise God cannot do evil, the world's evil must come from a separate source. This is based on human analogies of praise and blame.
Maturidi is reporting their argument, not endorsing it.
3
Identify the schools
Note that the dualists are called 'al-thunawiyya' and the Magians identify good as Allah and evil as Satan.
This shows the historical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول : هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه . لكنهم اختلفوا؛ فمنهم من يجعل أصله طينة أحدث الباري منها هذا العالم والباري يجعله قوم واحداً ، وقوم يجعلونه النجوم والشمس والقمر ، بما كُن يجرين دائبات، وبجريهن نشوء العالم. ويجعلون للجري ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له . ومنهم من يجعلها تعترض فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولّد العالم؛ يسمونه من قبل «هيولى»، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع ، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيّره من حال إلى حال. ومنهم من يقول : أصله اثنان نور وظلمة؛ من النور كل خير ونفع، ومن الظلمة كل شر وضار. لكن منهم من يقول : كانا متباينين فامتزجا على ما مربيانه. وعلى قول أصحاب الهيولى والطينة يجيئ أن يكون كانا واحداً فتفرقا؛ إذ هو الأصل، فصار أصلاً للشر والخير ، فبالتفرق عمل كل عمله . على أن عامة هؤلاء يجعلون كون العالم بالطبيعة لا بالفعل والثالث الاعتبار بالمعاني ، فقالوا: إنا نجد العالم اشتمل على نفع وضرر، وعلى خير وشر . ثم في العرف أن فاعل الخير محمود، ومن ينفع غيره [ فهو ] رحيم حكيم؛ وأن فاعل الشر مذموم ، ومن يضر غيره [ فهو] قاس سفيه. لم يجز أن يجيئ من الله الذي هو حكيم رحيم فعل الشر أو الضرر بأحد، ومثله في الشاهد، ولا له السفه والقساوة. وهذا مما ينتفع به أو يدفع الضرر عن نفسه، فكيف لمن لا ينتفع بشيء ولا يضره شيء ؟ على قولهم: إن الحكيم في من يجرّ بفعله النفع له لا الضرر . فأما من يضر غيره بلا نفع له فليس هو بحكيم. فقالوا : هذا باختلاف الأصل الذي منه العالم ليرجع كل موجود فيه إلى أصله من خير أو شر؛ أو كان واحدا فيه الجوهران فتفرقا، فكان من كل ما يكون من مثله؛ أو بما اعترضت فيه الأعراض اختلف . الشاهد فرجع إلى هذا قول الدهرية المنكرة للصانع والمثبتة جميعا لعدد، فسمت الثنوية الخير بجوهره نورا والشر ظلمة، والمجوس سمّوا الخير «الله»، والشر «الشيطان».
And among them is one who says: This world came from an origin in which making occurred. But they differed; among them is one who makes its origin to be a clay from which the Creator made this world, and the Creator — some make it one, and some make it the stars, the sun, and the moon, because they were running continuously, and by their running the world arises. And they make for the running a beginning by making the existence of one thing from another go back to what has no first. And among them is one who makes it — it is subject to accidents, and from that the world is generated; they call it before that 'hayula', and describe it as the people of tawhid describe the Maker, then they invalidated that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state. And among them is one who says: Its origin is two, light and darkness; from light comes every good and benefit, and from darkness every evil and harm. But among them is one who says: They were distinct then mixed as they claim. And according to the saying of the adherents of hayula and clay, it is possible that it was one then split; since it is the origin, it became an origin for evil and good, so by the splitting each did its work. But the majority of these make the existence of the world by nature not by action, and the third consideration is by meanings. They said: We find that the world contains benefit and harm, good and evil. Then customarily, the doer of good is praised, and one who benefits others is merciful, wise; and the doer of evil is blamed, and one who harms others is harsh, foolish. It is not permissible that from Allah, who is wise and merciful, should come an act of evil or harm to anyone, and similarly in the witnessed world, nor is foolishness and harshness attributed to Him. And this is something by which one benefits or repels harm from oneself, so how about one who does not benefit from anything nor is harmed by anything? According to their saying: The wise one is he who by his action brings benefit to himself, not harm. As for one who harms others without benefit to himself, he is not wise. So they said: This is due to the difference of the origin from which the world comes, so that every existent in it returns to its origin of good or evil; or it was one containing two essences then they split, so from each came what is like it; or by what accidents intervened in it, it differed. The witnessed returns to this saying: the materialists who deny the Maker and those who affirm [Him] all [come] to a number, so the dualists called good by its essence light and evil darkness, and the Magians called good 'Allah' and evil 'Satan'.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents a series of materialist and dualistic theories about the origin of the world, followed by the argument from the presence of good and evil that leads to dualism. The author reports that some say the world originated from a clay that the Creator made, while others say the origin is the stars, sun, and moon whose continuous motion generates the world. Others posit prime matter (hayula) that receives accidents, but they invalidate this because matter changes. Another group posits two eternal principles: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). Some within this group say these were distinct and then mixed, while others (adherents of hayula and clay) say the origin was one and then split into good and evil. The majority hold that the world proceeds by nature, not by action, and they consider meanings (moral qualities). Their argument is: since the world contains benefit and harm, good and evil, and since custom dictates that a doer of good is praised and wise, while a doer of evil is blamed and foolish, it cannot be that Allah, who is wise and merciful, commits evil or harm. Even in human experience, one who harms others without benefit is not wise. Therefore, they conclude that the world must have two origins (or a split origin) to explain the coexistence of good and evil. This leads to the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who name good as light and evil as darkness, and the Magians who name good as Allah and evil as Satan. The author is reporting these views, setting them up for refutation.

Key Terms
نور
ظلمة
ثنوية
مجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that a wise God cannot be responsible for evil, so evil must come from a separate eternal principle.
Correction: Maturidi will later argue that God's actions are not bound by human analogies; evil is created by God for wise purposes humans may not understand.

Checkpoint
According to the dualists' moral argument, why must there be two origins for the world?
A) Because light and darkness cannot coexist
B) Because a wise God cannot commit evil, so evil must come from another source
C) Because the world is made of two elements
D) Because the Magians teach so

Key Takeaway
Dualists attempt to explain the coexistence of good and evil by positing two eternal principles, but their argument relies on human analogies that do not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience evil or harm in your life, do you ever find yourself attributing it to a source other than Allah—perhaps blaming circumstances, people, or even Satan as a separate power? How does this lesson challenge you to see all events, both good and evil, as coming from Allah's decree alone?
?
The dualists argued that a wise and merciful being cannot cause harm. Have you ever felt that a trial you faced was inconsistent with Allah's mercy? In what way does this lesson call you to repent of such thoughts and submit to His wisdom, even when you do not understand?
?
If you truly believed that every evil in your life is allowed by Allah for a purpose beyond your limited understanding, how would your response to hardship change today? What is one concrete step you can take to trust His decree more deeply?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Nature is Subjugated and Proves a Subjugator
Maturidi argues that nature is subjugated, proving a subjugator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nature cannot be self-sufficient and how its subjugation points to a knowing subjugator.

Core Idea
Nature's inability to resist proves it is governed by a knowing subjugator.

Maturidi directly confronts the naturalist claim that natures act autonomously. He begins by establishing that nature is inherently constrained—it cannot refrain from its own disposition and can be prevented by external forces. This fragility reveals dependence on a higher power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on nature's subjugation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi defines 'طبع' (nature) and 'مقهور' (subjugated).
The key point is that nature cannot resist its own action.
2
Analyze the logic of prevention
Maturidi argues that because nature can be prevented from acting by another, its action serves another, not itself. Ask yourself: if nature were self-sufficient, could it be stopped?
This mirrors the argument from causal dependency.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The passage concludes that nature is subjugated under a 'knowing subjugator' (قاهر عليم). Understand how 'knowing' is essential—the subjugator must have knowledge to direct nature.
This sets up the need for a wise creator in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحاب الطبائع فإن الطابع مقهور لا يقدر على الامتناع عما طبع عليه، بل يقدر غير كل ذي طبع أن يمنع إياه عن توليده فثبت أن عمله لغيره ما يعمل؛ إذ قد يُمنع بغيره عن العمل. ولو كان بنفسه يعمل ذلك ما احتمل ما دامت نفسه؛ مع ما إذ كان لا يمتنع من عمل ثبت أنه مقهور تحت قاهر عليم. ثم كل ذي طبع لا يعمل في شيء بطبعه إلا أن يكون الآخر مجعولاً بحيث يقبل ذلك، نحو الشيء الذي لا يتأذى لا يؤذيه الفعل الذي في غيره مؤذ؛ وكذلك المؤلم والملذ؛ وكذلك الأصباغ. وليس عمل الطبع أن يجعل شيئاً بحيث يقبل طبعه أو يباين ويتأثر به، فثبت به كون غير الطبائع. مع ما لو خُلّى بين ذي الطبع وعمله لكان لا يُؤلّف ولا يُصوّر؛ فدل وجودها على غير ذلك أن لها مُنشئا. وبعد، فإنه لو خلّى بين الأصباغ وانصباغ الأشياء بها ليخرج فاسدا مستمجاً، وإنما يصلح ذلك لحكيم عليم يضع كل شيء موضعه. فمثله أثر الطبائع؛ وهو في شأن الطبائع أحق، إذ هي تتنافر، وفيها التباعد، أو تقدح في الأشياء بلا حد، وفيه الفساد؛ فدل الاتساق وقيام الأعيان بها على عليم قاهر جمع بينها وقهرها معا. مع ما كان لكل مجتمع الطبائع حامل يحملها، لكن ليس هو لهن فيثبت بالضرورة وجودهن. وقد مضى من هذا النوع ما فيه مقنع. وقد نجد الحرارة كأنها ترتفع بطبعها والبرودة تنحدر، وقد يجتمعان في جسم. ثبت أن ذلك لمدبر قاهر عليم.
As for the adherents of natures, indeed the nature is subjugated, unable to refrain from what it is naturally disposed to, rather other than everything possessing a nature is able to prevent it from its production. So it is established that its action is for another that it acts; for it may be prevented by another from acting. And if it acted by itself, it would not bear that as long as its self exists; and since it does not refrain from acting, it is established that it is subjugated under a subjugator who is knowing. Then every possessor of a nature does not act upon a thing by its nature unless the other is made such that it accepts that, like the thing that is not harmed is not harmed by an act that harms another; likewise the painful and the pleasurable; and likewise dyes. And the action of nature is not to make a thing such that it accepts its nature or differs and is affected by it, so the existence of something other than natures is established thereby. Moreover, if the possessor of a nature were left alone with its action, it would neither compose nor form; so their existence in a different way indicates that they have a creator. Furthermore, if dyes were left alone and things were dyed with them, they would come out spoiled and disgusting; and only a wise, knowing one who puts everything in its place can rectify that. Likewise is the effect of natures; and that is more appropriate concerning the matter of natures, since they are mutually repulsive, and in them is divergence, or they afflict things without limit, and in that is corruption. So coherence and the subsistence of essences indicate a knowing, subjugating one who brought them together and subjugated them together. Moreover, every composite of natures has a bearer that carries them, but it is not of them, so their existence is necessarily established. And sufficient has passed of this type. And we find heat as if it rises by its nature and cold descends, and they may combine in a body. It is established that that is due to a wise, subjugating governor.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists, who claim that natures (natural properties) are self-sufficient causes. The author provides multiple arguments: (1) Nature is subjugated (مقهور), unable to resist its own disposition, and can be prevented by another, so its action is for another. (2) If nature acted by itself, it would not change; but we see it prevented, proving a subjugator. (3) Nature only acts when the recipient is suitably prepared, implying a wise agent who sets up conditions. (4) The existence of order and composition (e.g., bodies) shows a creator, since left alone, natures would not produce form. (5) An analogy with dyes: without a wise dyer, dyes spoil; similarly, natures require a wise arranger. (6) Natures are mutually repulsive; their coexistence and coherence in bodies indicate a subjugator that brings them together. (7) Observable phenomena like heat rising and cold descending, yet sometimes combining, prove a governor. The passage concludes that all this points to a wise, powerful subjugator.

Key Terms
الطبع
المقهور
القاهر
العليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natures act autonomously and are self-sufficient causes.
Correction: Natures are constrained and can be prevented by external forces, showing they are dependent.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about nature from the observation that it can be prevented?
A) Nature is self-sufficient.
B) Nature is subjugated under a knowing subjugator.
C) Nature acts randomly.
D) Nature is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Nature's inability to resist prevention proves that it is not autonomous but subjugated under a knowing subjugator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you still assume that patterns are fixed and independent of Allah's will? How would your daily actions change if you truly believed that even those patterns are subjugated to a Knowing Subjugator? What is one step you will take today to submit that area to Him?
?
Consider something you rely on—like your health, income, or relationships. If these are subjugated like nature, they can be prevented by Allah at any moment. Does this thought fill you with fear or trust? How can you train your heart to see these as gifts under His control rather than guarantees?
?
The argument shows that nature's inability to change its course is evidence of its subjugation. Is there a habit or sin you feel 'stuck' in, as if it's your nature? How does this lesson challenge you to see that even your own inclinations are under Allah's control, and that He can prevent them? What will you ask Him to prevent in you today?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Natures Require a Wise Creator for Order
Maturidi gives four arguments for a wise creator from nature's limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the order and coherence in nature demand a wise creator who arranges and subjugates opposing forces.

Core Idea
Nature alone produces chaos; order requires a wise, subjugating creator.

Having established that nature is subjugated, Maturidi now shows that nature alone cannot produce the organized world we see. He presents several arguments: nature acts only when conditions are set, it cannot compose forms by itself, and left to itself it would cause corruption. The dye analogy illustrates this vividly. Finally, the coexistence of opposing qualities like heat and cold in one body points to a governor.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended passage
Read the Arabic continuation and its translation. Notice the multiple arguments: preparation, composition, dye analogy, coherence, and the heat-cold example.
Each sub-argument reinforces the need for an external agent.
2
Analyze the dye analogy deeply
Maturidi compares natures to dyes: without a wise dyer, dyes spoil. Ask: what does this show about natures?
The dye analogy is a metaphor for the need for a wise arranger.
3
Examine the coherence argument
Natures are mutually repulsive (e.g., heat and cold), yet they coexist in bodies. This supernatural cohesion proves a subjugator.
This is a powerful argument from opposing forces being held together.
4
Reflect on the heat-cold example
Heat rises naturally, cold descends, yet they combine. This observable fact directly points to a wise governor.
Maturidi uses empirical evidence to support his rational argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice the predictable rising of the sun and the changing seasons, do you take that for granted as 'just nature,' or does it provoke awe and gratitude toward the Wise Governor who sustains that order? How might your daily awareness change if you saw each natural event as a sign of His direct subjugation?
?

?

Where have you assumed your own natural inclinations would produce good results, only to find spoilage? Will you invite Him to put each 'thing in its place'?

The Subjugation of Natural Temperaments
Maturidi argues from subjugation to the need for a subduer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that natural temperaments are subjugated and their actions are attributable to a knowing subduer.

Core Idea
Nature is not autonomous; it is subjugated under a knowing subduer.

Maturidi begins his refutation of the naturalists by pointing out that natural temperaments are inherently subjugated—they cannot choose to refrain from their innate actions and can be prevented by external forces. This leads to the conclusion that their actions belong to another, not to themselves. In this lesson, we explore this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's opening claim about the subjugation of temperaments.
Notice the repeated emphasis on subjugation (مقهور).
2
Analyze the reasoning
Maturidi argues that if something can be prevented, its action is not its own. Trace this logical step.
Think about what it means for an action to be 'belonging to another'.
3
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that the temperament is subjugated under a 'knowing subduer' (قاهر عليم). What does this imply about the existence of a higher power?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الطبائعية

وأما أصحاب الطبائع فإن الطابع مقهور لا يقدر على الامتناع عما طبع عليه، بل يقدر غير كل ذي طبع أن يمنعه عن توليده فثبت أن عمله لغيره ما يعمل؛ إذ قد يُمنع بغيره عن العمل. ولو كان بنفسه يعمل ذلك ما احتمل ما دامت نفسه؛ مع ما إذ كان لا يمتنع من عمل ثبت أنه مقهور تحت قاهر عليم. ثم كل ذي طبع لا يعمل في شيء بطبعه إلا أن يكون الآخر مجعولاً بحيث يقبل ذلك، نحو الشيء الذي لا يتأذى لا يؤذيه الفعل الذي في غيره مؤذ؛ وكذلك المؤلم والملذ؛ وكذلك الأصباغ. وليس عمل الطبع أن يجعل شيئاً بحيث يقبل طبعه أو يباين ويتأثر به، فثبت به كون غير الطبائع. مع ما لو خُلّي بين ذي الطبع وعمله لكان لا يؤلف ولا يصور؛ فدل وجودها على غير ذلك أن لها منشئاً. وبعد، فإنه لو خُلّي بين الأصباغ وانصباغ الأشياء بها ليخرج فاسداً مستمجاً، وإنما يصلح ذلك لحكيم عليم يضع كل شيء موضعه. فمثله أثر الطبائع؛ وهو في شأن الطبائع أحق، إذ هي تتنافر، وفيها التباعد، أو تقدح في الأشياء بلا حد، وفيه الفساد؛ فدل الاتساق وقيام الأعيان بها على عليم قاهر جمع بينها وقهرها معاً. مع ما كان لكل مجتمع الطبائع حامل يحملها، لكن ليس هو لهن فيثبت بالضرورة وجودهن. وقد مضى من هذا النوع ما فيه مقنع. وقد نجد الحرارة كأنها ترتفع بطبعها والبرودة تنحدر، وقد يجتمعان في جسم. ثبت أن ذلك لمدبر قاهر عليم.
In refutation of the naturalists: As for the adherents of natural temperaments, indeed the temperament is subjugated, incapable of refraining from that to which it is disposed. Rather, someone other than every possessor of a temperament is able to prevent it from its production. So it is established that its action belongs to another, for it may be prevented by another from acting. And if it operated by itself, it would not endure as long as its self exists; moreover, since it does not refrain from acting, it is established that it is subjugated under a knowing subduer. Then every possessor of a temperament does not act upon anything by its temperament unless the other is made such that it accepts that, like something that does not feel pain is not affected by an action which in another would be painful; similarly the painful and the pleasant; and similarly colorings. And it is not the action of temperament to make something such that it accepts its temperament or to differ and be affected by it. So by this the existence of other than temperaments is established. Moreover, if the possessor of a temperament and its action were left alone, it would not compose nor form; so their existence in a different state indicates that they have an originator. Furthermore, if colorings and the coloring of things by them were left alone, it would come out corrupt and repugnant; that is only set right by a wise knowing one who places everything in its proper place. The same applies to the effect of temperaments; it is even more true concerning the affairs of temperaments, for they are mutually repelled, have distance among them, or they attack things without limit, leading to corruption. So the consistency and the subsistence of entities by them indicates a knowing subduer who gathered them and subdued them together. Moreover, every congregation of temperaments has a bearer that bears them, but it is not for them, so by necessity their existence is established. And there has already passed of this type what is convincing. And we find heat as if it rises by its nature and cold descends, and they may combine in one body. It is established that this is due to a wise subduing manager.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the naturalists. Maturidi begins by stating that natural temperaments (طبائع) are subjugated (مقهور) – they cannot refrain from what they are disposed to, and can be prevented by another. This shows that their actions are attributable to another being. He then argues that if nature acted by itself, it would not endure; since it does not refrain, it must be under a knowing subduer. Then he explains that natural agents only act when the recipient is receptive, implying that the efficacy of nature depends on conditions. He then argues that if left alone, nature would not compose or form things, proving an originator. Similarly, colors and mixtures require a wise ordainer. He adds that natural temperaments are mutually repulsive and would cause corruption, so their consistency indicates a subduer who gathered and subdued them. Finally, he points to the combination of heat and cold in one body as evidence of a wise manager.

Key Terms
طابع
طبائع
مقهور
قاهر عليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that nature acts independently and without any external influence or control.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that natural temperaments are subjugated and can be prevented, proving their actions are not autonomous but depend on a higher power.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about natural temperaments in this passage?
A) They act autonomously.
B) They are subjugated and their actions belong to another.
C) They are eternal.
D) They are self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Natural temperaments are subjugated and their actions point to a knowing subduer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring habit or reaction you feel powerless to stop. Does this lesson's claim that your temperament is subjugated comfort or confront you? Will you today cry out to the Knowing Subduer to change what you cannot change yourself?
?
When you achieve something good, do you ever catch yourself inwardly thanking your own nature or effort? According to this passage, even the action belongs to another. Can you genuinely say 'All praise is for Allah' for every success, starting now?
?
Is there something you desperately cling to—a relationship, a status, a possession—believing it will always be there? The passage says if nature acted by itself it would endure perpetually, but it doesn't. What in your life has proven temporary? Will you entrust it to the Subduer today?

Causality and Created Things
