Nature's Dependence on Receptive Conditions
Maturidi argues from conditional efficacy to the existence of something beyond nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that natural agents only act when the recipient is receptive, proving that nature is not self-sufficient.

Core Idea
Nature's action is conditional on the recipient's readiness, not independent.

Maturidi continues his refutation by observing that natural temperaments only affect objects that are prepared to receive their influence. This shows that the effectiveness of nature is not absolute but depends on external factors, undermining the claim of self-sufficiency. In this lesson, we examine this conditional nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and examples
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the examples of pain, pleasure, and colors.
The examples illustrate that effect depends on the recipient's nature.
2
Understand the key argument
Maturidi says: a natural temperament does not act on something unless that thing is made receptive. Therefore, the action is not purely from nature.
This implies that another factor (the recipient's constitution) is involved.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since nature's action is conditional, it cannot be the ultimate cause. This leads to the existence of something beyond nature (غير الطبائع).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Is there any area of your life where you have been trying to change yourself or others by sheer force or repeated exposure, forgetting that only Allah can prepare the heart to accept? The Need for an Originator and Wise Manager
Maturidi presents multiple empirical arguments for a divine originator and manager.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments that natural processes require an originator and a wise manager who ensures order and consistency.

Core Idea
Left alone, nature leads to disorder; consistency requires a wise subduer.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by presenting several arguments that point to a divine originator and manager. He argues that if left to themselves, natural temperaments would not produce orderly forms; colors would result in corruption; and the repulsion among temperaments would cause chaos. The observed consistency indicates a wise subduer who gathered and controlled them. In this lesson, we explore these cumulative proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'مع ما لو خُلّي' to the end. Notice the multiple examples: composition, colors, repulsion, heat and cold.
This section combines several arguments.
2
Identify each sub-argument
Break down the passage into parts: (a) if left alone, nature wouldn't compose; (b) colors need a wise ordainer; (c) temperaments are repulsive and need a subduer; (d) heat and cold combine in one body.
Each sub-argument independently points to a creator.
3
Synthesize the overall conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the existence of order, consistency, and combination in nature is evidence of a knowing, powerful manager (مدبر قاهر عليم).

Source Text

Causality and Created Things
advanced
5 min
Refutation of the Distinguishing View as Self-Contradictory
Maturidi refutes the distinguishing view as contradictory and absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the view distinguishing between separation and union as essential states leads to contradiction and absurdity.

Core Idea
An essential pre-eternal state cannot cease without the essence itself ceasing.

Maturidi now takes aim at a specific view that tries to distinguish between two states—separation and union—as both being pre-eternal descriptions of God. He argues that this view is inherently contradictory because if a state is essential and pre-eternal, it cannot later be replaced without the essence changing. This passage exposes the logical flaw and shows how it undermines the very concept of pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally before you try to analyze it.
2
Identify the two states and the contradiction
Notice that the view requires one of the two states (separation or union) to be essential and pre-eternal. The contradiction arises when that state leaves without the essence itself leaving.
The key phrase is 'ذهب عنه ذلك من غير ذهاب نفسه' (that left it without its own leaving).
3
Understand the absurdity of cause without effect
Maturidi points out that this would mean the cause of the state exists while the state itself is removed. This is intellectually invalid.
The cause bringing something into existence cannot persist when its effect is gone.
4
Consider the extreme hypothetical
If such a change were possible, then the pre-eternal could become originated and the originated could become pre-eternal, destroying the concept of pre-eternity.
This shows the view leads to a complete breakdown of theological categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول المفرق بين الحالين ظاهر التناقض لأنه أوجب أحد الوجهين لنفسه من التباين أو الاجتماع إذ ذلك وصفه بالقدم ثم ذهب عنه ذلك من غير ذهاب نفسه فبطل ما كان عليه مع السبب الذي به كان وذلك وجود علة إيجاد الشيء في حال ارتفاعه وذلك فاسد في العقل مع ما لو جاز ذا لجاز أن يصير القديم حديثا والحديث قديما وفي ذلك بطلان قولهم في القدم
And the statement of the one who distinguishes between the two states is apparent contradiction, because he required one of the two aspects for itself from separation or union, since that is its description by pre-eternity, then that left it without its own leaving, so what it was upon became invalid along with the cause by which it was. And that is the existence of the cause of bringing something into existence in the state of its removal, and that is invalid in the intellect. Moreover, if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for the pre-eternal to become originated and the originated to become pre-eternal, and in that is the invalidation of their statement regarding pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that holds a real distinction between the two states (separation and union) while affirming both as pre-eternal. The refutation proceeds as follows: If something is described by one state (e.g., separation) essentially and pre-eternally, then it cannot later acquire the opposite state (union) without itself ceasing to be what it was. If it does acquire the opposite state, then the prior state vanishes without the thing itself vanishing, which means the cause that made it exist in the prior state continues to exist while its effect (the state) is gone. This is intellectually absurd. Moreover, if this were possible, then the pre-eternal could become originated and the originated pre-eternal, destroying the very concept of pre-eternity. Thus the view collapses into contradiction.

Key Terms
التناقض (contradiction)
القديم (pre-eternal)
الحديث (originated)
القدم (pre-eternity)
السبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think change of state is possible without change of essence, but Maturidi argues that if the state is essential (pre-eternal), any change would require the essence to change or the original cause to persist without effect.
Correction: An essential pre-eternal state cannot change without the essence itself changing or without an absurd cause without effect.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would be permissible if the distinguishing view were accepted?
A) The pre-eternal becoming originated and the originated becoming pre-eternal
B) The world becoming eternal
C) God becoming a body
D) The separation of essence and attributes

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the view by showing it leads to the absurd consequence of a cause persisting without its effect and the possible reversal of pre-eternity and origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any contradictory beliefs about Allah's attributes without realizing it? This lesson shows that even subtle contradictions lead to absurdity. Examine your own understanding: are there any attributes you ascribe to Allah that might implicitly conflict? Commit to studying His names and attributes more carefully to ensure consistency.
?
The argument demonstrates that if change were allowed in God's essential attributes, then pre-eternity itself would be invalid. How does this reinforce your conviction that Allah is unchanging and perfect? Are there areas in your life where you subconsciously expect Allah to change His nature to suit your desires? Repent from such expectations.
?
Maturidi's refutation relies on the intellect ('invalid in the intellect'). Do you trust your intellect to recognize contradictions in theological claims, or do you rely solely on tradition? This lesson calls you to use the intellect Allah gave you to guard your faith. Identify one area where you have uncritically accepted a belief about Allah. Will you now examine it rationally?

Impossibility of Essential Change and Its Consequences
Maturidi argues that if essential properties could change, all knowledge would be lost and contradictory opposites would be possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if essential properties could change, then all rational knowledge would be lost and contradictory opposites would become possible.

Core Idea
If essences can swap, knowledge collapses and opposites coexist.

Maturidi continues his refutation of Materialism by showing the devastating consequences of allowing essential properties to change. He presents a conditional argument: if what subsists by itself could cease, and what ceases could subsist, then existence and non-existence become interchangeable. This leads to two absurd consequences that undermine all rational knowledge and make contradictory opposites possible. The passage is a powerful demonstration of why stable essences are necessary for coherent thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that Maturidi uses a conditional structure: 'if...then...'
2
Identify the two aspects (وجهان)
The passage presents two 'aspects' or consequences of the initial hypothetical. Find them and understand each one.
The first concerns the temporality of the world; the second concerns the union of opposites.
3
Reflect on the collapse of knowledge
Maturidi says that if essences could change, knowledge of other things would be completely removed. Think about why stable essences are necessary for knowledge.
He argues that there would be no indicator (دليل) more indicative than what he mentioned.
4
Connect to Materialism (الدهر)
The passage ends by stating that this argument corrupts the doctrine of Materialism. Understand how the absurdities refute that position.
Materialism asserts pre-eternity of separation and union, but Maturidi shows this leads to contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لو جاز وجود ما ثبت بنفسه زائلاً وما زال بنفسه ثابتاً لجاز وجود ما وجد بنفسه عديماً وعدم ما عدم بنفسه موجوداً وفي ذلك وجهان أحدهما كون العالم بعد أن لم يكن ووجوده بعد العدم وفي ذلك فساد مذهبهم ووجوب القول بحدث العالم بلا أصل له ولا قوة إلا بالله والثاني لو جاز أن يصير المجتمع بذاته متفرقاً والمتفرق بذاته مجتمعاً من غير حدث به لجاز كون المجتمع متفرقاً وقت كونه مجتمعاً إذ ذاته قائم وذلك مما لا صبر للعقل عليه مع ما يزول به معرفة الأغيار ألبتة إذ لا علم عليه أدل من الذي ذكرت وفي ذلك جواز جعل الشر خيراً والظلمة نوراً والحى ميتاً والمتحرك ساكناً والبارد حاراً ونحو ذلك الأضداد وفي جواز ذلك بطلان القول بقدم التباين والاجتماع إذ كانا معاً وفي ذلك فساد القول بالدهر ولا قوة إلا بالله
Moreover, if it were permissible for something that subsists by itself to be passing away and what ceases to exist by itself to be subsisting, then it would be permissible for something that exists by itself to be non-existent, and for something that is non-existent by itself to be existent. And in that are two aspects: One is the world's being after it was not and its existence after non-existence, and in that is the corruption of their doctrine and the necessity of stating the temporality of the world without any origin for it. And there is no power except with Allah. The second: if it were permissible for something united by itself to become separated and something separated by itself to become united without an event occurring in it, then it would be permissible for something united to be separated at the time of its being united, since its essence is subsisting. And that is something that the intellect cannot bear. Moreover, with that, the knowledge of other things would be completely removed, since there is no pointer more indicative than what I mentioned. And in that is the permissibility of making evil good, darkness light, living dead, moving stationary, cold hot, and other such opposites. And in the permissibility of that is the invalidation of the statement of the pre-eternity of separation and union, since they would be together. And in that is the corruption of the statement of Materialism. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a further argument against the Materialist position that essential properties like separation and union can change. Maturidi argues that if something subsisting by itself (i.e., essential) could cease or its opposite could become essential, then existence and non-existence would be interchangeable, leading to two absurd consequences. First, the world could come into existence after non-existence, which actually proves the world's temporality—but this contradicts the Materialists' claim of pre-eternity. Second, if a united thing could become separated by itself while remaining united, then a thing could be both united and separated at the same time, which is absurd. This would erase all stable knowledge of things, since we rely on essential characteristics. It would also allow contradictory opposites (evil/good, darkness/light, life/death) to be interchangeable, which is impossible. Thus the view that separation and union are pre-eternal is refuted, and Materialism collapses.

Key Terms
ما ثبت بنفسه (what subsists by itself)
حادث العالم (temporality of the world)
الأضداد (opposites)
الدهر (Materialism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that change of state is a minor issue, but Maturidi shows it leads to the collapse of all rational knowledge and the possibility of contradictory opposites coexisting.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that allowing essential properties to change without an event would make it impossible to have stable knowledge of anything, and would permit absurdities like evil being good and darkness being light.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would become permissible if essential properties could change without an event?
Making evil good, darkness light, living dead, moving stationary
The world becoming eternal
God becoming corporeal
Prophets making mistakes

Key Takeaway
Stable essences are necessary for coherent knowledge; if they could change, contradictions would arise and Materialism would be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake up each morning, do you take for granted that things are as they were—that your mind will remember, your body will move, and the world will be recognizable? This lesson shows that without Allah's preserving essential natures, all stability would dissolve. How often do you thank Allah for maintaining the very fabric of existence? Write down one specific way you can express gratitude today for the order in your life.
?
If you truly believed that good could become evil and evil good without warning, how would that change your moral compass? Would you still strive for righteousness? Reflect on a time you assumed something was inherently good or evil. Are you basing your moral judgments on the stable essences Allah has fixed, or on shifting opinions? Commit to grounding your ethics in revelation, not in changing trends.
?
The argument shows that if essences could change, knowledge would be impossible. In your own life, where do you rely on the stability of God's creation—your health, your relationships, your faith? Are you placing ultimate trust in these things or in Allah, the Preserver of all essences? Pray for a heart that relies on Him alone.

The Impermissibility of Asking 'How' About God's Creation
Maturidi refutes the question of how God created by analyzing its possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'how did God create?' is invalid from a Maturidi perspective, including the analysis of its possible meanings and the ultimate refutation.

Core Idea
God's act has no 'how' because it is incomparable to any created act.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common theological question: how did God create the world? He shows that the question is ambiguous. If it means the process, it is impermissible because God creates without tools. If it means what or why, it refers to the created thing or its purpose. Ultimately, the Faqih argues that the very notion of 'how' implies likeness, which cannot apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from Maturidi's initial response to the Faqih's stronger argument.
2
Understand Maturidi's three interpretations
Identify the three possible meanings of 'how' that Maturidi lists: process, what, and why. Note how each is either rejected or reinterpreted.
The third leads to a different answer (why).
3
Grasp the Faqih's rebuttal
The Faqih says the question should be rejected altogether because God's act has no 'how'. Understand the reasoning: every 'how' has likes, but God is unique.
This is the core argument.
4
Consider the two views on creation's identity
Look at the two positions on whether creation is identical to the created thing or an attribute. Both lead to the conclusion that asking 'how' is invalid.
The first view means no comparison; the second compares to Divine attributes.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the passage defends Divine transcendence by ruling out any 'how' in God's creative act.
This protects tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب لسؤال كيف خلق الله الخلق؟: إنه لو أراد به المعالجة في الفعل فهو غير جائز، بل ابتدعه وأحدث عينه بلا علاج. ولو أراد أي شيء خلق؟ يشار إلى الجواهر من نحو السماء وغيرها، إذ خلق الشيء [في] زعمه هو ذلك الشيء. ولو أراد به «لم خلق؟» فلمنافع الخلق في دينهم وما هو أصلح لهم فيما كلفهم. وقال الفقيه رحمه الله: جواب هذا السؤال دفعه أن ليس لفعله كيف؛ إذ كل ذي كيف هو ذو أمثال. ثم القول في كون خلق الشيء إنه هو أو غيره اختلاف. فمنهم من يقول: هو هو، وبه يقول؛ والسؤال على مذهبه فاسد، لأنه لا غير لخلقه فيمثل هو به. ومنهم من يقول: خلق الشيء فهو صفته التي وصف بها في الأزل، فالسؤال عن كيفيته هو السؤال عن كيفية ذاته وعلمه وقدرته، وذلك فاسد ذلك.
He answered the question 'How did God create creation?': If he meant by it the processing in the act, then it is not permissible; rather, He originated it and brought it into existence without processing. And if he meant 'What did He create?' he points to substances such as the heavens and others, since creating a thing, in his opinion, is that thing itself. And if he meant by it 'Why did He create?' then for the benefits of creation in their religion and what is most beneficial for them in what He entrusted them with. And the Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: The answer to this question is to reject it, because His act has no 'how'; for every 'how' has like. Then the saying regarding whether the creation of a thing is it itself or something else is a point of disagreement. Some say it is it itself, and he holds this view; the question according to his school is invalid because there is no other for His creation to be compared to it. And some say: the creation of a thing is His attribute which He described in eternity, so asking about its how is asking about the how of His essence, knowledge, and power, and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage deals with how God creates. It distinguishes three possible interpretations of 'how': the process (negated), the what (points to the thing created), and the why (for benefits). Then the Faqih argues that asking 'how' is invalid because God's act has no quality (kayfiyya), and then discusses two views on whether creation is identical to the created thing or an attribute, both of which render the question invalid.

Key Terms
المعالجة
الكيف
ابتدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since human acts have a 'how', God's act must also have a 'how'.
Correction: God's act is unlike any human act; it is without process, tool, or quality.

Checkpoint
According to the Faqih, why is asking 'how' regarding God's act invalid?
A) Because God's act has no quality
B) Because creation is eternal
C) Because the question is about substances
D) Because it is permissible

Key Takeaway
The question 'how did God create?' is invalid because God's act transcends any 'how', being unique and incomparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking about God's creation as if it were a process similar to human manufacturing? How can you actively train your mind to abandon that analogy, given that God's act has no 'how'?
?
This lesson tells you that the very question 'how' is invalid. When you make du'a or ponder God's actions, are you still sometimes looking for a 'how'? What would it mean for your relationship with God to truly accept that His acts are beyond any 'how'?
?
The source presents two views on creation: either it is identical to the created thing or it is an eternal attribute. Which view resonates more with you, and how does it change the way you think about God's power and the world around you?

Creation from Nothing and Refutation of Mu'tazilite Thingness
Maturidi affirms creation from nothing and refutes Mu'tazilite thingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi affirms creation from nothing (lā min shay') and critiques the Mu'tazilite concept of thingness (shay'iyya) as resembling materialist philosophy.

Core Idea
Creation from nothing affirms Allah's absolute origination without pre-existing matter.

Maturidi addresses a key theological question: Did Allah create the world from pre-existing matter or from nothing? He firmly answers 'not from something' (lā min shay'), meaning absolute origination. He then critiques the Mu'tazilite position that objects had 'thingness' in non-existence, arguing it leads to resemblance with the Dahriyya. This passage establishes the Sunni doctrine of creatio ex nihilo against rationalist deviations.

Guided Path
1
Identify Maturidi's answer
Read the passage to find Maturidi's answer: 'lā min shay'', meaning not from something.
This is the core affirmation.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite view
Notice the Mu'tazilite claim that thingness (shay'iyya) precedes existence in non-existence.
They say things were things in non-existence.
3
Grasp the accusation
Maturidi charges that this resembles the Dahriyya (materialists) because it posits a kind of pre-existent reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأل: «أمن شيء خلق الأشياء أو من لا شيء؟» فقال: «لا من شيء»؛ معناه أن اخترع الأشياء أي ابتدعها من غير أصل. وهذا فيهما أخبر من حدث الأجسام. لكن مذهب المعتزلة أن شيئية الأشياء لم يكن بالله بل كان به وجودها فيكون على قولهم خلق الأشياء لا من شيء محالاً، بل لم يخلق الأشياء لكنه أوجد أعيانها عن العدم، وهن في العدم أشياء. وذلك من [أسباب] مضاهات الدهرية؛ والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عن وجوابه لسؤال غريب: إنه خلق لمنافع الخلق وسئل أنه لِمَ خَلَق؟ قال: «المنافع الخلق». ولِمَ خَلَق لمنافع الخلق، وأي حاجة كانت للخلق، ولا خلق ليخلق الخلق لمنافعهم؟ فلو جاز أن يقال: خلق خلقاً بلا حاجة تثبت له لمنافعهم، كيف لا خلق إذا لمنافع نفسه وإن لم يكن له حاجة؛ وهذا بقولهم أولى، لأنه كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم.
Then he answered the one who asked: 'From something did He create things or from nothing?' He said: 'Not from something' meaning He invented things, i.e., originated them without an origin. This is more informative about the occurrence of bodies. But the school of the Mu'tazilites is that the thingness (shay'iyya) of things was not from God, but rather their existence was from Him; so according to their statement, creating things not from something is impossible. Rather, He did not create things but He brought their essences into existence from non-existence, and they were things in non-existence. And that is from the causes of resembling the Dahriyya (materialists). And praise be to God who protected us from ... And his answer to the strange question: He created for the benefits of creation, and he was asked 'Why did He create?' He said: 'The benefits of creation.' And why did He create for the benefits of creation? What need did creation have? And there was no creation so that He would create creation for their benefits? If it is permissible to say that He created creation without a need being established for Him for their benefits, why not create for His own benefit, even if He had no need? And this is more fitting according to their statement, because He would be neither Creator nor Merciful.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage answers whether God created from something or nothing, affirming creation from nothing (lā min shay'). It then criticizes the Mu'tazilite view that things were existent in non-existence (shay'iyya), calling it similar to the Dahriyya. Finally, it critiques a strange answer that God created for the benefit of creation, showing the circularity: if He created for their benefit without need, why not create for His own benefit? The passage concludes with praise to God.

Key Terms
شيئية
العدم
الدهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'not from something' means from no material, but still from pre-existing forms
Correction: Maturidi insists no origin at all — absolute origination.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the Mu'tazilite view on thingness?
A) Thingness is created by God
B) Thingness is eternal
C) Thingness precedes existence in non-existence
D) Thingness is a property of matter

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms absolute creation from nothing, rejecting the Mu'tazilite notion that things had a prior reality in non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God created everything from nothing, or do you sometimes think certain things—like your talents, wealth, or status—exist independently of His will? How would your gratitude change if you fully believed that nothing exists except by His absolute origination?
?
When you look at the world, do you see it as 'already being there' in some sense, or do you recognize that it is contingent and brought into existence from nothing at every moment? What would it mean for your reliance on God if you truly internalized this?
?
The Mu'tazilite view subtly diminishes God's creative power by assuming things had a prior reality. Where in your own thinking do you assume something is 'just there' without God's direct creation? Will you repent of that mindset and acknowledge His sole power to bring things from nothing?

The Purpose of Creation: Refuting a Questionable Answer
Maturidi critiques the answer that God created for creation's benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the claim that God created for the benefit of creation, exposing its logical flaws.

Core Idea
Creating for creation's benefit is circular; God's attributes require no cause.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a strange answer given to the question: why did God create? The answer claimed He created for the benefit of creation. Maturidi deconstructs this by asking: what need did creation have before it existed? He then argues that if creation without need is permissible, then it is more fitting to say God created for His own benefit, which would preserve His attributes as Creator and Merciful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the questionable answer
Read the passage to find the answer: 'He created for the benefits of creation.'
2
Follow the logical critique
Maturidi asks: what need did creation have? There was no creation to benefit.
3
Understand the alternative
If creation without need is allowed, why not say He created for His own benefit? That preserves His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then he answered the one who asked: 'From something did He create things or from nothing?' He said: 'Not from something' meaning He invented things, i.e., originated them without an origin. This is more informative about the occurrence of bodies. But the school of the Mu'tazilites is that the thingness (shay'iyya) of things was not from God, but rather their existence was from Him; so according to their statement, creating things not from something is impossible. And that is from the causes of resembling the Dahriyya (materialists). And praise be to God who protected us from ... And why did He create for the benefits of creation? What need did creation have? And there was no creation so that He would create creation for their benefits? If it is permissible to say that He created creation without a need being established for Him for their benefits, why not create for His own benefit, even if He had no need? And this is more fitting according to their statement, because He would be neither Creator nor Merciful.

This passage answers whether God created from something or nothing, affirming creation from nothing (lā min shay'). It then criticizes the Mu'tazilite view that things were existent in non-existence (shay'iyya), calling it similar to the Dahriyya. Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to absurdity; God creates without need.

Why does Maturidi reject the answer that God created for the benefit of creation?
A) Because creation has no need
B) Because creation is eternal
C) Because God is not merciful
D) Because creation benefits God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the purpose of creation cannot be for creation's benefit, as that presumes a need in non-existent creatures, and suggests it would be more fitting to attribute creation to His own benefit, preserving His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Creation from Nothing: Refuting the Question 'From What?'
Maturidi answers 'from what?' and refutes Mu'tazila thingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's affirmation of creation ex nihilo and his critique of the Mu'tazila notion of 'thingness' in non-existence.

Core Idea
Allah created the world from nothing, not from pre-existing matter.

One of the first questions that arise when discussing creation is: 'From what did Allah create?' Maturidi directly addresses this by affirming that creation is 'not from something' – it is absolute origination. He then sharply distinguishes his view from the Mu'tazila, who mistakenly attributed a kind of 'thingness' to things before their existence, a position he compares to the materialists (Dahriyya). This lesson unpacks this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully focusing on the words 'لا من شيء' and the critique of Mu'tazila.
2
Understand 'not from something'
Recognize that Maturidi means creation ex nihilo – absolutely no pre-existing matter.
3
Identify the Mu'tazila error
See how they claimed things had 'thingness' before existence, which Maturidi refutes as imitating the Dahriyya.
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why this distinction matters for tawheed and Allah's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأل: «أمن شيء خلق الأشياء أو من لا شيء؟» فقال: «لا من شيء»؛ معناه أن اخترع الأشياء أي ابتدعها من غير أصل. وهذا فيهما أخبر من حدث الأجسام. لكن مذهب المعتزلة أن شيئية الأشياء لم يكن بالله بل كان به وجودها فيكون على قولهم خلق الأشياء لا من شيء محالاً، بل لم يخلق الأشياء لكنه أوجد أعيانها عن العدم، وهن في العدم أشياء. وذلك من مضاهات الدهرية؛ والحمد لله الذي عصمنا عنه. إنه خلق لمنافع الخلق. وسئل أنه لم خلق؟ قال: المنافع الخلق. ولم خلق لمنافع الخلق، وأي حاجة كانت للخلق، ولا خلق ليخلق الخلق لمنافعهم؟ فلو جاز أن يقال: خلق خلقاً بلا حاجة تثبت له لمنافعهم، كيف لا خلق إذا لمنافع نفسه وإن لم يكن له حاجة؛ وهذا بقولهم أولى، لأنه كان غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم، وهذه أسماء التعظيم والمدح، فكأنه انتفع بالخلق عندهم؛ إذ لم يكن كذلك بذاته فصار كذلك بخلقه؛ جل الله عن صفات الحاجات والمنافع. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he answered the one who asked: 'From something did He create the things or from nothing?' He said: 'Not from something' – meaning He invented the things, i.e., originated them without an origin. This informs about the occurrence of bodies. But the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that the thingness of things was not by God (bi-llah) but rather their existence was by Him, so according to them creation of things from nothing is impossible; rather He did not create the things but He brought their essences into being from non-existence, and they were things in non-existence. That is from the imitations of the Dahriyya. Praise be to God who protected us from it. Indeed He created for the benefits of creation. And he was asked why He created? He said: 'The benefits are the creation.' And why did He create for the benefits of creation, and what need did creation have? And there is no creation for Him to create creation for their benefits? If it is permissible to say: He created a creation without a need established for Him for their benefits, how would He not then create for His own benefits even if He had no need? This is more appropriate according to them, because He would be neither Creator nor Merciful nor Compassionate. These are names of glorification and praise, so it is as if He benefited from creation according to them, since He was not like that in His essence, so He became like that by His creation. Exalted is God above attributes of needs and benefits. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage contains two related refutations. Maturidi first clarifies the answer to 'from what did God create?': He created from nothing, not from something. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for positing thingness in non-existence, calling it an imitation of the Dahriyya (materialists). Next, he addresses 'why did He create?': for the benefit of creation. He argues that if God created for their benefit without need, then it would be more consistent for the Mu'tazila to say He created for His own benefit, but that would contradict His attributes of mercy and compassion. Maturidi concludes by exalting God above any need or benefit, reaffirming His self-sufficiency.

Key Terms
لا من شيء
شيئية الأشياء
مضاهات الدهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'creation from nothing' means things existed as potentialities or essences before creation.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly denies any prior 'thingness' in non-existence. Things come into existence from absolute non-existence, not from any pre-existing state.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi characterize the Mu'tazila view that things have thingness in non-existence?
A) As correct and consistent.
B) As an imitation of the Dahriyya.
C) As the position of Ahl al-Sunna.
D) As a necessary belief.

Key Takeaway
Allah created the world from absolute nothingness, without any pre-existing matter or essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously assumed that Allah created the world from some pre-existing 'stuff'? When you look at a tree or a star, do you truly see it as brought into being from nothing, or do you imagine it as formed from prior matter? How would your worship change if you deeply believed that every atom is a direct creation from nonexistence?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were exalting Allah by saying He brings pre-existing essences into being. Is there any belief you hold about Allah's power that secretly limits it—perhaps by thinking He has to work within natural laws or human logic? What would it mean for you to submit to the idea that Allah's creative act is absolutely unconditioned?
?
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila view is an 'imitation of the materialists'. In what areas of your life—your worldview, your trust in causes, your sense of self-sufficiency—might you be unconsciously imitating materialist assumptions? Will you commit today to identifying one such area and replacing it with tawḥīd-based thinking?

Creation for Benefits: Refuting the Question 'Why?'
Maturidi answers 'why?' and argues creation is for benefit of creation, not for Allah's need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah created for the benefit of creation, and how this refutes the notion that creation serves Allah's own needs.

Core Idea
Allah created the universe for the benefit of creation, not for His own need or benefit.

Another question often posed is: 'Why did Allah create?' Maturidi answers that creation is for the benefit of the created beings themselves. He then engages in a rational argument to show that if one were to claim Allah created for their benefit without need, it would be more consistent to say He created for His own benefit—but that would contradict His attributes of mercy and compassion. Thus, Allah is exalted above all need.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second part of the source, focusing on the question 'why created?' and the subsequent argument.
2
Understand 'creation for benefits'
Recognize that the benefit (manafi') is for the creation, not for the Creator.
3
Follow the dialectical argument
Trace Maturidi's reasoning: if creation for their benefit, then why not for His own? This leads to a contradiction.
4
Appreciate divine transcendence
Conclude that Allah is free from need and benefit; His attributes are intrinsic.

Refuting the Objection of Fitting the World into an Egg
Maturidi refutes a sophistical objection about God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection about fitting the world into an egg is logically flawed and how Maturidi asserts God's power without contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradictions are not limitations of power.

This lesson examines a sophistical objection to God's omnipotence. The objector asks if God can put the entire world inside an egg. Maturidi first identifies the question as obstinate disputation, then provides a detailed logical refutation. Through this, we learn how kalam deals with apparent paradoxes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the objection to the detailed answer.
The passage has two parts: Maturidi's initial response and the jurist's elaborated answer.
2
Identify Maturidi's characterization of the question
Find where Maturidi says the question is 'obstinate' (ta'annut) and why he compares it to the Sophists. Understand that for such questions, discipline is preferred over argument.
This sets the tone: not every challenge deserves a rational proof.
3
Analyze the initial contradiction
The objector asks: 'Can He insert the world into an egg?' Maturidi replies with a contradiction because the egg is part of the world. Grasp why this is a logical impossibility: a part cannot contain the whole.
This is a clear part-whole fallacy.
4
Explore the jurist's three scenarios
The jurist (Maturidi) breaks down the question into three meanings: (1) keeping the egg as a part of the world, (2) the egg being something else from the world, (3) shrinking the world or enlarging the egg. Only the first two are impossible; the third is within God's power.
This nuanced answer reveals that the objection fails because it assumes an impossible scenario.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on the key point: God's power is unlimited, but logical contradictions are not valid challenges. The objection does not disprove omnipotence.
This aligns with the core idea: contradictions are not limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سأل نفسه أشياء لا معنى للسؤال عنها إلا عن التعنّت. وحق جواب التعنّت عن التأديب بما يمنعه لا الاستدلال بالأدلة على نحو ما بينا من شأن سوفسطائي . فسأل عن أنه أليس على كل شيء قدير ؟ فأجاب بـ «نعم». فقال: يقدر على إدخال الدنيا في بيضة؟» فأجاب بالتناقض، لما في ذلك جعل البيضة أوسع منها، وقد جعلها أضيق منها، إذ هي جزء منها، وكذلك هذا التأويل في الأصغر والأكبر. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجوابه عندنا أنه [إن] أراد بما قال على إبقاء البيضة بعضاً للدنيا ؛ فهو محال لما فيها انقلاب بعض كلاً، وكُلّ بعضاً بلا تغيّر عن حاله ، وذلك تناقض. وإن أراد بالبيضة غير البيضة من الدنيا يُجعل فيها، فهو على وجهين أحدهما أن يكونا بحالها، فقد أحال لما ذكر محمد بن شبيب ؛ وإن أراد بذلك تصغير ما قال أو توسيع البيض حتى يسع ما وصف فهو على ذلك قادر. وبالله التوفيق.
Then he asked himself questions that have no meaning except obstinate disputation. And the proper reply to obstinate disputation is discipline that prevents it, not inference by proofs, as we have explained concerning the Sophists. So he asked: 'Is He not powerful over all things?' He answered: 'Yes.' He said: 'Can He insert the world into an egg?' He answered with a contradiction, because that would require making the egg larger than itself, while he made it narrower, since it is a part of it. And likewise this interpretation applies to the smaller and the larger. The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Our answer is that if he meant by what he said to preserve the egg as a part of the world, that is impossible because it would involve changing a part into a whole and a whole into a part without change of state, and that is a contradiction. If he meant by the egg something other than the egg from the world to be placed in it, then it has two aspects: one is that they remain as they are – that is impossible as Muhammad ibn Shabib mentioned. And if he meant by that to shrink what he said or to enlarge the egg until it contains what he described, then He is powerful over that. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector asks a loaded question about God's power. Maturidi first identifies the question as obstinate (ta'annut) and says the proper response is discipline, not rational argument. However, he provides a detailed refutation: The question is self-contradictory because an egg is part of the world; making the world fit inside it would require the part to be larger than the whole. The jurist (likely Maturidi) then gives a more nuanced answer: If the objector intends the egg to remain a physical part of the world, that is impossible because it would require a part to encompass the whole. If he intends the egg to be something else, there are two possibilities: either the egg retains its size (impossible to contain the world) or God shrinks the world or enlarges the egg. The latter is within God's power, but the question presumes the original sizes, so the objection fails. Thus, the argument does not undermine God's omnipotence.

Key Terms
التعنّت
سوفسطائي
تناقض
البيضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this objection proves God cannot do something.
Correction: The objection is based on a logical contradiction (part-whole), not a limitation of power. God's power encompasses all logical possibilities, but contradictions are not valid objects of power.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the objection about putting the world into an egg is considered contradictory?
A) Because an egg is too small
B) Because the egg is part of the world, so containing the whole would violate part-whole relationship
C) Because God cannot change sizes
D) Because eggs are fragile

Key Takeaway
God's power is unlimited, but logical contradictions are not matters of power; the objection fails because it assumes an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter questions that seem to challenge your faith, do you examine your own intention first? Are you seeking understanding or are you falling into obstinacy? How can you discipline yourself to avoid the path of the Sophists this week?
?
This lesson shows that some doubts are based on logical contradictions. Are there any questions lingering in your heart about God's power that you need to reframe as matters of logic rather than genuine limits? Write down one such question and analyze it using Maturidi's approach.
?
Maturidi says the proper response to obstinacy is discipline, not proofs. The next time someone throws a clever objection at your belief, will you be quick to argue or will you pause and discern their intention? Commit to a response that prioritizes wisdom over winning the debate.

Proof of the Beginning of Circular Motions
Al-Maturidi proves that circular motions must have a beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that circular motions must have a beginning and why they cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Circular motions, though continuous, cannot be eternal because each part passes away, leading to contradiction.

Al-Maturidi presents a rational proof to demonstrate that circular motions, such as the movement of celestial spheres, are not eternal but have a beginning. This argument challenges those who claim the eternity of the heavens. In this lesson, we walk through his logical steps, from the definition of greatness to the analogy with straight motion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial premise about greatness and density
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Maturidi discusses the emergence of greatness and density after non-existence. Notice how he rejects anything preceding to avoid equality, given observed difference.
2
Examine the argument for circular motions leading to contradiction
Focus on the central argument: if circular motions were eternal, their annihilation would also be eternal, causing the impossible coexistence of existence and non-existence in pre-eternity.
This is the key logical move.
3
Apply the analogy with straight motions
Consider the analogy of two objects moving in a straight line. Their differing speeds imply a beginning; removing the limit contradicts observation. The same reasoning applies to circular motions.
The analogy reinforces the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك عن أصغر حال يكون عِظَمه وكثافته بعد أن لم يكن . ولطف عظمه وكثافته في الكون بعد أن لم يكن لا بشيء تقدمه ؛ لأن في التقديم إيجاب الاستواء، وقد ثبت التفاوت. فثبت أن الذي تقدم هو حدث بعد أن لم يكن؛ إذ هو في معنى ما ما هو مع ما لو كانت الحركات - إذ هي مستديرة - لو جعلت مستقيمة من جهة ليكون بعضها على إثر بعض، وفي وجود بعضها فناء البعض، ولو وجب قدم الحركات ليجب قدم فنائها، فتكون في الأزل معدومة موجودةً، وذلك متناقض ؛ إذ لا يجوز اجتماع الوجود والفناء في حال، فكذا في كل الأحوال، وفي ذلك لزوم الابتداء. مع ما لو تفاوت في رأى العين ذهاب سرعة أحد شيئين " يسيران [سيرا] مستقيما، لا يُحتمل أن لا يكون ابتداء أحدهما قبل ابتداء الآخر أو يسير أحدهما أسرع من الآخر، وفي رفع النهاية عنهما بطلان النهاية بينهما، وفي بطلانه نقض المحسوس . ثبت لهما الابتداء ؛ وكذلك هذا المعنى في المستدير من الحركات . والله الموفق.
Likewise, concerning the smallest state of its greatness and density after it had not been. And the subtlety of its greatness and density in existence after it had not been is not due to something preceding it; because in preceding there is an affirmation of equality, but difference has been established. So it is established that that which preceded is an event after it had not been; since it is in the meaning of what it is with that if movements — since they are circular — were made straight in one direction so that some follow others, and in the existence of some is the annihilation of others, and if the eternity of movements were necessary then the eternity of their annihilation would be necessary, so they would be in pre-eternity both non-existent and existent, and that is contradictory; because the combination of existence and annihilation in one state is not permissible, and similarly in all states, and in that is the necessity of beginning. Moreover, if two things moving straight differ in apparent speed, it is not possible that the beginning of one is not before the beginning of the other, or one moves faster than the other, and in removing the limit from both is the invalidation of the limit between them, and in its invalidation is the contradiction of the perceptible. Their beginning is established; and likewise this meaning applies to circular movements. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (دليل عقلي) demonstrating that circular motions (الحركات المستديرة), such as the movement of celestial spheres, cannot be eternal and must have a beginning. The proof proceeds stepwise: (1) Consider the smallest state of greatness and density (عظم وكثافة) of a body after it was not. Its coming into existence with subtlety (لطف) is not due to anything preceding it, because if it were preceded by something, that would imply equality (استواء), but we observe difference (تفاوت). Therefore, the thing that preceded (i.e., the state of non-existence before existence) is itself an originated event (حادث). (2) The same reasoning applies to motions. If motions are circular, they could be considered as if they were linear, with each part following the next. In the existence of some parts, others are annihilated. If motions were eternal, their annihilation would also be eternal, leading to a contradiction: something simultaneously existing and non-existing in pre-eternity. This is impossible. (3) In our experience, two things moving in a straight line have a beginning; their speed differs, and there is a limit between them. Removing the limit contradicts observation. Therefore, they have a beginning. The same applies to circular motions. Thus, circular motions are originated, not eternal. This refutes the claims of those who hold the eternity of celestial motions.

Key Terms
حركات مستديرة
قدم
ابتداء
تفاوت
محسوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular motions are exempt from the need for a beginning because they appear continuous.
Correction: Circular motions have sequential parts that pass away, leading to contradiction if eternal; they must have a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Maturidi's argument, what follows if circular motions were eternal?
A) They would have no beginning or end.
B) Their annihilation would also be eternal, leading to a contradiction.
C) They would be perfectly straight.
D) They would be faster than linear motions.

Key Takeaway
Circular motions cannot be eternal because their sequential parts imply annihilation, contradicting simultaneous existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the most continuous and unbreakable patterns in the universe—like the rotation of the heavens—must have a beginning, what does that say about the patterns in your life that you treat as permanent? Identify one routine you assume will always be there. How differently would you live today if you believed it could vanish at any moment?
?
The proof shows that something cannot be both existent and non-existent in the same state. In your spiritual life, are you trying to hold onto something that is passing away while pretending it will last? What is one attachment you need to 'annihilate' so that your existence can be fully for Allah? Define one concrete action to take before the end of this week.
?
Al-Maturidi uses a simple perceptual observation—two moving objects cannot both lack a beginning—to prove a profound truth about creation. What overlooked 'small' observation in your daily life might be pointing to a deeper reality about your dependence on the Creator? How will you let that observation change your awareness of Him during your next prayer?

Critique of the Theory of Natures
Al-Maturidi critiques the theory of natures as effects rather than causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's argument that natures are effects of the world's states (motion and rest), not independent causes.

Core Idea
Natures are generated, not generative.

Al-Maturidi begins by questioning the common view that natures are fundamental causes. He argues that what we call 'natures' are actually results of motion and rest, making them products of the world. This passage challenges us to rethink causality from a sensory perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Al-Maturidi's claim. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the causal relationship he draws.
2
Identify the components
Note that he mentions two pairs: agitation/movement generates heat, stillness/rest generates moisture.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Al-Maturidi says natures are 'coming to be from the states of the world' and why this is 'closer to the truth of the senses'.
This is the core of his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أمر الطبائع، فإنه في الوجود إن كثرة الاضطراب والتحرك تُولّد الحرارة في نفس المضطرب المتحرك، وكثرة السكون والقرار تولد الرطوبة، فتكون الطبائع هي الحادثة من أحوال العالم، دون أن يكون العالم هو المتولد عنها، وهذا أقرب إلى حق الحواس. ثم يقال : إن اضطراب الفلك وتحرك النجوم وتقلبها على أحوال الاجتماع والتفرق يكون بتقلب أحوال جواهر " الأرضين وما فيها من أنواع الأشجار والبحار والمياه، وجوهرِ التفرق التي يعلو بخارها أو هي بجوهرها كالنيران والجواهر الحقيقية"، وبها تنقلب أحوال أمر النجوم وما ذكر . فهذا أحق، إذ هو أقرب إلى العين وأولى أن يكون دليلاً لما غاب عنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
As for the matter of natures, indeed in existence, much agitation and movement generates heat in the agitated moving thing, and much stillness and rest generates moisture. Thus the natures are [things] that come to be from the states of the world, not that the world is generated from them. This is closer to the truth of the senses. Then it is said: The disturbance of the heaven and the movement of the stars and their variations in states of conjunction and separation occur due to the variation of the states of the substances of the earths and what is in them of kinds of trees, seas, and waters, and the substance of separation whose vapor rises, or they are in their substance like fires and real substances, and by them the states of the matter of the stars and what has been mentioned change. So this is more rightful, for it is closer to the eye and more proper to be a proof for what is absent from us. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected arguments. First, Al-Maturidi critiques the theory of natures (طبائع). He argues that observed phenomena such as heat and moisture are generated by motion (اضطراب) and rest (سكون) respectively. Therefore, the so-called 'natures' are actually products of the states of the world, not the original causes that generate the world. This view is closer to sensory truth. Second, he offers an alternative explanation for celestial phenomena: the disturbances of the heaven, movements of stars, and their conjunctions and separations are due to the changing states of earthly substances — earths, trees, seas, waters, and the 'substance of separation' (جوهر التفرق) whose vapor rises, or substances like fire and other real substances. These changes in turn affect the states of the stars. This explanation is better because it is based on observable phenomena (closer to the eye) and thus more suitable as evidence for what is unseen. Thus, Al-Maturidi reverses the causality: instead of the stars managing the world, the world's own processes influence the stars.

Key Terms
طبائع
اضطراب
سكون
حرارة
رطوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think that heat and moisture are intrinsic properties of natures independent of motion or rest.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows they are generated by motion (heat) and rest (moisture), so natures are effects, not causes.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Maturidi, what generates heat?
A) The inherent nature of fire
B) Agitation and movement
C) Stillness and rest
D) The stars

Key Takeaway
Natures are effects of motion and rest, not original causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a strong emotion like anger (heat) or calm (stillness), do you tend to attribute it to your own nature or to the circumstances? How does Al-Maturidi's argument that these are generated by movements challenge you to take responsibility for your reactions?
?
If natures are not independent causes but only results of the world's states, then what does that imply about the true cause behind everything you experience? How does this shift your trust from natural processes to the Creator?
?
Think of a situation where you felt 'it's just natural' to behave a certain way. How does this lesson call you to see that even the concept of 'nature' is created and subject to a higher power? What will you change in your outlook today?

Alternative Explanation of Celestial Influence
Al-Maturidi explains celestial movements as resulting from earthly substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's explanation that celestial phenomena are influenced by earthly substances, not the other way around.

Core Idea
The heavens respond to the earth, not rule it.

After rejecting the theory of natures, Al-Maturidi offers a positive alternative. He explains the movements of stars and heavens as being affected by changes in earthly substances like earth, water, and fire. This view inverts the common belief that the heavens govern the earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the second part. Then read the translation.
Notice the list of earthly substances.
2
Identify the causal chain
Al-Maturidi says the disturbance of the heaven and movement of stars occur due to variation in states of earthly substances. List these substances.
Substances: earths, trees, seas, waters, substance of separation, fires.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why Al-Maturidi says this explanation is 'closer to the eye' and thus more proper as a proof for unseen things.
He prioritizes observable evidence.

The Self-Refutation of Astrologers Claiming No Choice
Maturidi refutes astrologers' determinism by exposing self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that humans have no choice by showing it leads to self-contradiction and absurdity.

Core Idea
Claiming no choice is self-refuting because it undermines the claim itself.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi engages with astrologers who argue that humans have no free will and are compelled in all actions. He demonstrates that this very claim is self-defeating: if they have no choice, then their own statement is compelled, and they cannot claim truth for it. Maturidi then presents two logical consequences that force the astrologer into an untenable position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through.
Notice the compact style typical of Maturidi.
2
Identify the initial claim
The astrologers claim that humans have no choice (ikhtiyār) and are compelled (muḍṭarrūn) in all actions. Identify this in the text.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi argues that if their claim is true, then their own statement is compelled, leading to a contradiction: they deny choice while using choice to argue.
The contradiction shows the claim is self-refuting.
4
Understand the two outcomes
Maturidi presents two possibilities: either the astrologers' statement falls (so monotheism stands) or they must deny the evident reality of choice, which is absurd.
Either way, the astrologer is refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هؤلاء قد أقروا بقولهم أن ليس لهم قول، لأنهم زعموا أن لا اختيار لهم، لكنهم مضطرون فيما يقولون، وكذلك خصومهم فيما يكذبونهم فيكون ذلك التكاذب والتناقض من هذا المدبر، ومن ذلك تدبيره فهو المفسد، ومن ذلك قدر قوله فهو لم يقل عند نفسه. وفي ذلك وجهان؛ أحدهما سقوط قوله فيبقى قول الموحدين والثاني إنكاره العيان والاختيار الذي يعلمه كل أحد وكل عاقل. ومن أنكر العيان الذي يحيط به حسته، ثم يدعى غائبا لا يبلغه حسته بالذي أنكر مما أدركه حسته فهو بحمد الله مكفى المؤونة وحقيق الهجر. وبالله المعونة.
Moreover, these people have admitted by their statement that they have no statement, because they claimed that they have no choice, but they are compelled in what they say, and likewise their opponents in what they deny them. So that mutual denial and contradiction comes from this planner, and from that is his planning, so he is the corruptor, and from that is the decree of his statement, so he did not speak concerning himself. In this are two aspects: one of them is the fall of his statement, so the statement of the monotheists remains; and the second is his denial of the evident and the choice which every person and every rational being knows. And whoever denies the evident which his sense encompasses, and then claims an unseen which his sense does not reach, by that which he denied from what his sense perceived, then he is, praise be to God, spared the burden and deserving of abandonment. And with God is the help.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Maturidi points out that astrologers claim they have no choice, which means their own statements are compelled. This leads to a contradiction: if their statements are compelled, then their denial of choice is also compelled, and their opponents' denials are likewise compelled. The contradiction must come from the planner (i.e., God) which would make Him the corruptor, an absurdity. Maturidi then presents two logical outcomes: either the astrologers' claim falls, leaving monotheism, or they must deny obvious self-evident choice, which is absurd. He concludes that anyone who denies what his senses perceive while claiming unseen things is refuted and deserves to be ignored.

Key Terms
اختيار
اضطرار
العيان
الموحدون
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is denying that God is the ultimate creator of actions, but he is arguing against the claim that humans have no choice at all.
Correction: Maturidi is not rejecting divine omnipotence; he is refuting the astrologers' extreme determinism that eliminates human accountability.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible outcomes Maturidi gives for the astrologers' claim of no choice?
A) Either the astrologers' argument succeeds or it fails.
B) Either the astrologers' statement falls, leaving monotheism, or they deny obvious choice.
C) Either God is responsible for everything or humans are free.
D) Either the stars cause everything or nothing.

Key Takeaway
The claim that humans have no choice is self-refuting because the very act of making that claim requires choice, leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you genuinely believed you were completely compelled in every action, would you still strive to improve your character or correct your mistakes? Why?
?
The passage says denying choice is self-refuting. In what specific area of your life do you quietly assume powerlessness? What would it look like for you to take full responsibility there starting today?

Diversity of Actions Proves a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues that diverse actions cannot come from a single nature, thus requiring a wise creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observable diversity of human actions to refute natural and astrological determinism, and to affirm a wise creator.

Core Idea
Diverse actions require a wise creator, not a single nature.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument from our everyday experience. Look at how people act: some eat despite fear, others refrain despite desire. If actions were compelled by nature or stars, everyone would react identically. This variation proves that actions are not determined by a fixed nature but are created by a wise being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses everyday examples like eating and drinking.
2
Identify the examples Maturidi gives
The passage mentions three pairs: abandoning eating/drinking out of fear vs. approaching out of desire, and attaining pleasure. Also note the contrast between great and small.
These examples show variation in human behaviour.
3
Trace the logical chain
Maturidi argues: if actions were caused by nature or stars, they would be uniform. But we see variety. Therefore, they cannot come from a single nature.
The key move is from variety to impossibility of a single cause.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the origin of such diverse actions must be a wise creator who makes each thing by creation and existence.
This is a proof of God's existence and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت الأحوال مدفوعة إليها لما ترك أحد الأكل والشرب لخوف، ولما أقدم عليهما لشهوة، ولما أصاب لشيء من ذلك لذة. وكل ذلك موجود فيها عليه الطباع، حتى عظم من ذلك أقل منه فيمن صغر ، ولو كان بالطبيعة أو اتصال بالنجوم يجب أن يكون على كل تلبيب به. وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المختلفة أحوالها محال وجودها من ذي طبع كالتبريد والتسخين والشر والخير؛ فثبت أن ليس أصل شيء منه بذي طبع ولكن بعليم حكيم جعل كل شيء على ذلك بالخلقة والوجود.
And if the states were driven [compelled] to them, no one would abandon eating and drinking out of fear, nor would they approach them out of desire, nor would they attain pleasure from any of that. And all that exists in them upon nature, even to the extent that the great among them has less of that than the small. And if it were by nature or connection with the stars, it would have to be upon every fixed constitution. Then, the emergence of actions with different states is impossible to exist from one possessing a nature, like cooling and heating, evil and good. So it is established that the origin of none of it is from a possessor of nature, but from a wise knowing one who made everything upon that by creation and existence.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a proof of a wise creator from the diversity of human actions. Maturidi argues that if actions were compelled by nature or astral influence, then everyone would react to fear, desire, and pleasure in the same way. But we see variation: some people act despite fear, others refrain despite desire. This diversity cannot come from a single nature, because a single nature would produce uniform effects (like fire always heats). Since we observe opposite actions (good and evil, heating and cooling) from the same type of being, the cause must be a wise creator who deliberately creates each action.

Key Terms
طبع
اتصال بالنجوم
عليم حكيم
الخلقة والوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that nature has any role in actions.
Correction: Maturidi is refuting the idea that nature or stars are the ultimate cause, not denying that nature exists as a proximate cause.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say actions like cooling and heating cannot both come from a single nature?
A) Because nature is uniform and cannot produce opposites.
B) Because cooling and heating require different agents.
C) Because nature operates without choice.
D) Because stars only cause heating.

Key Takeaway
Human actions vary too much to be caused by a single nature or stars; they require a wise creator who acts with purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this lesson, what does that variation reveal about the source of your actions? How will it change your awareness of God's presence in your daily decisions?
?
If every action, from the smallest kindness to the harshest word, is ultimately created by a wise knowing one, how does that transform the way you evaluate your own actions? What accountability does this place on you to align your actions with wisdom and goodness?

Refuting Pure Compulsion with Human Experience
Maturidi refutes absolute compulsion with analogies and real examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the doctrine of absolute compulsion (jabr) by using rational examples and observable human experience.

Core Idea
Absolute compulsion is false because even the paralyzed know they could act otherwise if cured.

This passage presents a powerful rational argument against extreme determinism. Maturidi uses vivid analogies and common human experience to show that pure compulsion cannot account for our sense of ability and choice. As you read, notice how he moves from thought experiments to real-world cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the vivid analogies Maturidi uses.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage first gives hypothetical examples (pushed, falling, tied) then presents real-world examples (paralyzed, blind, defective organ). Identify each part.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument works by showing that if compulsion were true, agents could never refrain. But real-world cases show agents are aware of their ability to refrain if conditions change. This contradicts compulsion.
The conclusion is that the statement of universal necessity is false.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself why this refutation matters for Islamic theology. How does it preserve human accountability?
This connects to the broader issue of free will and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت الأفعال بالدفع لم يمكن للفاعل الامتناع، كالمدفوع في قفاه، والذي يُهوى من فوق بيت، والموثوق بالحبال. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وفي الوجود أن المفلوج يعلم أنه لا يمتنع عما تلي، وكذلك الأعمى وكل ذي آلة مؤوفة؛ ثم هو يعلم ارتفاع تلك الآفات والتمكين من الخلاف لتلك الأحوال؛ ثبت أن القول بالضرورة في الجملة كذب.
And if actions were by compulsion, it would not be possible for the agent to refrain, like one pushed in his back, and one who falls from above a house, and one tied with ropes. And there is no power except with God. And in existence, the paralyzed person knows that he does not refrain from what is easy [for him], and likewise the blind person and everyone with a defective organ; then he knows the removal of those defects and the ability to oppose those states. So it is established that the statement of necessity in totality is false.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a proof against absolute determinism. Maturidi gives analogies: if actions were purely compelled like a push or a fall, the agent could not refrain. But we observe cases like the paralyzed person: he knows he cannot do certain things, but he also knows that if his paralysis were removed, he would have the ability to act otherwise. This shows that we have a real sense of ability and choice, contradicting the claim that all actions are necessary or compelled.

Key Terms
دفع
الامتناع
المفلوج
الأعمى
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves libertarian free will.
Correction: Maturidi only refutes absolute compulsion, not that God's power encompasses all. The argument preserves human accountability without negating divine omnipotence.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi use to show that actions are not purely compelled?
A) A person eating and drinking.
B) A paralyzed person who knows he is not forced to do what he cannot do.
C) A star moving in its orbit.
D) A person who is pushed from behind.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes absolute compulsion by showing that even the impaired recognize their ability to act otherwise if their impairment were removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I couldn't help it'? How does this passage strip away that excuse and call you to own your choices?
?
The paralyzed person knows he could act otherwise if cured. What 'paralysis' have you been hiding behind—your past, your environment, your habits—that you know in your heart could be removed if you truly sought change?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you, 'Did you know that you had the ability to oppose that sin?' what would you say? Are you ready to answer with honesty, or will you still cling to the lie of compulsion?
