Exposition of the Theory of Hylic Matter (Hyle)
Maturidi expounds the Aristotelian theory of prime matter (hyle) as an ancient, qualityless substance that generates accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key claims of the Aristotelian theory of prime matter (hyle) as described by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Hyle is a primordial substance with no qualities, whose inherent power naturally produces all accidents.

Imam Maturidi here presents the view of a group that the basic stuff of the universe is a pre-eternal, qualityless matter called 'hyle'. This theory, often attributed to Aristotle, posits that from this featureless substrate, through an inherent power, all qualities and distinctions arise. Understanding this theory is important for comparing it with the Islamic theological perspective on creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the technical terms: هيولى (hyle), جوهر (substance), عرض (accident).
The passage is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the primordial attributes of hyle
List the qualities that hyle is said to lack. The passage lists many: length, width, depth, weight, color, etc. Note that it also has no accidents initially.
This helps grasp the radical featurelessness of hyle.
3
Understand the transition from hyle to jawhar
The passage describes how hyle, through an inherent power and by nature (not choice), generates accidents. At that point it is called 'jawhar' (substance). Trace the causal chain: hyle → power → accidents → substance.
This is the core of the theory: matter produces qualities by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم صنف أن طينة العالم كانت قديمة، سميت «هيولى»، معها قوة؛ لم تزل بصفتها : أن لا طول لها ولا عرض ولا عمق ولا وزن ولا مساحة ولا لون ولا طعم ولا رائحة ولا لين ولا خشونة ولا حر ولا برد ولا بلّة ولا حركة ولا سكون ؛ ولا شيء معها في أوليتها من الأعراض. سُمّيت إذ ذاك «هيولى». وقلبت الهيولى إلى القوة بطباع منها لا باختيار فحدثت هذه الأعراض، فسمى «جوهرا»، وهو جوهر واحد، وهو جوهر العالم. والافتراق والاتفاق إنما جاء من قبل الأعراض؛ والأعراض لا توصف بالاختلاف والاتفاق، لأنهما لا يكونان إلا بغير هما والعرض لا يقوم بالعرض وإنما يقوم بالجوهر، فاختلف به الجوهر واتفق.
And a group claimed that the clay [matter] of the world was pre-eternal, called 'hyle', having with it a power; it never ceased in its attribute: it has no length, no width, no depth, no weight, no area, no color, no taste, no smell, no softness, no roughness, no heat, no cold, no moisture, no movement, no rest; and nothing with it in its primordial state of accidents. It was then called 'hyle'. And the hyle turned into power by a nature from it, not by choice, and these accidents occurred, so it was called 'substance' (jawhar), and it is one substance, and it is the substance of the world. Separation and union only came from accidents; and accidents are not described by difference and union, because they do not occur except by other than them. And an accident does not subsist in an accident, but only subsists in a substance, so the substance differed and united through it.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a philosophical theory attributed to Aristotle. It describes a view that primordial matter (hyle) is pre-eternal and devoid of all qualities. This matter, with an inherent power, naturally (not by choice) generates accidents, becoming 'substance' (jawhar). This single substance is the substance of the world. Differences and unions in the world arise from accidents. Accidents cannot subsist in each other but only in substance; thus the substance changes through accidents. Maturidi presents this view as a claim of a certain group, likely for refutation or comparison.

Key Terms
هيولى
جوهر
عرض
قوة
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Maturidi endorses this theory.
Correction: Maturidi presents this as the view of a group (z.ama), likely for criticism or comparison, not as his own belief.

Checkpoint
According to the theory described, what is the primordial state of hyle?
A) It has length, width, and depth.
B) It has no accidents or qualities.
C) It is already a substance with color and taste.
D) It is made of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The theory of hyle posits an eternal, qualityless matter with an inherent power that naturally produces all accidents, forming the single substance of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The hyle is said to produce accidents by its own inherent power, not by choice. Do you ever act as if your own 'nature' or habits are the real cause of your actions, forgetting that it is Allah who creates your choices? What would it mean for you to acknowledge Allah's direct involvement in every movement you make?
?
Maturidi reports this theory without endorsing it. How often do you hear claims about the world—from science or philosophy—and accept them uncritically? This week, choose one common belief about how the world works and ask yourself: Does revelation affirm this, or am I being influenced by a worldview that competes with tawhid?
?
The passage describes hyle as having no length, width, or any attribute. Have you ever considered what it means for Allah alone to be the Creator of all attributes? When you look at the material world, do you see it as a collection of self-sufficient things, or as a display of Allah's constant creation of accidents in every substance?

Human Behavior Contradicts Compulsion
Maturidi uses human behavior to refute compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are not compelled because people respond differently to fear and desire.

Core Idea
If actions were compelled, no one would fear or desire differently.

Maturidi begins his refutation by pointing to everyday experience: people avoid eating out of fear and seek it out of desire. This diversity of response would be impossible under compulsion. Let us examine his opening reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp Maturidi's observation about eating and drinking.
Notice that he gives three scenarios: leaving, embarking, and attaining pleasure.
2
Identify the conditional logic
Maturidi uses 'if...then' structure. Identify what would be true if actions were compelled, and what we actually observe.
3
Reflect on the uniformity argument
He adds that if nature or stars caused actions, they would be uniform. Consider why this is a powerful objection.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت الأحوال مدفوعة إليها لما ترك أحد الأكل والشرب لخوف، ولما أقدم عليهما لشهوة، ولما أصاب لشيء من ذلك لذة. وكل ذلك موجود فيها عليه الطباع، حتى عظم من ذلك أقل منه فيمن صغر، ولو كان بالطبيعة أو اتصال بالنجوم يجب أن يكون على كل تلبيب به. وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المختلفة أحوالها محال وجودها من ذي طبع كالتبريد والتسخين والشر والخير؛ فثبت أن ليس أصل شيء منه بذي طبع ولكن بعليم حكيم جعل كل شيء على ذلك بالخلقة والوجود. ولو كانت الأفعال بالدفع لم يمكن للفاعل الامتناع، كالمدفوع في قفاه، والذي يُهوى من فوق بيت، والموثوق بالحبال. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وفي الوجود أن المفلوج يعلم أنه لا يمتنع عما تلي، وكذلك الأعمى وكل ذي آلة مؤوفة؛ ثم هو يعلم ارتفاع تلك الآفات والتمكين من الخلاف لتلك الأحوال؛ ثبت أن القول بالضرورة في الجملة كذب.
If the states (of actions) were compelled, no one would leave eating and drinking out of fear, nor would he embark upon them out of desire, nor would he attain pleasure from any of that. All that exists in them according to nature, such that there is more of that in a large person than in a small one. And if it were by nature or connection to the stars, it would have to be uniform in every person with full mind. Moreover, the emergence of different actions with different states is impossible from a single nature, like heating and cooling, evil and good. So it is established that the origin of nothing is from a nature, but from an All-Knowing, All-Wise who made everything so by creation and existence. And if actions were by compulsion, the agent could not refrain, like one pushed from behind, or one falling from a roof, or one tied with ropes. And there is no power but with Allah. And in existence, the paralyzed person knows that he cannot refrain from what follows, likewise the blind and everyone with a defective organ; then he knows the removal of those defects and being enabled to oppose those states. So it is established that the statement of necessity in general is false.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that human actions are compelled by nature or celestial influence. Maturidi begins by observing that if actions were forced, people would not simultaneously avoid eating out of fear and seek it out of desire, nor would they experience pleasure. Next, he argues that different outcomes (heating/cooling, good/evil) cannot arise from a single nature. Furthermore, if actions were compelled, the agent could not refrain—but we see that a paralyzed person, for example, knows he could act if healed. Thus, actions are not necessary but proceed from a wise Creator.

Key Terms
أحوال مدفوعة
طباع
نجوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if actions were compelled?
A) People would eat only out of desire
B) People would never leave eating due to fear
C) People would act uniformly regardless of size
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Human actions vary based on fear and desire, proving they are not compelled by nature or stars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi says that if actions were compelled, no one would leave eating out of fear. Think of a time you left a sin out of fear of Allah. Did that action feel compelled, or was it a choice? What does that reveal about your responsibility for your actions?
?
When you say 'that's just the way I am' to excuse a bad habit, you are essentially claiming your nature compels you. After reading this passage, how can you break free from that lie and take ownership of change?
?
The passage links fear and desire to action. Is your fear of Allah greater than your desires? If not, what would need to change in your heart for that to be true, and what concrete step will you take today to begin that change?

Diverse Actions Require a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues from diversity to a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the diversity of actions cannot originate from a single nature, but from a wise Creator.

Core Idea
One nature cannot produce both good and evil; a wise Creator is needed.

Maturidi now deepens his argument: if actions were caused by nature alone, how could one nature produce opposites like heat and cold, good and evil? This leads to the conclusion that actions come from an All-Knowing, All-Wise Creator. Let us read his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the examples of opposites: heating/cooling, evil/good.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage has a premise (impossible from one nature) and a conclusion (origin is from a wise Creator). Trace the logic.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does this argument refute compulsion? Because it shows that actions need an intelligent source, not blind force.
This ties into the learning objective.

?

?

?

Analogy of Compulsion and the Paralyzed Person
Maturidi uses analogy and example to refute compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's analogical arguments using physical compulsion and the example of a paralyzed person to refute the doctrine of necessity.

Core Idea
Compulsion makes action unavoidable; but we see people can refrain, so actions are not compelled.

Maturidi offers vivid analogies: a person pushed from behind cannot stop, but we are not like that. He also uses the example of a paralyzed person, who knows he could act if healed, to show that actions are not forced. Let us explore these powerful illustrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Visualize the examples: being pushed, falling, tied, and the paralyzed person.
2
Analyze the analogy
Compare the compelled person (pushed, falling) with the paralyzed person. How does each illustrate the nature of action?
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the claim of necessity is false. See how the examples lead to this.
This fulfills the learning objective.

The Materialists' Categories and Their Error
Maturidi presents materialist categories and censures their ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist classification of categories and how Maturidi refutes their claims as rooted in ignorance of God's blessings.

Core Idea
Materialist categories are not neutral; they stem from ignorance of divine blessings.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a materialist system of categories—a supposedly exhaustive list of how things can be described. But far from endorsing it, he exposes its underlying error: these thinkers deny the need for a wise Creator, attributing causality to a force they claim is 'ignorant' and acting by mere nature. Maturidi’s response shows that such a worldview is both intellectually untenable and spiritually blind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the materialist claims and Maturidi's response come through.
Note the list of categories and the shift to critique.
2
Identify the eight categories
The passage lists how, when, quantity, relation, possessor, state, agent, and patient. Find each one in the Arabic and English.
3
Understand the materialist claim about force
They claim that force is ignorant and acts by nature, and that prime matter (hyle) needs no accidents. Recognize this as a denial of divine wisdom.
This is the core error Maturidi targets.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's critique
Maturidi says their error stems from ignorance of God's blessings, leading to blindness and astray. Their whole system is a fantasy rejected by intellect and desire.
Notice the moral and theological dimension of his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقولك: كيف ; و«الوقت» متى ; والعدد بـ «كم»; والمضاف مما في ذكر الواحد ذكر الآخر كالأب والابن والعبد والسيد والشريك ونحوه ; وذو ، كقولك: ذو شرف وذو أهل ونحو ذلك سموه باب الجدة و«النصبة»، كالقيام والقعود; و«الفاعل»، كقولك: آكل ونحوه ; و«المفعول»، كقولك: مأكول . لا يقدر أحد أن يذكر ما يخرج عن جملة ذلك. وزعموا في القوة أنها جاهلة تفعل بالطباع، وليس بالهيولى حاجة إلى الأعراض. { قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } فمن تأمل ما صار هؤلاء إليه علم أنهم أوتوا ذلك لجهلهم نعم الله فعموا عن سبيل الرشد ،فضلوا، ثم بعثتهم حيرة الضلال إلى الاستيناس بمثل هذا الخيال الذي لا يصير عليه عقل ولا يستجلبه هوى والله المستعان .
By your saying: 'how'; and 'time' when?; and number by 'how many?'; and the relative wherein mentioning one mentions the other like father and son and slave and master and partner and the like; and 'possessor', as you say: possessor of honor and possessor of family and the like, they call it the chapter of 'relation' and 'state', like standing and sitting; and 'the agent', as you say: eater and the like; and 'the patient', as you say: eaten. No one is able to mention what falls outside all of that. And they claimed concerning the force that it is ignorant, acting by nature, and that hyle has no need for accidents. {The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said:} So whoever reflects on what these people have come to knows that they were given that due to their ignorance of God's blessings, so they became blind to the path of rectitude and went astray; then the perplexity of error sent them to seek comfort in such fantasy which no intellect accepts nor desire attracts. And God is the one whose help is sought.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes a materialist classification of categories. The author first lists the categories (how, when, quantity, relation, possessor, state, agent, patient) and claims that no one can go beyond these. Then he states their doctrine: they claim that the 'force' (qūwa) is ignorant and acts by nature, and that prime matter (hayūlā) has no need for accidents. The author (al-faqīh) comments that whoever examines their position sees that they were led to this by ignorance of God's blessings, which blinded them to the right path. Their perplexity drove them to seek comfort in this fantasy, which no sound intellect accepts and no desire seeks. Thus he dismisses their entire system as baseless, attributing it to misguidance and lack of divine gratitude.

Key Terms
القوة (al-quwwa)
الهيولى (al-hayūlā)
الأعراض (al-a‘rāḍ)
الخيال (al-khayāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is presenting these categories as valid or neutral.
Correction: Maturidi presents them only to criticize the materialist worldview. His commentary shows he considers it a fantasy and a result of ingratitude.

Checkpoint
What do the materialists claim about the nature of the force (al-quwwa)?
A) That it is wise and acts deliberately.
B) That it is ignorant and acts by nature.
C) That it is the same as prime matter.
D) That it creates the world from nothing.

Key Takeaway
The materialist system of categories is built on an assumption of an ignorant, natural force, which Maturidi refutes as a fantasy born of ingratitude and ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you been relying on explanations that ignore God's blessings? Is there a 'fantasy' you are tempted to believe instead of acknowledging His grace? How will you actively remember His blessings today?
?
When you face perplexity, where do you instinctively turn for comfort? Do you seek refuge in intellectual systems that exclude God, or do you turn to Him? What specific step can you take this week to trust in God's guidance rather than your own reasoning?
?
The materialists became blind to the path of rectitude because of ingratitude. Where in your life have you allowed ingratitude to cloud your vision of the truth? What blessing have you overlooked that, if acknowledged, would lead you back to the right path—and how will you acknowledge it right now?

The Self-Contradiction of the Materialist Origin
Maturidi shows materialists turn preeternal into atoms, losing its eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the internal contradiction in the materialist view that reduces the preeternal principle to multiple atoms, thus losing its eternality.

Core Idea
Materialists destroy their own preeternal principle by making it multiple atoms.

This lesson focuses on the first part of Maturidi's refutation. He argues that materialists, having borrowed the concept of tawḥīd from the people of tawḥīd, misapply it to their hyle, then turn around and break it into atoms, thereby annihilating its original preeternity and leaving only atoms and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the selected Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the sequence: they heard tawḥīd, applied it to hyle, then made it into atoms.
Notice how the text uses 'then' repeatedly to show the chain of errors.
2
Identify the transformation
Identify the exact steps by which the materialists turn the preeternal principle into atoms. The text says they made it 'جوهًرا ثم جوهًرا ثم جواهر' (an atom, then an atom, then atoms).
3
Note the consequence
Observe the result: 'ثم صار بحيث لم يبق من أوليته أثر' (then it became such that no trace of its preeternity remained). Only atoms and accidents are left; the original principle perishes.
This directly contradicts their claim of an eternal origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولولا ذلك ما الذي كان يُعرفهم أن ابتداء العالم ما ذكر ؟ ثم اسمه الذي وصف ثمة فيه ما ذكر، وعمله الذي نُعت ليس في جوهر العالم دليله ولا في السمع احتماله. لكنهم سمعوا قول أهل التوحيد في وصف الله بالذي وصفوا به الهيولى عندهم ، ولم ينظروا فيما ألزمهم القول به، فرجعوا فنقضوا ما قد أثبتوه؛ إذ صيروا الذي لذاته خارج عن احتمال الأعراض ممتنع عن معنى الجواهر جوهرا ثم جوهرا ثم جواهر ، ثم صار بحيث لم يبق من أوليته أثر . وما بقى مما انتهى أمر العالم من القديم والحديث إلا الجواهر والأعراض، وذهب الذي لم يكن بهذا الوصف. فيكون في ذلك فناء العالم بنفسه ، واستحالة القديم بذاته بأعراض قهرته وأفنته مما لا قيام لها بنفسها. ويكون في ذلك القولُ بحدث جميع العالم، الذي دفعهم عظم هذا القول إلى ذلك الخيال؛ إذ كل ما هو مأخوذ إنما هو عرض وجوهر، ولم يكن الأول .. ثم يبطل قوله إذا نفسه حكيما ألزم غيره الصدود عن رأيه والاتباع هواه، بعد قوله : إن الأصل الذي منه كان كان جاهلاً سفيها ، وإن الأعراض هي أغيار ولّدتها القوة السقيمة التي لا حكمة فيها ولا علم لديها ; وهو أحد أبنائها الذي لم ينل شيئًا إلا بها. فمن أين قدم نفسه عليها ؟ وإذا جاز ذلك ، من غير أصل له به صار كذلك، فليقل في جميع العالم بمثل الذي قال بنفسه . ثم لا يخلو القوة التي هي قلبت الهيولى من أن يكون لها سلطان على غيرها بما به قلبتها ; فليقل هو في الله سبحانه : أنشأ الهيولى أو ما شاء على وجه يقبل التقليب ويقوم به التركيب، ثم ليُسم بما شاء هو على فناء ما قلبه . فإذا بطل الأصل الذي به العالم وهلك; الإحالة أن يهلك القائم بذاته ليكون بهلاكه انقلاب غير وقيامه. مع ما يكون في الهيولى ، فتصير هي بلا قوة التقليب فيكون في ذلك إبطال العالم وتقلبه من حال إلى حال دائما . فدل وجوده على فساد هذا الأصل.
If not for that, what would have made them know that the beginning of the world is what was mentioned? Then its name which was described there contains what was mentioned, and its action which was described is not found as evidence in the substance of the world nor is its likelihood in revelation. But they heard the statement of the people of tawḥīd in describing God with that with which they described hyle according to them, and did not consider what the statement obligated them to, so they turned back and demolished what they had established; for they made that which in its essence is beyond the possibility of accidents, immune to the meaning of atoms, into an atom, then an atom, then atoms, then it became such that no trace of its preeternity remained. And nothing remained of what the world's affair had reached, whether old or new, except atoms and accidents, and what was not of this description perished. In that would be the annihilation of the world itself, and the transformation of the preeternal in its essence by accidents that overpowered and annihilated it, which have no subsistence by themselves. And in that would be the statement of the temporality of the entire world—the magnitude of which statement drove them to that fantasy—since everything that is taken is only accident and atom, and the First was not... Then his statement is invalidated when he himself (claiming to be wise) obliges others to turn away from his own opinion and to follow his desire, after his statement that the principle from which it came was ignorant and stupid, and that accidents are changes generated by the sick force which has no wisdom and no knowledge within it; and he is one of its children who attained nothing except through it. So from where did he advance himself over it? And if that is permissible without an origin by which he became thus, let him say concerning the entire world the like of what he said concerning himself. Then it is not free that the force which transformed the hyle has authority over other than it by that by which it transformed it; so let him say concerning God, exalted: He originated the hyle or whatever He willed in such a way that it accepts transformation and that composition arises in it, then let him name it as he wills, and He is [responsible] for the annihilation of what He transformed. So if the principle by which the world [exists] perishes and it perishes, it is impossible that what subsists by itself should perish such that its perishing would cause the transformation of other and its subsistence. Along with what is in the hyle, so it becomes without the force of transformation, and in that would be the invalidation of the world and its fluctuation from state to state perpetually. So its existence indicates the corruption of this principle.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the materialist account of the world's origin. It critiques their claim that the world originates from a preeternal principle (hyle) and force. The author argues that their view leads to contradictions: they take the concept of tawḥīd (divine unity) and misapply it to hyle, then they turn around and make their preeternal principle into multiple atoms, thus losing its original eternity. Consequently, only atoms and accidents remain, and the original principle vanishes—implying the world's self-annihilation and the preeternal being changed by weak accidents. Moreover, their claim that the force is ignorant and that the origin is stupid leads to the absurdity that the one making such a claim (i.e., the philosopher) is himself a product of that force and thus cannot claim superiority. The author then points out that if the force has power over hyle, one could say the same about God: that He created hyle and then transforms it. But if the principle perishes, the world would cease to exist, and the preeternal cannot perish. Thus, the very existence of the world (with its order) shows the corruption of this materialist principle.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayūlā)
الجواهر (al-jawāhir)
الأعراض (al-a‘rāḍ)
القديم (al-qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is endorsing atomism here.
Correction: He is not endorsing atomism; he is using the materialists' own atomistic framework to show their contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to the materialists' preeternal principle (al-qadīm) in their own system?
A) It remains unchanged and eternal.
B) It becomes multiple atoms and loses its preeternity.
C) It is identical to the force.
D) It creates accidents freely.

Key Takeaway
The materialist view self-destructs because it turns its own eternal foundation into perishable atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a theological term (like 'eternal' or 'uncreated') without fully understanding its implications, only to find it contradicted your actual belief? What term was it, and how did you resolve the inconsistency? This lesson warns that misapplying language can destroy a foundation. Will you commit to studying terms in their proper context before using them?
?
The materialists reduced their transcendent principle to atoms and lost its preeternity. In your life, have you ever reduced something sacred—like worship, du'a, or a relationship with Allah—to mere routine or empty words? What 'trace of preeternity' did you lose? What one step can you take today to restore its weight?
?
This passage shows that internal contradictions can dismantle an entire worldview. What foundational belief do you hold that you have never seriously questioned? Are you willing to test it for consistency, even if it means losing something comfortable? If not, what are you protecting?

6 min
The Absurdity of the Materialist's Force
Maturidi argues materialist's own origin force contradicts his claim to wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the materialist's description of the originating force as ignorant and foolish leads to self-refutation and that the existence of an ordered world disproves their entire principle.

Core Idea
If the force is ignorant, the materialist philosopher cannot claim wisdom.

In this second lesson, Maturidi continues his refutation by targeting the materialist's claim that the force (al-quwwa) which transforms hyle is ignorant and foolish. He shows that this undermines the philosopher's own authority and leads to absurd consequences. Finally, he argues that the very existence of the world—with its order and stability—contradicts their principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and trace the argument
Read the selected Arabic passage and its translation. Follow the logical chain: materialist claims force is ignorant → philosopher is product of that force → philosopher cannot claim superiority → if force can transform hyle, God can too → world's existence disproves the principle.
2
Identify the self-refutation
Notice how the materialist philosopher declares the force 'ignorant' and 'stupid', yet he himself is a product of that force. How can he then claim wisdom and oblige others to follow him?
This is a classic ad hominem argument from within their own framework.
3
Understand the alternative and conclusion
Maturidi then offers a parallel: if the force can transform hyle, why not say God does the same? But if the principle perishes, the world would be in constant flux, which contradicts observed stability. The existence of the world thus proves the corruption of their principle.
He concludes: 'فدل وجوده على فساد هذا الأصل' (so its existence indicates the corruption of this principle).

?

The Argument from Observation for a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues from observation that purpose requires a wise agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses empirical observation to prove the existence of a wise creator and how this refutes materialist claims.

Core Idea
Observation shows that purpose requires a wise agent.

This passage presents Maturidi's core argument from the witnessed world. He draws an analogy between everyday experience and the origin of the cosmos. The goal is to refute materialists who believe the world arises from a non-wise principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation without analyzing. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the key term 'shāhid' (witnessed world).
2
Identify the key claim about fitness
The passage states that nothing becomes fit for a purpose it was not fit for except through a wise agent. Mark this premise.
This is the observational foundation.
3
Understand the analogy from shāhid to gha'ib
Maturidi applies this observed rule to the unseen: the world's beginning must also require a wise agent. Recognize the analogical reasoning.
The world's atoms and accidents are used as evidence.
4
Reflect on the refutation of materialism
Consider why this argument specifically targets materialists who posit a non-wise origin (e.g., hyle or chance).
This is not a generic design argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما في الشاهد أن لا يوجد شيء يصير بحيث يصلح لشيء لم يكن يصلح له إلا بحكيم يجعله كذلك. فثبت أن ابتداء العالم إن صلح أن يحتمل كون هذه الجواهر والأعراض [منه] كان كذلك كل على جَعله كذلك.
Along with what is in the witnessed world: that nothing exists which becomes fit for something for which it was not fit except through a wise one who makes it so. So it is established that the beginning of the world—if it is possible that these atoms and accidents come from it—it was thus only by One who made it thus.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a brief but powerful argument from analogy based on observation (shāhid). The author notes that in the empirical world, whenever a thing becomes suitable for a purpose it was not previously suitable for, it is due to a wise agent who makes it so. Applying this to the world's beginning: if the world (composed of atoms and accidents) came from some principle (whether hyle or something else), it can only be that this principle was made suitable by a wise being—i.e., God. The conclusion is that the origin of the world requires a wise creator. This is a classic kalām argument from design, but here it is specifically directed against the materialists who claim that the world arises from a non-wise principle (force/hyle).

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shāhid)
الحكيم (al-ḥakīm)
صلح (ṣaluḥa)
الجواهر والأعراض (al-jawāhir wa al-a‘rāḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from design is a general proof of God's existence.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is specifically aimed at materialists who claim the world arises from a non-wise principle; it assumes a principle (hyle) and argues it must be made suitable by a wise agent.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim about things that become suitable for a purpose they were not previously fit for?
A) They do so by their own nature.
B) They require a wise agent to make them so.
C) They are the result of random chance.
D) They are illusions.

Key Takeaway
The argument from observation shows that the world's origin must be from a wise creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see order in your life—your health, your provision, your circumstances—do you instinctively attribute it to a wise Creator, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you consciously recognized His wisdom in every fitting detail?
?
This argument shows that purpose requires a wise agent. Look at your own body: how many things had to 'become fit' for you to be alive right now? Can you honestly say that happened by chance? Why does it matter to your soul to answer that question truthfully?
?
If you truly believe the world's beginning was from a wise Creator, how should that affect the way you live today? Is there any area of your life where you are acting as if the world came about without purpose, as if your existence is random? What will you do to align your actions with the truth of this argument?

Final Refutation on Force, Hyle, and Accidents
Maturidi delivers the final refutation of force-hyle-accidents by internal critique and reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final arguments that expose the internal contradictions in the materialist concepts of force, hyle, and accidents.

Core Idea
If force is inseparable from hyle by nature, it should have acted eternally—its inaction contradicts materialism.

Maturidi's final arguments dismantle the materialist framework by exposing its logical flaws. He first shows that if force is naturally inseparable from hyle, it should have always been active, contradicting their claim that it only acted at a later time. Then he presents a dilemma about accidents: either they preexisted in hyle (which they deny) or they came from nothing (which implies creation). Finally, he notes that one can simply reverse their entire argument, showing its arbitrariness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to get the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical moves.
2
Follow the argument about force and preeternity
Identify the first point: if force transforms hyle by nature, it cannot be separate, so why was it inactive in preeternity? Notice that natural properties are constant in the witnessed world.
3
Understand the dilemma about accidents
Look at the either-or scenario: accidents either existed in hyle (contradicting their claim) or arose from nothing (implying creation ex nihilo). This shows the materialist position is untenable.
The text says 'since he described the force with what he described the hyle with'—meaning both lack accidents initially.
4
See the reversal and conclusion
Notice Maturidi's final move: the argument can be reversed—what they say about force can be applied to hyle. Also note that force and hyle are two distinct things, undermining their monism.
The passage ends abruptly; the points made are sufficient for refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن القوة إذ هي قلبته بالطبع فهي غير مفارقة عنه، فما بالها خلت عن عملها في القدم، وذو الطبع لا يخلو عن عمله في الشاهد . على أن الأعراض التي أُحدثت إما أن كانت في الهيولى فيبطل قوله : كانت خالية عنها حتى حدثت ، أو لم تكن فحدثت من غير شيء; إذ وصف القوة بما وصف به الهيولى، ولم يكن فيها أعراض، فثبت أيضا كونها لا عن شيء. وهذا المعنى ألزمهم القول بالذي قالوا، فبطل بحمد الله . على أنه أمكن القلب عليهم في كل ما قالوا للقوة: أن يُجعل ذلك للهيولى في القوة. على أنها لا تخلو من غير : أن يكون غير الهيولى ، فهما اثنان وزعم أن الكم من باب العدد، فلم
And furthermore, since the force transformed it by nature, it is not separate from it, so why was it devoid of its action in preeternity, while that which has a nature is not devoid of its action in the witnessed world? And regarding the accidents that were brought into existence: either they were in the hyle, in which case his statement is invalid that it was devoid of them until they occurred, or they were not and so they occurred from nothing—since he described the force with what he described the hyle with, and there were no accidents in it, so it is also established that they came from nothing. And this meaning necessitated that they say what they said, so it is invalidated by the praise of God. Moreover, it is possible to reverse the argument against them concerning everything they said about the force: that same thing could be attributed to the hyle in the force. And it is not free from other: that it is other than the hyle, so they are two. And he claimed that quantity belongs to the category of number, so not...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage concludes the refutation with two final points. First, if the force is inseparable from hyle by nature (as they claim), then it should have acted from eternity (since natural properties are constant), but they claim it only acted at a later time—this contradicts observed behavior. Second, they must explain the origin of accidents: either accidents preexisted in hyle (which they deny) or they arose from nothing (which implies creation ex nihilo, a concept they reject). Thus their position is contradictory. The author then notes that one could simply reverse their argument, attributing to hyle what they say about force, thereby showing the arbitrariness of their system. Finally, he points out that force and hyle are distinct entities, undermining their monism, and remarks on their claim about quantity (likely tying it to their misuse of categories). The argument effectively demolishes the materialist framework, showing it leads to absurdities.

Key Terms
الطبع (al-ṭab‘)
القدم (al-qidam)
الأعراض (al-a‘rāḍ)
القلب (al-qalb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Materialists believe force and hyle are eternal and accidents arise from them without external cause.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to contradictions: either accidents preexist (contradicting their claim) or they come from nothing (affirming creation ex nihilo).

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the force and its action in preeternity?
A) It must have acted from eternity because natural properties are constant.
B) It was free to choose when to act.
C) It only acted when hyle became suitable.
D) It did not exist before the world.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes materialism by showing its core claims lead to self-contradiction or affirmation of creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume your habits and actions are simply 'your nature.' But if nature must act constantly, what does your inconsistency reveal about the true cause behind your choices? Will you examine one habit today and ask whether it stems from nature or a deeper will?
?
Maturidi shows that if force acts by nature, it must act eternally. Where in your life have you assumed that something 'by nature' must continue unchanged — and how does this lesson challenge that assumption? Will you identify one unexamined pattern and put it before God?
?
The materialists claimed hyle was devoid of accidents until they occurred. Do you ever claim to be 'free' of a certain fault, only to later find it emerging from within? If accidents came from nothing, they affirm creation from nothing. Can you confess to God that your own faults arise from a similar hidden source, and ask Him to renew your heart?

The Impossibility of Inactive Force in Pre-Eternity
Maturidi exposes the contradiction of inactive natural force in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that a natural force cannot be inactive in pre-eternity, exposing a contradiction in materialist cosmology.

Core Idea
A natural force must always act—inactivity contradicts its nature.

Maturidi begins his refutation by examining the materialist claim that a force inherent in matter caused the world's transformation. He points out a logical flaw: if this force is natural and inseparable from matter, it should be continuously active. Yet the materialists assert it was inactive before creation. In this passage, Maturidi highlights this contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on force
Read the Arabic passage about force (al-quwwa) and its nature. Note the key terms: طبع (nature), قدم (pre-eternity), شاهد (witnessed world).
The passage contrasts pre-eternity with the witnessed world.
2
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi argues that if force is natural, it cannot be devoid of action. Find where he asks 'why was it devoid of its action in pre-eternity?'
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument undermines the materialist claim that the world began from a pre-existing matter through an inherent force.
This shows that the materialist position requires force to be inactive, which contradicts its natural necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن القوة إذ هي قلبته بالطبع فهي غير مفارقة عنه، فما بالها خلت عن عملها في القدم، وذو الطبع لا يخلو عن عمله في الشاهد. على أن الأعراض التي أُحدثت إما أن كانت في الهيولى فيبطل قوله: كانت خالية عنها حتى حدثت، أو لم تكن فحدثت من غير شيء؛ إذ وصف القوة بما وصف به الهيولى، ولم يكن فيها أعراض، فثبت أيضا كونها لا عن شيء. وهذا المعنى ألزمهم القول بالذي قالوا، فبطل بحمد الله. على أنه أمكن القلب عليهم في كل ما قالوا للقوة: أن يُجعل ذلك للهيولى في القوة. على أنها لا تخلو من غير: أن يكون غير الهيولى، فهما اثنان وزعم أن الكم من باب العدد، فلم يكن ثمة حدث، وقد أوجبه هنالك؛ أو هي هيولى، فيبطل قوله: هي مع الهيولى؛ أو هي التي قلبت الهيولى، وكأنها قلبت نفسها لا الهيولى. مع ما زعم أن تلك الأعراض اعترضت في الهيولى فحركته وسكنته، ورفعته وخفضته من غير أن كان ثمة غير إليه يتحرك أو فيه يسكن أو إليه يرتفع وينحط. مع وجود أمثال فاسد فيما عنه تولده، فهو في أصله أشد فسادًا.
And then, since the force turned it over by nature, it is inseparable from it, so why was it devoid of its action in pre-eternity, while that which has a nature is not devoid of its action in the witnessed world? Moreover, the accidents that were originated either existed in the hyle, which invalidates his statement that they were devoid of them until they occurred, or they did not exist and then occurred from nothing; since he described the force with what he described the hyle, and there were no accidents in it, so it is also established that it came from nothing. And this meaning compelled them to the statement they made, so it is invalidated, praise be to God. Moreover, it is possible to turn the tables on them in everything they said about the force: that the same could be attributed to the hyle regarding the force. Because it is not free from being other than the hyle, so they are two, and he claimed that quantity is of the category of number, so there was no occurrence there, and he had necessitated it there; or it is hyle, which invalidates his statement: 'it is with the hyle'; or it is that which turned over the hyle, as if it turned over itself, not the hyle. Along with his claim that those accidents intervened in the hyle and moved it and stilled it, raised it and lowered it, without there being any other towards which it moves or in which it rests or towards which it rises and falls. Along with the existence of corrupt examples in what it generates, so it is even more corrupt in its origin.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage contains two interconnected refutations. First, Maturidi argues that if force is natural and inseparable from matter, it must act continuously; but the materialists claim the world had a beginning, implying force was inactive in pre-eternity, which contradicts natural necessity. Then he addresses the origin of accidents: either they pre-existed in matter (contradicting the claim that matter was initially free of them) or they arose from nothing (impossible). This forces the materialists into a contradiction. Second, Maturidi shows that the same argument can be reversed: if force can cause transformation, then matter itself could be the cause, leading to absurdities such as matter turning itself over. He then criticizes the notion that accidents inherent in matter cause motion and rest without an external agent, which is even more corrupt than the original claim.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
طبع (tab')
قدم (qidam)
شاهد (shahid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi is merely repeating Aristotle's arguments.
Correction: He is actually using dialectical refutation against materialists, not endorsing Aristotelian physics.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as a problem with the claim that force is natural and inseparable from matter?
A) Force must always act, so it cannot be inactive in pre-eternity.
B) Force is not natural but acquired.
C) Matter is eternal and force is created.
D) Force and matter are the same substance.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that natural force must be continuously active, so its inactivity in pre-eternity is impossible, refuting materialist cosmology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You habitually rely on natural causes—fire burning, water flowing—as if they are self-sufficient. What does this lesson reveal about your hidden assumption that these forces are eternal and independent? Will you now see each natural event as a direct expression of God's constant creative will?

If a natural force cannot be inactive even for a moment in pre-eternity, then your very existence this very second is sustained by a power beyond nature. How does that change the way you view your own heartbeat, your breath, your ability to think? Are you living as if these are automatic, or are you grateful every moment?
?
Maturidi exposes the absurdity of claiming that something natural can be idle. What are you 'idling' in your life—your intellect, your limbs, your time—that you claim is 'natural' but is actually a gift from the Creator that demands constant use for His sake? What will you start doing about it today?

The Origin of Accidents and the Reversal of Arguments
Maturidi refutes materialist theory of accidents and reverses their argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of materialist claims about accidents and his demonstration that their arguments can be turned against them.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot arise from nothing nor pre-exist in matter—and the materialist logic leads to absurdities.

Maturidi continues his dialectical attack by focusing on the accidents that supposedly transformed matter. He presents a dilemma: either they pre-existed in matter (contradicting the claim that matter was initially free of them) or they came from nothing (impossible). He then shows that the materialist's own reasoning can be reversed, leading to absurd conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on accidents
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'على أن الأعراض التي أُحدثت' until the end. Notice the structure: first a dilemma, then a reversal.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Maturidi argues that accidents either existed in the hyle (matter) or came from nothing. Both options invalidate the materialist position. Identify why each option is problematic.
The first option contradicts the claim that matter was originally devoid of accidents; the second implies creation ex nihilo, which materialists deny.
3
Understand the reversal
Maturidi shows that the same argument used for force can be applied to matter, leading to absurdities like matter turning itself over. This exposes the inconsistency in their system.

Source Text

Refuting the Force as an Independent Cause
Maturidi refutes the force theory by logical alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that a separate force (quwwa) acting on prime matter (hyhle) leads to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
A force cannot cause change without being part of the material world.

Maturidi engages with materialist theories that posit a force acting on prime matter. He shows that such a force, if it is not itself hyhle, must either be with it or turn it, leading to absurdities. Let's examine his logical dismantling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp Maturidi's line of reasoning.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Maturidi presents two possibilities: the force is either with the hyhle or it turns the hyhle. Notice how each leads to a contradiction.
If it is with hyhle, the claim that it acts on hyhle collapses.
3
Examine the criticism of accidents
He criticizes the view that accidents occur in hyhle, moving it without an external agent. This contradicts the idea of a separate force.
Accidents cannot cause motion without a subject of motion.
4
Understand the overall refutation
Connect the dots: the force theory is self-contradictory because it either reduces to hyhle itself or implies impossible scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكن ثمة حدث، وقد أوجبه هنالك "؛ أو هي هيولى، فيبطل قوله : هي مع الهيولى؛ أو هي التي قلبت الهيولى، وكأنها قلبت نفسها لا الهيولى. مع ما زعم أن تلك الأعراض اعترضت في الهيولى فحركته وسكنته، ورفعته وخفضته من غير أن كان ثمة غير إليه يتحرك أو فيه يسكن أو إليه يرتفع وينحط. مع وجود أمثال فاسد فيما عنه تولده، فهو في أصله أشد فسادًا. وزعم محمد بن شبيب أنه يسمى القوة حركة. وفي روايته أنها لا توصف بما لا توصف به الهيولى. وقد ذكر عنه الإباء في الهيولى. فلا أدرى أيصح ذا أو لا؟ إلا أنه سمى القوة حركة وهي فيه، فيبطل قوله: إن الهيولى لا يوصف بحركة، إذ قد وصفه بها. ثم لا يخلو من أن تكون مماسة له أو مباينة عنه، وأيهما قال فيه إثبات الجسمية والعرضية؛ إذ البينونة والمماسة غير الذي يماس ويباين.
There was no occurrence, and he had necessitated it there; or it is hyhle, so his statement is invalid: it is with the hyhle; or it is that which turned the hyhle, as if it turned itself not the hyhle. Along with his claim that those accidents occurred in the hyhle, moving it and making it still, raising it and lowering it without there being anything else to which it moves or in which it rests or to which it rises and descends. Along with the existence of corrupt analogies in what it generates, so it is in its origin more corrupt. And Muhammad ibn Shabib claimed that the force is called motion. And in his narration, it is not described by what the hyhle is not described by. And refusal has been reported from him regarding the hyhle. So I do not know whether this is correct or not? Except that he named the force motion while it is in it, so his statement is invalid: that the hyhle is not described by motion, since he has described it by it. Then it is inevitable that either it is contiguous with it or separate from it, and whichever he says, there is in it the affirmation of body and accident; for separation and contiguity are other than that which touches and separates.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage refutes the materialist theory that a force (quwwa) acts on prime matter (hyhle) to bring about change, specifically the claim that the force is either the cause of events or is itself hyhle. The author examines the logical consequences: if the force is with the hyhle, the statement is invalid; if it turns the hyhle, it would imply the force turns itself. He then criticizes the view that accidents occur in hyhle by moving it without any other agent, pointing out that this involves contradictory assumptions. Next, he addresses Muhammad ibn Shabib's claim that force is called motion, noting the inconsistency that if force is in hyhle and is motion, then hyhle is described by motion, contradicting the claim that it is not. Finally, he argues that force must be either contiguous with or separate from hyhle; either way, it implies the existence of body (jism) and accident (‛arad), because contiguity and separation are predicates distinct from the substances they qualify.

Key Terms
قوة
هيولى
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A force (quwwa) exists as a separate entity that acts on prime matter (hyhle) to produce change.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a force leads to contradictions: if it is with hyhle, it is redundant; if it turns hyhle, it implies self-action. Moreover, accidents cannot arise without a subject and a goal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if the force is the one that turned the hyhle, what does it imply?
A) It is a separate substance.
B) It turned itself, not the hyhle.
C) It is motion.
D) It is an accident.

Key Takeaway
A force distinct from prime matter cannot be a coherent cause of change; it either coincides with matter or leads to self-action.

What specific step will you take today to live out this conviction?

Muhammad ibn Shabib's View and the Contiguity Argument
Maturidi critiques ibn Shabib and uses contiguity to force materiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Muhammad ibn Shabib's claim that force is motion, and why the dichotomy of contiguity or separation forces affirmation of body and accident.

Core Idea
Whether force touches hyhle or not, it must be a material entity.

Maturidi turns to a specific proponent, Muhammad ibn Shabib, who defines force as motion. He spots an inconsistency: if force is in hyhle, then hyhle is described by motion, contrary to their claim. He then shows that whether force is contiguous or separate, both entail body and accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once. Then read the translation to follow the logic.
2
Identify ibn Shabib's claim
Ibn Shabib says force is called motion and that it is not described by what hyhle is not described by. Understand his position.
He seems to deny that hyhle has motion.
3
Notice the inconsistency
Maturidi objects: if force is motion and is in hyhle, then hyhle is described by motion, contradicting ibn Shabib's denial.
The force is in hyhle, so hyhle must have that motion.
4
Consider the contiguity dichotomy
Force must be either contiguous with hyhle or separate from it. Each option implies body and accident, disproving the original theory.
Contiguity and separation are predicates of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

There was no occurrence, and he had necessitated it there; or it is hyhle, so his statement is invalid: it is with the hyhle; or it is that which turned the hyhle, as if it turned itself not the hyhle. Commentary
This passage refutes the materialist theory that a force (quwwa) acts on prime matter (hyhle) to bring about change, specifically the claim that the force is either the cause of events or is itself hyhle. The author examines the logical consequences: if the force is with the hyhle, the statement is invalid; if it turns the hyhle, it would imply the force turns itself. Key Terms

The claim that force is motion is self-defeating, and the very relation of force to hyhle requires material attributes.

?

?

?
The dichotomy of contiguity or separation forces a clear choice. In your daily life, are there situations where you avoid facing a logical dichotomy? Where you try to have it both ways? Identify one such dilemma and commit to resolving it by choosing one side and facing its consequences.

Refuting the Origin of Atoms from Motions
Maturidi refutes the claim that atoms arise from motions of an original principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the theory that atoms emerge from motions of an original principle, based on the diversity of atomic existence.

Core Idea
Atoms exist everywhere, but the supposed source motions are impossible to unite.

Maturidi confronts a materialist and astrologer claim that the basic atoms of the universe are generated from the motions of some original principle. He argues that atoms are observed in all directions, while the required motions would be contradictory. This short but powerful refutation shows the weakness of such causal theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's argument first-hand.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the claim
The claim is that atoms (jawahir) came into existence from motions of an origin (harakat al-asl). Also the astrologers' view is similar.
3
Understand the observation
Maturidi notes that atoms exist from high and low and from every side. This is a factual observation about the distribution of atoms.
The diversity of positions is key.
4
Grasp the contradiction
The motions that supposedly produce atoms would be various and mutually impossible (ihalah). For example, motions upward and downward cannot be from a single origin simultaneously.
If the motions are contradictory, the cause cannot be singular or coherent.
5
See the conclusion
Therefore, the claim is false (batil). The existence of atoms in all directions disproves the theory because the required motions are impossible to combine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قول هؤلاء أن حدثت الجواهر من حركات الأصل، وكذلك قول المنجمة. ومعلوم وجود جواهر من علوّ وسفل ومن كل جانب، على إحالة تلك الحركات المختلفة، فثبت أن ذا باطل.
Then the statement of these people that the atoms arose from the motions of the origin, and likewise the statement of the astrologers. And it is known that atoms exist from high and low and from every side, despite the impossibility of those various motions, so it is established that this is false.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This short passage presents a refutation of the materialist and astrologer claim that the primary atoms (jawahir) are generated from the motions of an original principle (حركات الأصل). The author argues that atoms are observed to exist in all directions—above, below, and every side—whereas the motions that supposedly produce them would be contradictory and mutually impossible (إحالة). Since such diverse motions cannot coexist coherently, the claim that atoms originate from them is false.

Key Terms
جواهر
حركات الأصل
منجمة
إحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that atoms exist at all.
Correction: The argument only refutes the claim that atoms arise from motions of an origin; it does not deny the existence of atoms.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that atoms arise from original motions?
A) Atoms are eternal.
B) Atoms exist in all directions despite impossible motions.
C) Motions are uniform.
D) Atoms are simple.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of atomic positions makes it impossible for them to be generated from a single set of motions, so the theory is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when examined closely, required contradictory things to be true? How did you respond? Commit this week to applying the same logical scrutiny to any belief you hold without proof.
?
This passage shows that the diversity of creation—atoms in every direction—points to the impossibility of a purely physical origin. Do you live as though the world around you is just random motion, or as a sign of a wise Creator? Let this lesson challenge you to see in every side—high and low—a purposeful hand, not blind chance.
?
The refutation here is based on simple observation: atoms exist in all directions. Are you attentive to the evidence around you that contradicts false worldviews? Or do you passively accept what you're told? Challenge yourself today to always reason from what is plainly known to test every claim you encounter.

Al-Nazzam's Objection on Causal Inadequacy of Turning Force
Al-Nazzam objects that turning force cannot produce multiple accidents; Maturidi responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's critique of the materialist theory of turning force and why it fails to explain the multiplicity of accidents.

Core Idea
A single-direction force cannot produce multiple simultaneous accidents.

Al-Nazzam raises a logical objection against the materialist claim that the turning of a force within hyhle causes the diversity of accidents. He argues that if the motion is from only one direction, it cannot generate multiple distinct accidents at once. The materialists respond by suggesting multiple directions, but al-Nazzam shows that even then, the number of directions is insufficient to account for the observed multiplicity. This passage illustrates how careful causal reasoning is used in kalam to refute physical theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand al-Nazzam's argument. Note the logical structure: premise, objection, counter-response, and conclusion.
2
Identify the key numbers
Notice that al-Nazzam uses numbers: six directions vs. more than twelve accidents. Understand why this disproportionality is the core of his argument.
The materialists claimed up to six directions, but accidents exceed twelve.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how Maturidi presents this objection as a successful refutation. What does this show about the method of argumentation in kalam?
This demonstrates the use of logical consistency and numerical disproportionality to refute a theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الفصل ناقضهم النظام، أنه إذا كان تقليب القوة الهيولى سبب حدوث تختلف كاللون والطعم والحرّ واللين ونحو ذلك، فيحدث ذلك كله في الأعراض، ثم هي في وقت واحد وبحركة إنما هي تكون من جهة واحدة؟ فقيل: تكون من جهات. فزعم أن أكثرها ستة، وقد يحدث أكثر من اثنى عشر من تلك الأعراض، فثبت أن ذلك ليس لتقليب القوة. على أن التقليب يكون من جهة، والأعراض تكثر، فثبت أن ذلك ليس بما ذكر.
And by this section al-Nazzam contradicted them, that if the turning of the force of the hyhle is the cause of the occurrence of differences like color, taste, heat, softness and the like, then all that occurs in the accidents, then they are at one time and by one motion while it is only from one direction? So it was said: they are from directions. Then he claimed that the most of them are six, and more than twelve of those accidents may occur, so it is established that that is not due to the turning of the force. Moreover, turning is from one direction, and the accidents multiply, so it is established that that is not what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nazzam's objection against the materialist claim that the turning (taqlib) of a force within hyhle causes diverse accidents (like color, taste, heat, etc.). Al-Nazzam argues that if the force's motion is from only one direction, how can it produce multiple accidents simultaneously? The materialists respond that motion comes from multiple directions (up to six). Even so, al-Nazzam notes that more than twelve accidents can occur, whereas the maximum directions are six, so the turning cannot be the cause. Additionally, turning is from a single direction, yet accidents are many. Thus, the theory is refuted.

Key Terms
تقليب
قوة
هيولى
أعراض
جهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Nazzam denies the multiplicity of accidents altogether.
Correction: Al-Nazzam does not deny multiplicity; he argues that a single-direction turning force is insufficient to cause such multiplicity.

Checkpoint
What is al-Nazzam's key argument against the turning theory?
A) Motion cannot be in hyhle.
B) Turning produces only one accident.
C) The number of directions (max six) is less than the number of accidents (more than twelve).
D) Accidents are not real.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's argument shows that the materialist theory of a single turning force is causally insufficient to explain the diversity of accidents, thus refuting that view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto an explanation for something in your life simply because it was comfortable, even when it could not account for the complexity you observed? What might you need to re-examine today based on al-Nazzam's insistence on logical consistency?
?
If a single turning force cannot produce the diversity of accidents, what does that imply about the ultimate cause of the variety you see in creation? Is your reliance on 'natural causes' actually displacing the role of the Creator in your heart?
?
Al-Nazzam's argument forces the materialist to admit that their theory is insufficient. Are there areas of your faith where you have accepted insufficient explanations? What would it take for you to seek the truth with the same rigorous demand for coherence?

Muhammad ibn Shabib's Objection and Maturidi's Answer on Length
Ibn Shabib objects; Maturidi answers with lime and arsenic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends the possibility of length emerging from non-long components through the example of lime and arsenic.

Core Idea
New properties can emerge from combination without each part possessing them.

Muhammad ibn Shabib raises a powerful objection: if neither hyhle nor accidents are long individually, how can length appear when they combine? Maturidi answers by drawing an analogy to lime and arsenic, which individually do not burn but together produce fire. This lesson unpacks the logic of combination and preventers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to Ibn Shabib's analogy of 'void' and his conclusion that the combination is absurd.
Notice how Ibn Shabib uses the example of 'what is not void' and 'void' to parallel 'non-long' and 'long'.
2
Understand the lime-arsenic analogy
Maturidi answers with the example of lime and arsenic: neither burns alone, but together they burn. Reflect on how this shows that a new property can arise from combination without each part having that property.
The key mechanism is that one substance has a preventer of burning, and the other removes that preventer.
3
Apply the analogy to length
Now see how the same logic applies to length: hyhle and accidents individually are not long, but when combined, length emerges. This refutes Ibn Shabib's objection.
Maturidi also notes that the same principle applies to other accidents like blackness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارضهم محمد بن شبيب بما الهيولى قبل حدوث الأعراض ليست بطويلة، والأعراض ليست بطويلة، فكيف صار عند الوجود طويلاً؟ وكذلك العرض. ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يجمع بين ما ليس يخلو وما يخلو، فيصير خلو، ومثله في جميع الأعراض كالسواد يخلو، وسواد. فأجاب عنهم بالنورة والزرنيخ، أن كل واحد منهما على الانفراد لا يحرق، وعند الاجتماع يحرق. فيقال: ما يبعد أن يكون أحدهما يحرق لكن فيه ما يمنع عن الإحراق، وفي الآخر ما يمنع هذا المانع عن المنع فيحرق، لا أن لم يكن فيه إحراق، أو كلاهما كانا كذلك. وأمر الأعراض عندك على ما ذكرنا، ومحال حلول المانع فيه لو كان طويلاً أو أسود، وكذا في الهيولى، لذلك اختلفا.
And Muhammad ibn Shabib objected to them: the hyhle before the occurrence of accidents is not long, and the accidents are not long, so how did it become long upon existence? And likewise the accident. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to combine what is not void and what is void, so it would become void, and the like in all accidents like blackness voids, and blackness. So he answered them by the example of lime and arsenic, that each one of them individually does not burn, and when combined they burn. So it is said: what prevents that one of them burns but in it there is something that prevents burning, and in the other there is something that prevents this preventer from preventing, so it burns, not that there was no burning in it, or both were like that. And the matter of accidents according to you is as we mentioned, and the indwelling of the preventer in it is impossible if it were long or black, and likewise in the hyhle, therefore they differed.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Muhammad ibn Shabib and its answer. Ibn Shabib asks: if hyhle and accidents individually are not long, how does length arise when they combine? He draws an analogy: it would be like combining what is not void and what is void to produce void, which is absurd. The answer uses the example of lime (nura) and arsenic (zarnikh): neither burns alone, but together they burn. This is explained by one substance containing a preventer (مانع) of burning, and the other containing that which removes the preventer (مانع المنع). Thus, the combination produces a new property (burning) without each part having that property individually. Similarly, length arises from the combination of hyhle and accidents, not from each alone. The author then notes that the same principle applies to other accidents like blackness, and that it is impossible for a preventer to inhere in something that is itself long or black, thus the difference remains.

Key Terms
هيولى
طول
عرض
نورة
زرنيخ
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is that length is impossible to arise from non-long parts.
Correction: The answer shows that length can arise through combination, just as burning arises from non-burning lime and arsenic when combined.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to answer how length arises from non-long components?
A) Water and oil
B) Lime and arsenic
C) Fire and air
D) Hot and cold

Key Takeaway
Length can arise from combination of non-long components, just as burning arises from lime and arsenic together.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection assumes that if parts lack a property, the whole cannot have it. In your own spiritual life, are there virtues (like patience, sincerity, humility) that you think you cannot attain because you don't see any trace of them in your current actions? What 'preventer' might be blocking them that Allah could remove through combination with other people or circumstances?
?
Lime and arsenic alone are harmless, but together they burn. Think of two habits or influences in your life that seem neutral individually but produce destructive effects when combined. What small 'preventer-remover' are you allowing into your environment that quietly unleashes harm?
?
If Allah brings new qualities from combinations we cannot foresee, are you willing to trust His wisdom in mixing your trials and blessings to produce growth you cannot now detect? What would it cost you to let go of the assumption that your current lacks define your potential?

Refuting the Objection with Lime and Arsenic
Maturidi answers an objection about matter and accidents using mixture analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the lime and arsenic analogy to refute an objection about emergent properties in matter and accidents.

Core Idea
Emergent properties arise from combination, not from isolated parts.

Imam Maturidi was challenged with a paradox: how can matter and accidents, each lacking length, produce a long body when combined? He answers with a striking analogy from everyday chemistry: lime and arsenic. This lesson unpacks that analogy and shows how it defends the reality of efficient causes in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read Muhammad bin Shabib's challenge. He argues that matter and accidents individually lack length, yet a long body emerges upon combination.
Note that the objector assumes properties of parts must be present in the whole.
2
Identify the core problem
The objection tries to show that emergent properties would be impossible without a prior cause in the components. Understand why this is a problem for Maturidi's view.
3
Examine the analogy
Read the lime and arsenic example. Each alone does not burn, but together they burn. This demonstrates that combination can produce new properties.
The analogy is a concrete counterexample to the objector's assumption.
4
Understand the answer's mechanism
Maturidi explains that one substance may have a preventer of burning, and the other removes that preventer. This shows why combined properties differ without requiring a separate cause in the substrate.
This refutes the objection without conceding that accidents inhere in matter.
5
Connect to the larger point
Realize that the same logic applies to matter and accidents. The analogy preserves the distinction between matter and accidents while accounting for emergent properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارضهم محمد بن شبيب بما الهيولى قبل حدوث الأعراض ليست بطويلة، والأعراض ليست بطويلة، فكيف صار عند الوجود طويلاً؟ وكذلك العرض. ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يجمع بين ما ليس يخلو وما يخلو، فيصير خلو، ومثله في جميع الأعراض كالسواد يخلو، وسواد. فأجاب عنهم بالنُّورَة والزرنيخ، أن كل واحد منهما على الانفراد لا يُحرق، وعند الاجتماع يحرق. فيقال: ما يبعد أن يكون أحدهما يحرق لكن فيه ما يمنع عن الإحراق، وفي الآخر ما يمنع هذا المانع عن المنع فيحرق، لا أن لم يكن فيه إحراق، أو كلاهما كانا كذلك. وأمر الأعراض عندك على ما ذكرنا، ومحال حلول المانع فيه لو كان طويلاً أو أسود، وكذا في الهيولى، لذلك اختلفا.
And Muhammad bin Shabib opposed them with [the argument] that matter before the occurrence of accidents is not long, and accidents are not long, so how did it become long upon existence? And likewise the accident. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to combine between what is not empty and what is empty, and it would become empty, and similar in all accidents like blackness becomes empty, and blackness. So he answered them with [the example of] lime and arsenic, that each one of them individually does not burn, and upon combination it burns. So it is said: What prevents that one of them burns but there is something in it that prevents burning, and in the other something that prevents this preventer from preventing, so it burns, not that there was no burning in it, or both were like that. And the affair of accidents according to you is as we mentioned, and it is impossible for the preventer to inhere in it if it were long or black, and likewise in matter, therefore they differ.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector, Muhammad bin Shabib, argues against the materialist view that accidents arise from the turning of power in matter. He points out that matter before accidents is not long, and accidents themselves are not long, yet when combined they produce length. Similarly, he claims that if it were possible to combine what is not empty with what is empty to get emptiness, this would apply to all accidents like blackness. The answer uses the example of lime and arsenic: each alone does not burn, but together they burn. The explanation is that one of them inherently burns but has a preventer, and the other removes that preventer, so burning occurs. This shows that combined properties can differ from individual ones without requiring a separate cause in the substrate. Maturidi concludes that the same logic applies to accidents and matter, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
هيولى (matter)
أعراض (accidents)
نورة (lime)
زرنيخ (arsenic)
مانع (preventer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Properties of compounds are merely the sum of properties of their parts.
Correction: Emergent properties can arise from combination without being present in the individual parts, as shown by lime and arsenic.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the lime and arsenic analogy in this passage?
A) To prove that matter and accidents are eternal
B) To show that combined properties can differ from individual ones without needing a new cause
C) To demonstrate that burning requires a special substance
D) To argue that all accidents are equally real

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the lime and arsenic analogy to show that emergent properties do not require a separate cause in the substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If lime and arsenic alone lack burning but together produce fire, what does that tell you about the hidden 'preventers' in your own soul that stop your good deeds from igniting? Can you name one such preventer you've been ignoring?
?
The objector assumed that properties must be traceable to parts. In your life, have you ever judged a person or situation based only on isolated traits, missing the transformative power of combination? How might you start seeing the whole as more than the sum of its parts?
?
Maturidi’s explanation involves one substance removing the other's preventer. Who in your life has been a 'remover of preventers' for you spiritually? More importantly, for whom are you called to be that remover today? What specific step will you take?
