Refutation of al-Nazzam's Causal Explanations for Animal Natures
Maturidi refutes al-Nazzam's causal arguments about animal differences as presumptuous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of causal reasoning about animal natures and his insistence that only God's creative will explains the diversity of creatures.

Core Idea
Seeking causes beyond God's creative will is presumptuous and futile.

Imam Maturidi here confronts a rationalist attempt to explain animal differences through natural causes. Al-Nazzam had argued with a sceptical group about why fish die on land, why some animals live in both water and land, and why bats and lions see differently. Maturidi dismisses this entire line of reasoning as frivolous and an arbitrary imposition on Allah. Let us read his response and understand why theological humility is essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the examples al-Nazzam gives and Maturidi's sharp dismissal.
The passage begins with a report of al-Nazzam's argument, then Maturidi's response.
2
Identify al-Nazzam's claims
List the four examples al-Nazzam uses: fish, water-dog, bat, lion. For each, note the cause he assigns (e.g., moisture and cold for fish).
These are attempts at causal explanation based on natural temperaments.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi calls the whole thing 'عبث' (frivolous) and 'تحكم على رب العالمين' (an arbitrary imposition on the Lord of the worlds). He states the correct view: things are as God created them, by His nature.
He also says humans have no permission or perception for such causal speculation.
4
Reflect on the methodological principle
Maturidi distinguishes between causal reasoning permitted by revelation (تحقيق الأعيان) and unauthorised speculation. Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology?
This lesson guards against overreliance on secondary causes when explaining creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلّف نوع ما كلّم النَّظام السُّمَنية مما لا يُجدِى نفعًا. فزعم أن الحيتان كان الغلبة في طبائعها الرطوبة والبرودة، فإذا صارت إلى الجد، والغالب عليه الحرارة واليبوسة، غلبتا على الرطوبة والنُّدُوة فأهلكتا [الحيتان]. وكذلك كل متضادين من الطبائع إذا غلب واحد ضده أهلكه. وكذلك أمر الطائر في السماء وكلب الماء، فإنه أشد اعتدالاً من الحوت، يعيش في الماء والبر. والخفاش فإن بصره مُستَرِقة ليست بالقوية، يُذهبه ضوء الشمس، نحو ما يغشى الرجل إذا نظر إلى عين الشمس، فإذا غابت الشمس ذهب ما أضعف بصره فأبصر، فإذا اشتدت الظلمة لا وأما الأسد فهو قوى البصر ، يبصر بالنهار أكثر ما يبصر غيره، وكذلك يبصر . المانع له بالليل أقل مما يمنع غيره. {قال / أبو منصور رحمه الله : } وذلك كله عبث. بل القول إنه كذلك خلق، وبهذا الطبع جبل : بعض الجواهر يطير في السماء، وآخر يسبح في الماء، والثالث يمشى على وجه الأرض. فتكلّف الاعتلال لمثل هذا تحكم على رب العالمين، واعتلال بما لم يؤذن له ولا له به درك . وليس ذلك من نوع ما ضمن الشرع فيه من تحقيق الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he undertook a type of argument that al-Nazzam had with the Samaniyya, which yields no benefit. He claimed that fish have moisture and cold prevailing in their natures, so when they go to the shore, where heat and dryness prevail, these overcome the moisture and dampness and destroy the fish. Likewise every two opposites of natures, if one overcomes its opposite, it destroys it. And similarly the matter of the bird in the sky and the water-dog, for it is more balanced than the fish, living in water and on land. And the bat: its sight is thin, not strong; sunlight overcomes it, like what happens to a person when he looks at the sun's disc; when the sun sets, what weakened his sight goes and he sees. But when darkness becomes intense, [he does not see]. As for the lion, his sight is strong; he sees by day more than others see, and likewise he sees; what prevents him at night is less than what prevents others. Abu Mansur said, may Allah have mercy on him: All that is frivolous. Rather, the statement is that it is created thus, and by this nature it is constituted: some substances fly in the sky, another swims in water, and the third walks on the face of the earth. Undertaking causal explanation for such things is an arbitrary imposition upon the Lord of the worlds, and a reasoning for which he has been given no permission and for which he has no perception. And that is not of the type about which the Law has undertaken the verification of entities. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Maturidi of al-Nazzam's attempt to explain the different natures of animals by causal reasoning. Al-Nazzam had argued with the Samaniyya (a sceptical group) about why fish die when they come to shore, why some animals live both in water and on land, why bats cannot see in light, and why lions see better at night. He posited causes like the predominance of moisture and cold in fish, the balance of the water-dog, and the weakness of the bat's vision relative to sunlight. Maturidi dismisses all this as 'عبث' (frivolous). He states the correct position: things are as God created them, and they are constituted by that nature. Some substances fly, some swim, some walk. Trying to find causal explanations for such things is an arbitrary imposition (تحكم) upon Allah, and it is a kind of reasoning for which humans have no warrant (إذن) and no capability (درك). Moreover, such causal speculation is not part of what the Law (الشرع) has undertaken to verify about the true natures of entities. Maturidi is arguing that God's creative will is the sufficient reason for the diversity of natures; seeking secondary causes beyond what revelation permits is presumptuous and leads to error.

Key Terms
الطبائع
الاعتلال
تحكم
الشرع
تحقيق الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural philosophy (causal explanation) is always permissible and can explain the diversity of animal natures adequately.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such causal reasoning about the natures of created things is presumptuous when it goes beyond what is revealed. The correct position is that things are as God created them, and seeking secondary causes is an arbitrary imposition on Allah.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi characterize al-Nazzam's causal explanations for animal natures?
A) Beneficial and correct
B) Frivolous (عبث) and an arbitrary imposition on God
C) A valid form of rational inquiry
D) Supported by the Law (الشرع)

Key Takeaway
Only God's creative will explains the diversity of natures; human causal reasoning in such matters is presumptuous and beyond our warrant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the intricate differences in creation—why some animals thrive in water, others in air, some by night, some by day—do you find yourself attributing it to 'nature' or 'evolution' as a self-sufficient explanation, rather than directly seeing the hand of Allah creating each as He wills? How does that subtle shift affect your sense of gratitude and dependence on Him?
?
Al-Nazzam constructed elaborate causal theories that seemed plausible, yet Maturidi calls them 'frivolous' and an 'arbitrary imposition upon Allah.' In what areas of your life do you cling to your own rationalizations to explain why things happen, rather than submitting to the simple truth that Allah is the doer of all? What would it cost you to surrender that need for a secondary cause?
?
This passage says that causal reasoning about animal natures is 'beyond human perception' and 'without permission.' Is there a matter in your own life where you are trying to penetrate Allah's wisdom—demanding to know 'why' something is as it is—when He has not granted you that insight? Would you be willing to stop and simply say, 'He created it thus,' without further inquiry?

Refutation of Naturalistic Explanations for Animal Habitats
Maturidi refutes natural causation for animal habitats, affirming creation by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of natural causation as an arbitrary imposition on God, and his affirmation that all creatures are directly created by God with their specific natures.

Core Idea
Natural causes are not independent; all creatures are created by God with their natures.

Imam Maturidi presents the arguments of al-Nazzam regarding how natural qualities explain differences in animal habitats. He then refutes these explanations as vain attempts to impose laws on God. This lesson examines both the naturalistic claims and Maturidi's response, highlighting the Islamic position that all creation is directly from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that Maturidi first reports al-Nazzam's argument using examples.
Pay attention to the examples of fish, otter, bat, and lion.
2
Identify al-Nazzam's naturalistic logic
Al-Nazzam explains animal traits by dominance of elemental qualities (moisture, cold, heat, dryness). For example, fish die on land because heat and dryness overcome their moist cold nature. Understand how each example fits this pattern.
This is a form of natural causation.
3
Analyze Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi declares all that as 'عبث' (vain) and says the true explanation is that God created creatures thus. Calling it an arbitrary imposition on God (تحكم على رب العالمين).
Note the key phrase 'تحكم على رب العالمين'.
4
Reflect on the theological conclusion
Maturidi affirms that creatures are created directly by God with their natures. Natural explanations without divine permission are beyond human knowledge and not part of the Law's concern.
This establishes that ultimate causation belongs to God alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلّف نوع ما كلّم النَّظام السُّمَنية مما لا يُجدِى نفعًا. فزعم أن الحيتان كان الغلبة في طبائعها الرطوبة والبرودة، فإذا صارت إلى الجد، والغالب عليه الحرارة واليبوسة، غلبتا على الرطوبة والنُّدُوة فأهلكتا [الحيتان]. وكذلك كل متضادين من الطبائع إذا غلب واحد ضده أهلكه. وكذلك أمر الطائر في السماء وكلب الماء، فإنه أشد اعتدالاً من الحوت، يعيش في الماء والبر. والخفاش فإن بصره مُستَرِقَة ليست بالقوية، يُذهبه ضوء الشمس، نحو ما يغشى الرجل إذا نظر إلى عين الشمس، فإذا غابت الشمس ذهب ما أضعف بصره فأبصر، فإذا اشتدت الظلمة لا وأما الأسد فهو قوى البصر ، يبصر بالنهار أكثر ما يبصر غيره، وكذلك يبصر . المانع له بالليل أقل مما يمنع غيره. {قال / أبو منصور رحمه الله : } وذلك كله عبث. بل القول إنه كذلك خلق، وبهذا الطبع جبل : بعض الجواهر يطير في السماء، وآخر يسبح في الماء، والثالث يمشى على وجه الأرض. فتكلّف الاعتلال لمثل هذا تحكم على رب العالمين، واعتلال بما لم يؤذن له ولا له به درك . وليس ذلك من نوع ما ضمن الشرع فيه من تحقيق الأعيان. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he burdens himself with a kind of speech that al-Nazzam spoke to the Summaniyya, which brings no benefit. He claimed that fish have the dominance in their natures of moisture and cold; when they come to the shore, where the dominant is heat and dryness, the two overcome the moisture and wetness and destroy the fish. Likewise every two opposites in natures when one overcomes its opposite it destroys it. Likewise the matter of the bird in the sky and the otter: it is more balanced than the fish, living in water and land. The bat: its vision is subtle and not strong; sunlight blinds it, like what happens to a man when he looks at the eye of the sun; when the sun sets, that which weakened his vision goes away and he sees. When darkness intensifies... And as for the lion, it has strong vision; it sees during the day more than others see, and similarly it sees at night less than what prevents others. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: All of that is vain. Rather, the statement is that it is created thus, and with this nature it is constituted: some substances fly in the sky, another swims in water, and a third walks on the face of the earth. To burden oneself with a causal explanation for such is an arbitrary imposition on the Lord of the worlds, and an explanation by what he has not been permitted, nor does he have perception of it. And that is not of the type that the law has guaranteed concerning the verification of entities. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of naturalistic causation. Maturidi reports an argument from al-Nazzam (a Mu'tazili figure) addressed to the Summaniyya (a skeptical sect). The argument explains why fish die on land (elemental dominance), why otters are amphibious (balanced nature), why bats can't see in sunlight (weak vision), and why lions see well in day but poorly at night, all based on elemental qualities. Maturidi responds that this is all vain. The true explanation is direct divine creation: God created creatures with their natures—some fly, some swim, some walk. Attempting to provide natural causes is an arbitrary imposition on God, without permission or knowledge. Moreover, this is not among matters the Law (shar') has undertaken to verify. Thus, Maturidi reaffirms creation by God and rejects reduction of it to natural necessity.

Refuting the Claim that Composition Causes Death
Maturidi refutes the philosopher's composition-necessitates-death argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's threefold refutation of the philosophical argument that composition necessarily leads to death.

Core Idea
Death is not inevitable due to composition alone; proof and variability refute it.

Philosophers argued that animals die because of their bodily composition—a necessary outcome of being made of parts. Maturidi exposes the weakness of this claim by pointing out its conflict with prophetic knowledge and its lack of universal applicability. This lesson unpacks his concise rebuttal, showing why such a causal argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosopher's claim
Start by understanding what the philosophers assert: that the composition of an animal is inherently mortal. This is the claim Maturidi is refuting.
The claim is that composition = death, universally.
2
Identify Maturidi's first argument
Recognize that Maturidi calls the claim foolish because it ignores the possibility of knowledge through messengers, which is supported by proof.
He ties the refutation to the broader issue of accepting revelation.
3
Examine the second argument
Note that the philosopher has not tested all possible natures or intellects, so his generalization is unjustified.
Empirical insufficiency undermines the claim.
4
Understand the third argument
If composition alone caused death, all living beings would have the same lifespan. But lifespans vary, so composition cannot be the sole cause.
This is a logical counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في قول الفلسفة : إن تركيب الحيوان تركيب يموت . تأملوا حماقته، بعد قول قوم: لو أدركوه لأدركوه بالرسل، ثم ينكر قول الرسل مع البرهان والثاني أنه لم يمتحن عقول الفلاسفة، ولا هم امتحنوا طبائع الجميع. والثالث أنه لو كان بالتركيب لما اختلف قدر جميع الحياة.
And he said concerning the statement of the philosophers: ‘The composition of the animal is a composition that dies.’ Consider his foolishness, after the saying of a people: ‘If they had grasped it, they would have grasped it by the messengers,’ then he denies the saying of the messengers along with proof. Second, that he did not test the intellects of the philosophers, nor did they test the natures of all. Third, that if it were due to composition, then the measure of all life would not differ.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a philosopher’s claim that death is a necessary result of animal composition. Al-Maturidi first calls this claim foolish because it contradicts those who say that true knowledge of death comes only through messengers. The philosopher rejects messenger-based knowledge despite clear proofs. Al-Maturidi then gives two additional rebuttals: (1) The philosopher has not tested all possible natures or intellects to generalize that composition always leads to death. (2) If death were purely due to composition, then all living beings would have the same lifespan; but lifespans vary greatly, showing that composition alone is not the cause. Thus, the philosopher’s argument is insufficient to deny the possibility of miracles or messenger-based knowledge.

Key Terms

فلسفة
حماقة
رسل
برهان
طبائع
حياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is necessarily caused by bodily composition; all composed things must die.
Correction: Maturidi shows that composition does not necessitate death because lifespans vary and the claim lacks universal empirical support.

Checkpoint

A) The philosophers have not tested all compositions.
B) If composition caused death, all life would have the same duration.
C) Death is known only through messengers.
D) The philosophers are fools.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the deterministic causal link between composition and death with logical and empirical counterarguments, defending the possibility of prophetic knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosopher rejected prophetic knowledge without testing all possibilities. Where have you rejected a divine message because it didn't fit your limited experience? Write down one area and commit to re-examining it with the openness this lesson demands.

Answer to the Fourth Objection: Special Power as a Sign
Maturidi answers the objection that a special power could be a natural cause, not a divine sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's response to the objection that the Qur'an's inimitability could be due to a natural special power, and how he defends it as a divine sign.

Core Idea
Special powers can't explain the Qur'an's uniqueness; unmatched uniqueness points to divine origin.

Maturidi addresses the fourth objection, which suggests that the Qur'an's miraculous nature could be a natural gift. He offers two arguments to show that such special powers are limited and never produce something truly unmatched. This passage clarifies that the Qur'an's inimitability is inherently a sign from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi structures his argument with two distinct points.
The text has some lacunae; focus on the overall logic.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two arguments: first, the analogy of the magnet stone; second, the observation that exceptional abilities are always matched by others. Locate each in the text.
The second argument is introduced by 'والثاني'.
3
Understand the implications
Ask yourself: How does the magnet analogy refute the idea that a special power makes one a prophet? Why does the fact that the Qur'an remains unmatched prove it is a divine sign?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب الرابع أن الله تعالى إذا خص أحدا بقوة لا يشاركه فيها أحد يمنعه عن دعوى النبوة باللفظ، كما منع من يظفر بحجر المغناطيس، ولو علم أنه يدعى لا يعطيه. والثاني أن لا أحد، في شيء له فضل قوة إلا طمع غيره استتمام ذلك أو عَمَلَ ذلك النوع بقدر قوته، والدليل ما يخرج عن الطباع . وبعد، فإنه لو كان له في ذلك فضل قوة / بها عمل لكان لا يتمكن [..] نیلها بهم، وليست لهم، إذ لا يوجد مثل ذا في شيء من الأمور ؛ دل أن الله جعل [ه] فيه ليكون آية لقوله. وسنذكر جمل هذه التأويلات بعد الفراغ من فصوله.
And the answer to the fourth [objection] is that God Almighty, when He singles out someone with a power in which no one shares, prevents him from claiming prophethood by [mere] words, just as He prevented the one who obtains the magnet stone [from claiming it], and if he knew that it is claimed, He would not give it. The second [point] is that no one who has an excess of power in something but that another desires to complete that or to do that type according to his power, and the evidence is what goes beyond natural temperaments. Moreover, if he had in that an excess of power by which he acted, then he would not be able to [؟] attain it [from] them, and they do not have it, since similar is not found in any matter; this indicates that God placed it in him to be a sign for his statement. And we will mention the sum of these interpretations after finishing its chapters.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a specific objection. The objection likely claimed that the Qur'an's uniqueness could be due to a natural gift rather than a divine sign. Maturidi counters with two arguments. First, if God gives someone an exclusive power, He prevents that person from using it to falsely claim prophethood, analogous to how the man who found a magnet stone did not magically gain all magnetic powers. Second, whenever someone has an exceptional ability, others will try to match or surpass it, but the Qur'an remains unmatched, indicating it is beyond natural causes. Therefore, the Qur'an's inimitability is a deliberate divine sign.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwah - power)
آية (āyah - sign)
طباع (ṭibāʿ - natural temperaments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Exceptional human abilities can explain the miracles of prophets.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such abilities are always matched by others, whereas the Qur'an remains unmatched, showing it is a divine sign.

Checkpoint

A) A poet's talent.
B) A man who finds a magnet stone.
C) A king's authority.
D) A philosopher's reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that special powers are limited and never produce something genuinely inimitable, so the Qur'an must be a divine sign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly feel its unmatched power as a sign from God, or has familiarity dulled your sense of awe? What would it take for you to approach it as if hearing it for the first time?

?
The magnet stone prevented its owner from falsely claiming prophethood. In your life, are there gifts or abilities you have that could lead you to claim something you are not—such as spiritual authority or expertise? How can you ensure that your strengths point to God, not to yourself?

Causality and Created Things

The Inability to Replicate Human Actions as Proof of Divine Agency
Maturidi argues human actions are not truly theirs due to inability to replicate them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are not fully within human control because they cannot be replicated, and how this proves the existence of God and His exclusive power.

Core Idea
You cannot recreate even one of your own actions—proving it is not truly yours.

Maturidi presents a profound argument from the nature of human action. He observes that when we try to reproduce an action exactly, we fail—the original moment with its specific conditions is gone. This leads him to distinguish between two aspects of action and to conclude that the creative aspect belongs solely to God. The proof is concise but powerful, using a logical conditional that ties human incapacity to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the technical terms like 'qadr' (measure) and 'al-wajh al-awwal' (first aspect).
2
Identify the two aspects of action
The passage distinguishes between a first aspect (the action's precise measure in space/place/limit) and a second aspect (voluntary movement like response to commands).
The first aspect is about the action's specific conditions; the second is about intentionality.
3
Trace the conditional argument
Maturidi argues: if actions were truly from the first aspect, then absurd consequences follow—like the world being from a powerless being and miracles from humans. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The conditional shows that the first aspect cannot originate from humans.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the absurd is false, the only conclusion is that human actions are beyond their capacity, just as miracles are beyond human power. This necessitates affirming the Maker and the Messengers.
The Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' supports the uniqueness of divine acts.
5
Reflect on the Quranic verse and final statement
The verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (Nothing is like Him) establishes that divine acts are incomparable, and human actions cannot resemble them in origination.
The final phrase 'La quwwata illa billah' emphasizes that all power is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكأخذ الفعل من قدر الجو والمكان والحد الذي لو أحب أن يعود إليه ما أمكنه تلاقيه، والثاني نحو التحرك والسكون بالمنهى والمأمور به ثبت أن فعلهم من الوجه الأول ليس لهم، ومن الثاني لهم. ولو جاز تحقيق فعلهم من الوجه الأول على ظهور خروجه عن قصدهم - وجملتهم مختلفة مما ذكر ـ وعجزهم عن العود إلى مثله الجاز كون العالم على ما عليه بمن لا يقدر ولا يعلم ولا يعرف مقادير كل شيء، ويجوز أيضاً آيات - على ما هي عليه ـ بالبشر وإن لم يكن لهم بمثلها علم ولا عليها قدرة. فإذ لزمهم القول بالصانع والرسل بخروج الذي ذكرت عن وسع الخلق فمثله أفعال الخلق ؛ ولذلك قال الله سبحانه: ليس كمثله شيء وأوجب أن تشابه الخلق من الوجه الذي قلت تماثل. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the taking of the action from the measure of the atmosphere and place and limit such that if one wished to return to it, he could not encounter it; and the second is like movement and rest by what is forbidden and commanded; it is established that their action from the first aspect is not theirs, and from the second it is theirs. And if it were permissible that their action be actualized from the first aspect, while it appears that it comes out contrary to their intention, and their situation is diverse from what was mentioned, and their inability to return to the like of it, then it would be permissible for the world to be as it is by one who does not have power, knowledge, or knowledge of the measures of everything; and it would also be permissible for signs (āyāt) as they are to be from humans even if they have no knowledge of their like nor power over them. Since they are compelled to affirm the Maker and the Messengers because what I have mentioned is beyond the capacity of creation, then similarly the actions of creation [are beyond them]. And therefore Allah the Exalted said: 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11), and He necessitated that the creation resemble from the aspect I said they are similar. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that human actions are not entirely within human control. Maturidi begins by analyzing the act of 'taking' an action from the perspective of its measure (qadr) in space, place, and limit. If a person wanted to reproduce that exact action, they cannot retrieve it. This shows a first aspect where the action is not theirs. The second aspect involves deliberate movements like being commanded or prohibited, where the action appears to be theirs. Maturidi argues that if one claims actions belong to humans in the first sense (as fully originating from them), then it would also be logically permissible for the world to be caused by a powerless, ignorant being, and for miracles to be performed by humans without knowledge or power. Since that is absurd, the only conclusion is that the Maker and Messengers are real, and human actions, like miracles, are beyond human capacity. The Quranic verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' reinforces that divine acts are unique; human actions cannot resemble them in the sense of being originated ex nihilo. The proof establishes that human actions are created by God.

Key Terms
فعل (action)
قدر (measure/capacity)
الوجه الأول (first aspect)
الصانع (the Maker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans believe they fully create their own actions, including the specific details and conditions.
Correction: Maturidi proves that we cannot even replicate a single action in its precise conditions, showing that the creative aspect belongs solely to Allah.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the first aspect of human action?
A) It is fully under human control
B) It cannot be replicated, so it is not truly theirs
C) It is the same as divine action
D) It is purely imaginary

Key Takeaway
Human actions, in their precise measures, are beyond human replication, proving they are created by Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today that you would like to recreate perfectly—same tone, same timing, same feeling. Why can't you? What does this failure reveal about the limits of your own power, and how should it drive you to rely on Allah every moment?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you think of your sin as entirely your own independent creation? How does knowing that Allah creates even the exact measure of that sin affect the way you seek repentance and trust in His mercy?
?
Every action you take is a unique, unrepeatable event. If you cannot return to the exact same action, what does that say about your grip on your own life? How does this realization compel you to make each action a conscious act of worship?

Argument from the Unknown Moral Quality of Actions
Maturidi argues that humans do not know the true extent of good or evil in their actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the unknown moral extent of actions proves that actions are not created by humans and that moral qualities must originate from Allah.

Core Idea
If you don't know the full good or evil of your action, you cannot claim it as your own.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a subtle argument: when we act, we intend good but often end up with something different. This shows that the moral quality of our actions is beyond our control. He then addresses a potential objection and draws a radical conclusion: if good and evil could exist without an originator, everything could, which would undermine Islam. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi observes that people do not know the extent of good or evil in their actions, yet they act as if they do.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يعلم أهلها أنها يبلغ في الحسن ذلك ولا في القبح' – they don't know the extent.
2
Consider the objection
Maturidi anticipates an objection: someone might claim that actions are good or evil in themselves. Identify this objection in the text.
3
Follow the refutation
Maturidi gives two refutations: first, if good/evil are inherent, Allah is more entitled to the action than itself; second, if good/evil could exist without an originator, then anything could, which leads to unbelief. Trace these steps.
Understand the logic: claiming inherent qualities leads to atheism.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final conclusion is that actions cannot be from humans in terms of their moral quality; they must be from Allah. Consider how this argument fits into Maturidi's overall theology of acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد أفعال العباد تخرج على حسن وقبح لا يعلم أهلها أنها يبلغ في الحسن ذلك ولا في القبح، بل الهم عند أنفسهم في تحسينها وتزيينها، وهي تخرج على غير ذلك، بأن جعل أفعالهم على ما هي عليه ليست لهم؛ ولو جاز كونها على ذلك لهم ـ وهم لا يعرفون مبلغ الحسن والقبح ـ فإذا لا جهل بقبح الفعل ولا بحسنه. فثبت أن فعلهم من هذا الوجه ليس لهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. اللهم إلا أن يقولوا: هي لأنفسها كانت كذلك. فإذا استقام حسن الفعل وقبحه بمنزلة الفعل نفسه، فالله تعالى أحق بالشيء من نفسه، إذ الشيء بحيث نفسه جاهل بما هو عليه. مع ما لو جاز كون حسن وقبح بلا منشئ له لجاز كون كل شيء بلا منشئ، وفي ذلك الخروج من الإسلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, we find that the actions of servants emerge upon good and evil, and their owners do not know that they reach that extent in good nor in evil; rather, their concern in themselves is to make them good and to embellish them, but they emerge contrary to that, because the making of their actions as they are is not from them. And if it were permissible that they be like that from them – while they do not know the extent of good and evil – then there would be no ignorance of the evil of the action nor of its good. Thus it is established that their action from this aspect is not theirs. And there is no power except in Allah. Unless they say: 'They were so in themselves.' Then if the good and evil of the action are established like the action itself, then Allah the Exalted is more entitled to the thing than itself, because the thing is such that it is ignorant of what it is. Moreover, if it were permissible that good and evil exist without an originator, then it would be permissible for everything to exist without an originator, and in that there is departure from Islam. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a proof that human actions are not created by humans, based on the unpredictable moral quality of actions. Maturidi observes that people often intend to do good and adorn their actions, yet the actions turn out differently – sometimes evil. This shows that the actual moral quality of the action is not under their control. If the action were truly theirs, they would know its full extent of good or evil, but they do not. Therefore, the action is not from them in this aspect. He then considers an objection: someone might claim that the action is good or evil in itself (by its own nature). Maturidi refutes this by saying that if an action's moral property were inherent, then Allah has more right to determine it than the action itself, because the action is ignorant of its own nature. He further argues that if good and evil could exist without an originator, then everything could exist without a creator, which leads to unbelief. Thus, the moral quality of actions must be from Allah.

Key Terms
حسن (good)
قبح (evil)
مبلغ (extent)
منشئ (originator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think actions are inherently good or evil, independent of divine determination.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if moral qualities are inherent, then Allah has more right to determine the action's nature, and claiming inherent goodness/evil without an originator leads to unbelief.

Checkpoint

A) That would prove the action is from the human
B) Allah is more entitled to that action than itself
C) The action becomes neutral
D) It contradicts free will

The moral quality of actions cannot be fully known by humans, proving that actions originate from Allah.

The Painful and Harmful Nature of Actions as Proof of Divine Creation
Maturidi argues that actions cause pain and fatigue, which humans do not intend

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the unintended harmful consequences of actions point to Allah as the creator of those actions.

Core Idea
Actions have effects we don't intend, revealing a Creator beyond ourselves.

Maturidi presents a subtle argument: when we act, we often experience pain or fatigue, even though we intended pleasure. This shows that not all aspects of our actions come from our own will. In this lesson, we will examine his reasoning and see how it establishes divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetition of 'harmful', 'tiring', 'painful'.
2
Identify Maturidi's key observation
Maturidi observes that actions are often harmful, tiring, and painful for the doer. This is a common experience.
3
Understand the logical implication
He argues that it is impossible for harm, fatigue, or pain to occur without a cause. Therefore, these effects must have an originator.
This is a causal principle.
4
Contrast intention with outcome
The doer's intention is to enjoy and benefit, not to suffer. Since the harmful effects are not intended, they cannot be from the doer.
5
Draw the conclusion
Thus, the harmful aspects of actions must come from an external originator, i.e., Allah. This shows that actions are not entirely created by humans.
This supports Maturidi's view on kasb (acquisition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is established that they are painful, tiring, and harmful due to their originator. And it is known that the intention of their owners is to enjoy them and take pleasure, so it is established that they are thus not from them. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This is a short proof that actions are created by God based on their unintended harmful consequences. Maturidi observes that actions often cause pain, fatigue, and harm to the doer, even though the doer intends pleasure and benefit. Since it is impossible for harm to occur without a cause, and the doer did not intend that harm, the harmful aspect must come from an external originator (Allah). Therefore, the action's overall reality (including its harmful effects) is not from the human agent alone.

متعبة (tiring)
مؤلمة (painful)
منشئ (originator)

Misconception: People assume all effects of their actions are intended and caused solely by themselves.
Correction: Maturidi shows that actions have unintended harmful effects, which must come from an external originator (Allah). This proves that humans are not the sole creators of their actions.

Checkpoint

C) Actions are always pleasant
D) Humans are incapable of action

Key Takeaway
The unintended harmful effects of our actions point to Allah as the true creator of those actions.

?

The Nature of Human Actions and Divine Power
Maturidi defines human actions and argues divine power over them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions are essentially movements and rests under Allah's power, and that human power is temporary, establishing human servitude.

Core Idea
Human actions are only movements and rests; all power ultimately belongs to Allah.

Maturidi begins by examining the nature of human actions. He argues that they are nothing more than movements and rests, which are fully within Allah's power. From this, he draws a profound conclusion about the temporary nature of human agency, reinforcing the distinction between Creator and servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Maturidi defines human actions as movements and rests.
This is a logical proof, not a mere statement.
2
Trace the logic of empowerment
Follow the reasoning: Allah is powerful over actions, so when He empowers a servant, the power is temporary and departs. This shows the servant is not independent.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that one described with temporary power is a servant, not a lord. Ask yourself: why does this matter for tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن العباد إذ أفعالهم في الحقيقة حركات وسكون في الظاهر، والله قادر عليها، لو لا ذلك ما أقدرهم عليها، فصارت هي لأنفسها تحت قدرته عليها، فإذا أقدر العبد على ذلك ذهبت عنه القدرة، فإذا قدرته زالت عنه، وصار قادرًا بقدرة تزول، ومن وصفه فهو عبد لا رب. والله الموفق. مع ما كانت الحركة والسكون ليسا بمخالفين في رأى العين لما كانا عليه، ولا سبيل للناظر إلى التفريق بينهما، ولولا حقيقة الاشتباه لاحتمل التفريق. وفي تشابه الفعلين لزوم القول فيهما بما له وجبت التسمية في أحدهما، وفي ذلك تشابه؛ لأن استواء الأفعال في الشاهد يوجب تشابه الفاعلين. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the servants – since their actions are in reality movements and rests in appearance, and Allah is powerful over them, for if it were not so, He would not have empowered them over them – so they become under His power. And when He empowers the servant over that, power departs from him; then when his power leaves him, he becomes powerful by a power that passes away, and whoever is described thus is a servant, not a lord. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Moreover, movement and rest are not different in the sight of the eye from what they are, and the observer has no way to distinguish between them. And were it not for the reality of resemblance, differentiation would be possible. And in the similarity of the two actions, there is a necessity to say concerning them what made the attribution (tasmīya) obligatory in one of them, and in that there is similarity; because the equality of actions in the witnessed realm necessitates the similarity of the agents. And there is no power except in Allah.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the nature of motion and rest. Maturidi argues that human actions are essentially movements and rests, which are visible. Allah has power over all such movements, and He grants humans power temporarily. If a human were truly the creator of his movement, his power would not be temporary and passing. Moreover, movement and rest look identical whether performed by God or a human; an observer cannot tell them apart. If both actions appear the same, then if one is attributed to God as creator, the other must be attributed in the same way – otherwise there would be an arbitrary distinction. Since we cannot see any difference, the actions must have the same source – i.e., Allah. This avoids anthropomorphism and preserves divine oneness.

Key Terms
حركة (movement)
سكون (rest)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans create their own actions independently.
Correction: Maturidi shows that actions are movements/rests, and Allah's power encompasses them; human power is only borrowed and temporary.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens when Allah empowers a servant over an action?
A) The servant becomes independent
B) The servant's power becomes eternal
C) The servant's power departs
D) The action becomes uncreated

Key Takeaway
Human actions are only movements and rests; their power is temporary, underscoring that only Allah is the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any action today—from the smallest movement to a major decision—will you pause to remember that your ability to do so is a fleeting gift from Allah? How might this awareness change your reliance on Him in that moment?
?
If all your power to act is temporary and will depart, what does that say about the deeds you are building your life upon? Are you investing in what lasts with Allah or in what passes away with your power?
?
The passage declares that only Allah is the true Lord; you are but a servant with borrowed strength. In which area of your life have you been acting as if you have independent, permanent ownership—your career, your relationships, your plans? Can you hand that area back to Him today?

The Visual Similarity of Movement and Rest
Maturidi argues from visual similarity to divine unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that because movement and rest look identical, they must be attributed to the same source – Allah.

Core Idea
If movement and rest appear the same, they cannot have different creators.

In the second part of his argument, Maturidi turns to the observable fact that movement and rest look exactly the same whether performed by God or humans. This visual indistinguishability, he argues, forces a conclusion about their origin. If two actions are identical in appearance, it is arbitrary to attribute one to God and the other to humans as a distinct creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on visual similarity

2
Understand the logical implication

Maturidi uses this to argue for divine oneness.
3
Apply to avoid anthropomorphism
Reflect: this reasoning prevents attributing creation of some actions to humans and others to Allah arbitrarily.
This preserves tawhid.

The Painful Nature of Actions Proves a Creator
Maturidi proves from the painful nature of actions that they must have a maker other than the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that since actions are inherently painful and tiring, they cannot be fully produced by human agents but must have a divine source.

Core Idea
Actions cause pain and fatigue; agents seek pleasure, so the pain must come from God.

Maturidi presents a logical proof based on the observable qualities of actions. He points out that actions are often harmful, tiring, and painful to the doer, yet the doer's intention is to derive pleasure. This contradiction indicates that the painful aspects must originate from a source other than the doer. In this lesson, we will examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's logic unfold.
Notice the use of terms like 'مؤذية' and 'متعبة'.
2
Identify the key observation
Maturidi observes that actions are experienced as harmful, tiring, and painful. What does he claim about the agents' intention?
3
Follow the argument from impossibility
He argues it is impossible for nature to be harmed without a harmer. Understand this causal principle.
This is a foundational kalam principle: every effect has a cause.
4
See the conclusion
Since agents intend pleasure, but actions cause pain, the pain must come from God. Understand why this points to a creator.
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this argument relates to the broader Islamic doctrine of divine sovereignty over all actions.
This argument supports that all actions are created by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد الأفعال مؤذية لأهلها ومتعبة ومؤلمة ومحال تأذى الطبع بلا مؤذ وتعب بلا متعب وتألم بلا مؤلم، ثبت أنها مؤلمة متعبة مؤذية بسبب منشئها. ومعلوم أن قصد أربابها إلى أن يتلذذوا بها ويتمتعوا، فثبت أنها كذلك لا بهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also we find that actions are harmful to their doers, tiring, and painful, and it is impossible for nature to be harmed without a harmer, to be tired without a tiring agent, and to be pained without a pain-causer. So it is established that they are painful, tiring, and harmful due to their source. And it is known that the intention of their agents is to delight in them and enjoy them, so it is established that they are such not by them. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the inherent qualities of actions. Maturidi observes that actions are experienced as harmful (مؤذية), tiring (متعبة), and painful (مؤلمة) to the people who perform them. He then argues that it is impossible for something to have these effects without an external cause. Specifically, nature (الطبع) cannot be harmed, tired, or pained without something that harms, tires, or pains it. So the actions must be caused by a source (منشئ) that produces these effects. He then notes that the doers of these actions (أرباب الأفعال) intend, through their actions, to derive pleasure and enjoyment. That means the painful and tiring aspects are not from the doers themselves, since the doers seek the opposite. Thus, the source of those negative qualities must be something other than the doer—namely, God. The argument highlights that the agent's intention is pleasure, so the pain must come from elsewhere, thereby proving divine causation.

Key Terms
أفعال
مؤذية
متعبة
مؤلمة
منشئ
أرباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actions are fully produced by human agents, including their painful aspects.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since agents seek pleasure but actions cause pain, the painful aspects must come from God, not the agent.

Checkpoint

A) Because agents intend pleasure, but actions cause pain.
B) Because agents are weak.
C) Because actions are invisible.
D) Because actions have no cause.

Key Takeaway
Actions are inherently painful and tiring, yet agents seek pleasure, proving that the pain must come from God alone.

?

?

?
If you truly believed that every moment of pain in your obedience is directly from Allah, would you complain less and trust more? What would it take for you to live with that conviction starting today?

Actions as Movements: God's Power and Human Agency
Maturidi explains that human actions are movements/stillness and God's power over them is essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that human actions (movements and stillness) are dependent on God's power, and that their observable similarity necessitates a single divine agent.

Core Idea
Human actions look the same; only God can be their true cause.

Maturidi presents a twofold argument about human actions. First, he establishes that all actions are fundamentally movements and stillness, and God has power over them. Second, he uses the fact that movements and stillness appear identical to the eye to argue that they must have the same creator. This lesson unpacks both parts and shows why human agency is dependent and temporary.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the two distinct sections: the first about power, the second about visual similarity.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms حركات (movements), سكون (stillness), قدرة (power), عبد (servant), رب (lord). How does Maturidi use them?
3
Analyze the first argument
Trace the logic: God has power over actions → He empowers humans → that power is temporary → humans are servants, not lords.
Consider what 'power vanishes' means for human agency.
4
Analyze the second argument
Movement and stillness look identical to the eye → no one can distinguish the source → similarity of effects implies similarity of causes → God is the cause of all actions.
This is a key kalam principle: likeness in effects indicates likeness in agents.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that since human actions are indistinguishable from God’s creative acts, they must be created by God. How does this affirm tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن العباد إذ أفعالهم في الحقيقة حركات وسكون في الظاهر، والله قادر عليها، لو لا ذلك ما أقدرهم عليها، فصارت هي لأنفسها تحت قدرته عليها، فإذا أقدر العبد على ذلك ذهبت عنه القدرة، فإذا قدرته زالت عنه، وصار قادرًا بقدرة تزول، ومن وصفه فهو عبد لا رب. والله الموفق. مع ما كانت الحركة والسكون ليسا بمخالفين في رأى العين لما كانا عليه، ولا سبيل للناظر إلى التفريق بينهما، ولولا حقيقة الاشتباه لاحتمل التفريق. وفي تشابه الفعلين لزوم القول فيهما بما له وجبت التسمية في أحدهما، وفي ذلك تشابه؛ لأن استواء الأفعال في الشاهد يوجب تشابه الفاعلين. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, indeed the servants—since their actions in reality are movements and stillness in appearance—and God is powerful over them. If that were not the case, He would not have empowered them over them. So they themselves become under His power over them. When He empowers the servant over that, the power departs from the servant, and when the servant's power leaves him, he becomes powerful with a power that vanishes. And whoever describes him thus, he is a servant, not a lord. And God is the giver of success. Along with the fact that movement and stillness are not different in the eye's view from what they were, and the observer has no way to distinguish between them. Were it not for the reality of ambiguity, differentiation might be possible. And in the similarity of the two actions, there is a necessity of saying concerning them that which caused the naming in one of them. And in that is similarity, because the equality of actions in the witnessed realm necessitates the similarity of the agents. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This argument focuses on the nature of human actions as movements (حركات) and stillness (سكون). Maturidi asserts that God has power over all actions. If He did not, He would not have given humans the ability to perform them. So all actions remain under God's power. When God gives a human power (القدرة) to act, that power is temporary; it leaves the human after the act. Thus, the human becomes powerful only with a borrowed, vanishing power. Anyone who has such a dependent power is a servant (عبد), not a lord (رب). The second part uses the observation that movement and stillness appear identical to the eye, and no observer can distinguish between a movement caused by God and one caused by a human by mere visual inspection. Since the actions look alike, we must judge that they have the same cause—namely, God. If two actions are identical in appearance, their agents must be similarly empowered. Since God's actions are obviously created, human actions must also be created by God. This argument uses the principle that similarity in effects implies similarity in causes.

Key Terms
حركات
سكون
قدرة
عبد
رب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human actions are fully independent and can be distinguished from God's actions by observation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that movements and stillness appear identical, so we cannot tell the source by looking; similarity of effects implies a single cause—God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can we not distinguish between a movement caused by God and one caused by a human?
A) Because they look identical to the eye.
B) Because humans are weak.
C) Because movements are invisible.
D) Because God hides His actions.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are movements and stillness; their observable similarity shows that only God can be their true cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you act as if your power to move and act is your own, or do you constantly recognize it as a borrowed gift from God? What would change if you truly internalized that every movement is sustained by Him?
?
When you commit a sin, do you blame your own 'free will' or do you acknowledge that even that sin occurs only because God allowed power to be in you? How can this understanding deepen your repentance?
?
If your closest ally and your worst enemy both perform actions that look identical, can you judge their hearts based on the action alone? Does this lesson challenge your assumptions about judging others’ deeds?

The Servant's Power is Granted by God
Maturidi argues that the servant's power is borrowed from God, so it cannot be independent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the servant's power to act is entirely dependent on God's granting, and therefore God is the sole Creator of all actions.

Core Idea
The grantor must possess what he grants; therefore God has power over our actions.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology about human agency: how can we be responsible for actions if God creates them? Maturidi uses a logical analogy: if someone teaches you knowledge, that teacher must himself know that knowledge. Similarly, if God grants you power to act, He must have power over that action. This leads to the conclusion that God is the Creator of the action.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and its translation carefully. Notice the analogy between granting power and teaching knowledge.
2
Identify the analogy
The passage compares enabling power to teaching knowledge. Understand why the analogy holds: the grantor must have the attribute he grants.
3
Understand the principle
If someone teaches you, he must know. If someone enables you, he must have the ability. This is the core logical principle.
4
Apply the principle to God
God enables the servant to have power. Therefore, God must have power over the action. Thus, the action cannot exist independently.
5
Conclude
Since God's power over the action is established, and what God has power over cannot exist by another, God is the Creator of the action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن العبد يقدر بإقدار الله إياه، فلا يجوز أن يقدر بإقدار من ليست له القدرة عليه، كما لا يجوز أن يعلم بإعلام من لا علم له به. أو لا يُرى أنه إذا لم يجز لأحد القدرة على إقدار غيره على شيء [إذا لم يقدر هو عليه، ومن له علم يُعلم به غيره لم يجز أن لا يعلم هو، فمثله الذي [۱۲۱] بينا . وإذا [قد] ثبت قدرة الله عليه " ، وما يقدر / الله عليه فهو محال وجوده بغيره " ، ثبت أنه خالق ذلك.
And also, the servant has power by God's enabling him to have power, so it is not permissible that he have power by the enabling of one who has no power over it, just as it is not permissible that he know by the teaching of one who has no knowledge of it. Or is it not seen that if it is not permissible for anyone to have the power to enable another to do something [if he himself has no power over it], and whoever has knowledge teaches it to another, it is not permissible that he himself does not know. So its example is what we clarified [earlier]. And since God's power over it is established, and what God has power over, its existence by other than Him is impossible, it is established that He is the Creator of that.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the principle that a cause must possess the attribute it grants. The servant's power to act is granted by God, so God must have power over that action. By analogy, if someone teaches knowledge, they must themselves know it. Similarly, the one who grants power must have that power. Since God grants the servant power, God must have power over the action. Therefore, the action cannot exist by another (i.e., the servant independently), and thus God is its Creator. The passage concludes by affirming God's creation of the action.

قدرة (power)
تعليم (teaching)
علم (knowledge)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the servant has independent power to act, because we observe human actions and choices.
Correction: The passage argues that the power to act is granted by God; the grantor must possess what he grants, so God must have power over the action, and therefore the action cannot be independent of God.

Checkpoint

A) Because the servant prays
B) Because God grants the servant power
C) Because the action is good
D) Because the servant is weak

Key Takeaway
God is the sole Creator of actions because He grants the power and only He can create what He has power over.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency in your daily actions—your work, your worship, your speech? What would change if you truly internalized that every ability you have is a direct, momentary grant from God?
?
Think of a time you were praised for an achievement. Did you, even subtly, feel that the credit belonged to you? How can you redirect that feeling to acknowledge God as the sole Creator of that success?
?
If you were to truly live knowing that your power to act is not your own but is borrowed from God, what habit or attitude in your life would have to change first? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself of this truth?

Causality and Created Things

God is the Creator of All Actions: Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi argues that denying God creates actions leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that God must be the Creator of all human actions, and why denying this leads to absurdities.

Core Idea
Denying God creates all actions leads to the absurd conclusion that the servant is more powerful than God.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a concise yet powerful refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that humans create their own actions. He shows that their own definitions and classifications lead to contradictions that both reason and revelation reject. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Define God's act as origination
Maturidi begins by defining God's act as bringing from non-existence to existence (ibdāʿ). This sets the baseline for the argument.
2
Note the Mu'tazilite inconsistency
The Mu'tazila apply the same meaning to the servant's act but assign power for acquisition (kash) only to the servant, not to God.
This makes the servant's power more comprehensive.
3
Observe the consequence of greater power
Since the servant's power can produce diverse acts while God's is limited according to their reasoning, the servant appears greater in power.
4
Examine the nature vs. choice classification
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila classify beings with one type of act as having nature (ṭabāʿ) and those with two acts as having choice (ikhtiyār), but apply this inconsistently to God and the servant.
5
Conclude the absurdity and affirmation
Denying that God creates acts leads to these contradictions, which both reason and revelation reject. Therefore, God is the Creator of all acts.
The phrase 'There is no power except with God' concludes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن معنى فعل الله هو الإبداع والإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود، وصيرت المعتزلة ذلك معنى فعل العبد؛ ثم جعلت للعبد قدرة على الكسب، ولم تجعل الله؛ فصار العبد بذلك أعظم في القدرة، إذ هي تقع على مختلف الأمر من الله؛ إذ قدرته ترجع إلى أحد الوجهين ومما يبين ذلك أن كل شيء فعله نوع واحد جعلوه طباعا، ومن كان فعلين جعلوه اختيارا عن قدرة، فيجب في الأول كذلك. وذلك هو الحق عند المعتزلة؛ لأنهم يجعلون للعبد قدرة على منع الرب عن فعله فيما ينفي الخيرة عنه، ولا يجعلون مثله الله إلا أن يذهب قدرة العبد. وإذا ثبت أن في نفي خلق الأفعال تحقيق ذلك ـ وذلك مما يأباه العقل والسمع جميعا ـ ثبت أن الله خالق الأفعال كلها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, the meaning of God's act is origination (al-ibdāʿ) and bringing forth from non-existence to existence. The Muʿtazila made that the meaning of the servant's act; then they assigned to the servant a power for acquisition (kash) and did not assign it to God. So the servant thereby became greater in power, since it [the servant's power] extends to diverse matters, while God's power returns to one of two aspects. And among what clarifies that is that every thing of which the act is one kind, they made it nature (ṭabāʿ); and whoever has two acts, they made it choice (ikhtiyār) by power, so it must be the same in the first. And that is the truth according to the Muʿtazila; because they assign to the servant a power to prevent the Lord from His act in what negates choice for Him, and they do not assign the like to God unless the servant's power ceases. And if it is established that in negating the creation of acts is the realization of that — and that is something that both reason and revelation reject — then it is established that God is the Creator of all acts. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is the Creator of all human actions. Maturidi begins by defining God's act as bringing something from non-existence into existence. He notes that the Muʿtazila applied this same meaning to the servant's act, yet they assigned to the servant a power for acquisition (kash) but not to God. This makes the servant's power more comprehensive than God's, since the servant's power can produce diverse acts, while God's power is limited to one of two aspects according to their own reasoning. He then points out a contradiction: the Muʿtazila classify single-act beings as having a nature (ṭabāʿ) and beings with two acts as having choice by power, but they apply this inconsistently to God and the servant. In reality, the Muʿtazila believe the servant can prevent God from acting in matters that negate God's choice, while they do not allow the same for God unless the servant's power ceases. Thus, denying that God creates acts leads to these absurdities, which both reason and revelation reject. Therefore, it is established that God is the Creator of all acts.

Key Terms
فعل
إبداع
قدرة
كسب
طباع
اختيار
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Maturidi's argument is merely philosophical without scriptural basis.
Correction: Maturidi grounds his argument in both rational consistency and revealed texts, as he states that reason and revelation both reject the Mu'tazilite position.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the meaning of God's act?
A) It is the same as the servant's act
B) It is origination and bringing from non-existence to existence
C) It is merely choice (ikhtiyār)
D) It is a kind of acquisition (kash)

Key Takeaway
God is the Creator of all acts, and any contrary view leads to logical and theological absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God creates every action you do, how does that change your attitude when you succeed at something? Do you still feel proud as if you accomplished it yourself? What would it look like to immediately attribute that success to God?
?
The Mu'tazila felt that giving God control over all actions would diminish human responsibility. Do you sometimes resist submitting to God's control over your life because you want to feel in charge? What would it take for you to surrender that feeling today?
?
Maturidi ends with 'There is no power except with God.' When you face a situation that seems beyond your strength, do you genuinely feel your powerlessness and turn to God, or do you try to solve it yourself first? Answer honestly: what is your default response?

The Mu'tazila Objection and Maturidi's Response on Divine Creation of Acts
The Mu'tazila objection and the Maturidi refutation with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection that if God creates human acts, then His commands would be directed to Himself, and how Imam al-Maturidi refutes this by showing that commanding an act is not the same as commanding its creation.

Core Idea
Commands are about actions, not about creating those actions.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God creates all human acts, then His commands and prohibitions become absurd—they would be commands to Himself. Imam al-Maturidi answers by drawing a sharp distinction between commanding an act and commanding its creation. In this passage, he uses a dilemma to expose the flaw in the Mu'tazila's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila claim that if God creates acts, then He commands Himself. What is the reasoning behind that? Reflect on why they think commanding and creating are tied.
This is a key point: they assume that commanding an act implies the act is not created by God.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and the English translation. Pay attention to the two-pronged response: the question about whether God commands servants to create faith.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer, then the dilemma.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Maturidi asks the objector: do you believe God commands creation of faith? If yes, that implies people are creators, which is shirk. If no, the objection collapses. Understand both branches.
The dilemma forces the objector to either commit to polytheism or abandon the objection.
4
Consider the final point
Maturidi then asks about faith and disbelief: are they not accidents and movements? This further supports that acts are created by God, not by humans.
This reinforces the idea that human acts are created entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتج من يأبى القول به في خلق الأفعال: أول شيء أنهم أمروا بها ونهوا عنها وذكروا الآيات في الأمر بها والنهي عنها. ولو جعلناها خلقا له لكان يصير كأنه أمر نفسه ونهى عن خلق ذلك. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: فيقال لمن احتج به: أتقول أمر العبد بخلق الإيمان ونحوه، ونهى عن خلق الكفر ونحوه؟ فإن قال: بلى، صرح بأن الله تعالى أمر الناس أن يكونوا خالقين، وقد أبي المسلمون أن يكون غيره خالقا، ولم يختلف المسلمون في جواز عبادة الخالق مطلقا، وأن الخالق هو الرب وهو الإله؛ فيجب بهذا جعل كل عبد كذلك، وذلك مما أباه الجميع. وإن قال: لا، قيل: فإذ لم يوجب الأمر بالفعل والنهي عنه أمرا بالخلق ونهيا عنه لم قلت: إنه لو كان الله خالق ذلك لوجب الأمر له والنهي عنه، ولم يثبت من الوجه الذي فيه الأمر والنهي أمر بالخلق وغيره؟ ثم يقال له: حدثنا عن الإيمان والكفر، هل يخلوان من أن يكونا شيئين عرضين، وحركتين دليلين على حدث الفاعل، وحجتين على حكمة الرجل وسفهه ومظهرى علمه وجهله؟ لا
Then he who refuses the statement concerning the creation of acts argued: The first thing is that they [people] are commanded and forbidden regarding them, and they mentioned the verses about commanding and forbidding them. If we made them His creation, it would become as if He commanded Himself and forbade the creation of that. Al-Faqīh, may God have mercy on him, said: It is said to him who argues with this: Do you say that God commanded the servant to create faith and the like, and forbade creating disbelief and the like? If he says: Yes, he explicitly states that God, may He be exalted, commanded people to be creators, and Muslims have rejected that anyone other than Him is a creator, and Muslims have not disagreed concerning the permissibility of worshiping the Creator absolutely, and that the Creator is the Lord and the God; so by this it would be necessary to make every servant like that, and that is something rejected by everyone. If he says: No, it is said: Since the command to do the act and the prohibition of it do not necessitate a command to create or a prohibition of it, why did you say: If God were the creator of that, it would necessitate commanding Him and forbidding Him? And it is not established from the aspect that contains command and prohibition a command to create or other than that. Then it is said to him: Tell us about faith and disbelief: are they free from being two accidental things, two movements indicating the temporal origination of the doer, and two proofs of a man's wisdom and foolishness, and manifesters of his knowledge and ignorance? No.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Muʿtazila against the doctrine that God creates human acts. Their objection is: If God creates all acts, then when He commands good and forbids evil, it would be as if He is commanding Himself and forbidding Himself, which is absurd. Maturidi replies through 'Al-Faqīh' (likely the author himself). The answer is structured as a dilemma. First, ask the objector: Do you say that God commands the servant to create faith and forbids creating disbelief? If the objector says yes, then he is saying God commands people to be creators, which the Muslims have unanimously rejected, for only God is Creator and worthy of worship. If he says no, then the argument collapses: since the command to do an act is not the same as a command to create it, why would it be necessary that if God creates the act, He would be commanding Himself? They are two different things. Maturidi then challenges: consider faith and disbelief—are they not accidents and movements that indicate the temporal nature of the agent and manifest his wisdom or foolishness? This further shows that the act is created by God and the servant acquires it. The passage ends abruptly but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
خلق
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila objection is strong and that it disproves God creating human acts.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the objection conflates commanding an action with creating it, and that accepting it leads to polytheism or is irrelevant.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of Maturidi's answer to the Mu'tazila's objection?
A) He denies that God commands or forbids
B) He asks whether the objector believes God commands people to create faith
C) He argues that God's command is metaphorical
D) He says the objection is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection conflates commanding an action with creating it; Maturidi's distinction preserves both divine creation and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God, do you feel pride as if you personally created that obedience? How does realizing that God creates the act while you acquire it change your sense of gratitude and humility?
?
Think of a specific sin you struggle with. Do you ever despair because you feel you are the sole creator of that sin? How does knowing that God creates even your sin affect the way you repent and rely on His mercy?
?
Consider a command from Allah that you find difficult. Do you mistakenly think that because God commanded it, He must do it for you without your effort? How does the distinction between command and creation push you to act while trusting in His provision?
