Why Observation Cannot Distinguish Divine from Human Action
Maturidi presents an objection about distinguishing God's action and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the cause of an action is not empirically knowable and thus cannot be used to distinguish God's action from human action.

Core Idea
Observation never reveals the true cause; only God knows it.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles a common objection: if we see two identical motions, why can't we tell which is from God and which from a human? He shows that the cause is hidden—it could be an angel, a devil, or an animal—so empirical observation alone cannot differentiate. This lesson walks through the objection, Maturidi's response, and the analogy he uses to clarify his point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponent claims that if two reeds move identically, one must search for the cause to distinguish God's action from another's.
Notice that the opponent assumes cause is discoverable by observation.
2
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi counters that the cause is unknowable—it could be an angel, devil, or animal. Therefore, the similarity of the effects cuts off any path to certain knowledge.
He also criticizes the opponent's view as implying God cannot do away with what He created.
3
Examine the analogy
Maturidi compares the argument to someone trying to distinguish the eternal from the temporal without proper consideration. Both attempts fail due to hidden causes.
This reinforces the point that empirical evidence alone is insufficient.
4
Reflect on the key lesson
Consider how this argument defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that divine actions are not distinguishable by empirical means, preserving God's transcendence.
This lesson has implications for causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض قول خصمه أن من عاين أعلى القصبتين تتحركان لا يفصل بين التي يحركها الله والتي يحركها آخر ثبت أنهما تشابها، فزعم أنه يجب الفصل بينهما بالبحث عن السبب. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : يقال له : لعل ملكا يحرك أو شيطانا أو دابة تحت الأرض، فأي سبب له يصل به إلى ما الله حقيقة دون ما لأحد من الخلق لا يعلمه. ليُعلم أن الله عندهم لا يقدر أن يذهب بما خلق. وليعلم أنه لشدة التشابه انقطع سبيل العلم به من حيث نفسه، على أن السبب ليس يفصل عنده فيما كان من الله، إذ ليس غير الذي يعاينه، فأنى يعرف ذلك؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال : وهذا كمستدل بالشاهد، ربما يعجز عن الفصل بين القديم والحديث بما لم ينظر من وجهه، فمثله الأول.
Then he objected to the statement of his opponent: 'He who observes the tops of two reeds moving does not distinguish between that which God moves and that which another moves, given that they are similar. So he claims that distinguishing between them must be by searching for the cause.' The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: It is said to him: Perhaps an angel moves it, or a devil, or an animal underground. So what cause can he attain to [know] what is truly from God, apart from that which is from any of the creation, which he does not know? Let it be known that God, in their view, is not able to do away with what He created. And let it be known that due to the intensity of similarity, the path to knowledge of it is cut off from its own perspective. Moreover, the cause does not distinguish for him in what is from God, since there is nothing other than what he observes. So how can he know that? There is no power except with God. He said: This is like one who argues from the witnessed; perhaps he is unable to distinguish between the eternal and the temporal due to not having considered it from its proper aspect. So the analogy is the same as the first.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response regarding the impossibility of distinguishing God's action from human action solely by observation. The objector argues that if one sees two reeds moving, one cannot tell which is moved by God and which by another, so one must investigate the cause. Maturidi responds that the cause is unknowable because it could be an angel, a devil, or an animal—none of which are apparent. Thus, the path to knowledge of the true cause is cut off due to the similarity of the effects. He adds that demanding such a distinction is like asking someone to distinguish the eternal from the temporal without proper consideration; the analogy is invalid because the cause itself is unobservable. The passage defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that divine actions are not distinguishable by empirical means alone.

Key Terms
القصبتين
السبب
الله حقيقة
فعل العبد
المستدل بالشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two events appear identical, their causes must be identical or at least discoverable by observation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the cause could be any unseen entity (angel, devil, animal), so observation alone cannot reveal the true cause. The similarity of effects does not guarantee knowledge of the cause.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can't one distinguish between God's action and a human's action just by observing the motion?
A) Because both actions are identical.
B) Because the cause is hidden and only God knows it.
C) Because humans cannot see motion.
D) Because God's action is not conceivable.

Key Takeaway
Empirical observation cannot distinguish divine from human action because the true cause is hidden, and only God knows it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe events in your life—success, hardship, a 'coincidence'—do you assume you know the true cause? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to admit your ignorance and turn to God as the only knower of the unseen?
?
Have you ever been quick to attribute an action entirely to human effort or entirely to divine intervention? How does this lesson call you to humility about your judgments and to rely on God's knowledge over your own observation?
?
What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that 'the path to knowledge of it is cut off' for most events? Would you worry less about controlling outcomes and trust God more? What step will you take today to live by this truth?

First Argument: No Path to the Cause
Maturidi presents the first of two refutations of the objector's analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument that the objector's demand for a distinguishing cause is meaningless because there is no empirical path to such knowledge.

Core Idea
Without a visible distinguishing mark, the cause of an action remains unknowable.

Maturidi begins his two-fold refutation by questioning the very possibility of knowing the cause in the hypothetical case the objector presents. He argues that since all actions appear similar, demanding a causal distinction is futile.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the main claim.
Notice the opening phrase 'وذلك عليه لوجهين' which signals two arguments.
2
Identify the first argument
Find the sentence that begins with 'أحدهما'. This is the first of the two refutations.
3
Understand why the demand is meaningless
Reflect on the reason Maturidi gives: there is no way to know the cause because what is from God is not empirically distinct from what is from creatures.
This undermines the objector's assumption that a distinguishing mark exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وذلك عليه لوجهين أحدهما أن لا سبيل في الأول إلى السبب والعلم به في الحقيقة، مع ما ليس من الله غير الذي نراه ليُعلم به، فلا معنى لهذا. الثاني أن الذي عارض به لا يجوز أن يكون على جهة واحدة تدل على أن عدم معرفة الحدث والقدم إنما هو في أنه لم ينظر إليه وأغفل عنه حيث لم ير موضع الدلالة. وما نحن فيه ليس ثمة ما يفصل، إن كان فهو في غيره. ثبت أنها بحيث أنفسهما شبيهان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: And that [refutation] is against him in two respects. First: There is no path in the first [case] to the cause and knowledge of it in reality, since what is from God is nothing other than what we see by which to know it. So this [demand] has no meaning. Second: That with which he objected is not allowed to be on a single basis indicating that the lack of knowledge of the temporal and the eternal is only due to his not having considered it and neglected it, having not seen the place of the indication. But in our case, there is nothing that distinguishes; if there were, it would be in something else. It is established that they are similar in themselves. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage classifies two arguments refuting the objector's analogy. The first argument: In the hypothetical case (the two reeds), there is no way to know the true cause because what is from God is not empirically distinguishable from what is from creatures. The objector's demand for a distinguishing mark is meaningless. The second argument: The objector's analogy is invalid because his claim that failure to distinguish arises from neglect of the evidence does not apply here; in our case, there is simply no distinguishing factor to be found. If there were such a factor, it would be in something else external to the actions themselves. The passage reinforces the Maturidi position that divine and creaturely actions are inherently similar in their observable aspect, and distinction is only possible through revealed knowledge.

Key Terms
الوجه
السبب
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector's reasoning is logically sound because if two things appear similar, it must be possible to find a distinguishing cause if we look hard enough.
Correction: Maturidi argues that in reality there is no empirical path to such knowledge because divine and creaturely actions are not distinct in their observable aspect. The demand is meaningless.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Maturidi gives for the first argument?
A) The objector's evidence is weak.
B) There is no path to knowledge of the cause in the first case.
C) The objector neglected to look for the cause.
D) The two reeds are not actually similar.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first argument asserts that without a visible distinguishing mark, the demand for a causal distinction is meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness events in your life, do you automatically attribute them to worldly causes (people, circumstances) without acknowledging the hidden Hand of God? This argument forces you to ask: Are you so sure you know the true cause of what you see?
?
How often do you trust what your eyes show you over what God has decreed? Let the ayat of the Quran teach you that the ultimate cause is unseen. Will you today repent for relying on visible means alone?
?
Consider a situation where you were proud of a success or ashamed of a failure. Did you look for the cause only within yourself? Maturidi's argument challenges you to admit that without a divine marker, you have no real knowledge. Will you submit to this humbling truth?

Second Argument: No Distinguishing Factor Exists
Maturidi presents the second refutation, targeting the objector's assumption about neglect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that the objector's assumption about neglect of evidence is wrong because there is simply no distinguishing factor to be found.

Core Idea
Failure to distinguish arises from the absence of a distinguishing factor, not from neglecting evidence.

Having established that the objector's demand is meaningless, Maturidi now attacks the objector's underlying assumption: that the failure to distinguish is due to not looking properly. Instead, Maturidi argues that no distinguishing factor exists in this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the main claim.
Notice that the second argument begins with 'الثاني'.
2
Identify the objector's assumption
Find the part where Maturidi describes what the objector claims: that lack of knowledge is due to not considering the evidence.
This is the premise Maturidi intends to refute.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi says that in our case there is no distinguishing factor. If there were, it would be in something else. Therefore, the objector's assumption is invalid.
The conclusion 'ثبت أنها بحيث أنفسهما شبيهان' reinforces the similarity of the actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وذلك عليه لوجهين أحدهما أن لا سبيل في الأول إلى السبب والعلم به في الحقيقة، مع ما ليس من الله غير الذي نراه ليُعلم به، فلا معنى لهذا. الثاني أن الذي عارض به لا يجوز أن يكون على جهة واحدة تدل على أن عدم معرفة الحدث والقدم إنما هو في أنه لم ينظر إليه وأغفل عنه حيث لم ير موضع الدلالة. وما نحن فيه ليس ثمة ما يفصل، إن كان فهو في غيره. ثبت أنها بحيث أنفسهما شبيهان. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Faqih, may God have mercy on him, said: And that [refutation] is against him in two respects. First: There is no path in the first [case] to the cause and knowledge of it in reality, since what is from God is nothing other than what we see by which to know it. So this [demand] has no meaning. Second: That with which he objected is not allowed to be on a single basis indicating that the lack of knowledge of the temporal and the eternal is only due to his not having considered it and neglected it, having not seen the place of the indication. But in our case, there is nothing that distinguishes; if there were, it would be in something else. It is established that they are similar in themselves. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage classifies two arguments refuting the objector's analogy. The first argument: In the hypothetical case (the two reeds), there is no way to know the true cause because what is from God is not empirically distinguishable from what is from creatures. The objector's demand for a distinguishing mark is meaningless. The second argument: The objector's analogy is invalid because his claim that failure to distinguish arises from neglect of the evidence does not apply here; in our case, there is simply no distinguishing factor to be found. If there were such a factor, it would be in something else external to the actions themselves. The passage reinforces the Maturidi position that divine and creaturely actions are inherently similar in their observable aspect, and distinction is only possible through revealed knowledge.

Key Terms
القدم والحدث
موضع الدلالة
الشبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two actions look alike, it must be our lack of observation that prevents differentiation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that in this case no differentiating factor exists, so even careful observation would not yield a distinction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the objector's assumption about neglect wrong?
A) The objector did not look carefully enough.
B) There is no distinguishing factor in this case.
C) The objector used the wrong analogy.
D) The cause is not important.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second argument shows that the objector's reasoning is based on a false premise about the nature of ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when you struggle to distinguish between your own plans and God's guidance, do you ever catch yourself assuming that the lack of clarity is due to your negligence? Based on this lesson, how might that assumption be a form of arrogance? What specific step will you take today to rely more on revelation than on your own reasoning in a decision you are facing?
?
Think of a time when you insisted on finding a rational explanation for a divine decree. Have you been guilty of demanding a sign that God has not provided? In that area of your life, are you willing to accept that the distinction may only come from revelation? Commit to one action of trust in God's wisdom over your own understanding.

The Argument That Motion Does Not Prove Creation
Maturidi argues that if motion is not known as created, then no accident can prove creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that denying that motion is known to be created leads to the collapse of all proofs for creation from accidents.

Core Idea
Denying motion's createdness unravels all proofs for creation.

This lesson presents a critical argument from Imam Maturidi against those who claim that motion itself does not indicate that it is created. By following this reasoning to its logical conclusion, Maturidi shows that such a position would invalidate all accidents as proofs for creation and ultimately undermine the knowledge of substances. He then counters this with the established principle that temporality proves creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure and the logical chain.
The passage begins with a reported statement and then draws out its consequences.
2
Identify the logical consequence
Observe how the claim about motion is extended to all accidents, and then to the impossibility of knowing substances. See how this leads to the conclusion that God would not have established a proof.
Maturidi calls this an extreme statement that even Satan would not conceive.
3
Understand the rebuttal
Maturidi then affirms the agreed principle that bodies are proven created by their temporality. Note how he re-establishes the link between origination and creation.
This counters the initial claim by providing a firm basis for proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبلغ قوله : إنه لا يُعلم بنفس الحركة أنها مخلوقة؛ فيجب هذا في كل عرض نحو الجمع والتفريق، فيبطل أن يكون في شيء من ذلك دلالة خلقه . ثم لا سبيل إلى معرفة حقيقة الأعيان بدونها. فكأن الله لم يُقم دلالة على خلقه بكون تلك بالله دون خلقه أبدا. وذلك قول لم يتوهمه الشيطان، لعله أن أحدا من أوليائه يبلغ بطاعته إياه هذا المبلغ . نسأل الله العصمة ذلك. وعلى قوله إن الشيء لا يدل على الله، إنما يدل - إذا علم سببه - إسقاطه الدلالة عن الأجسام من أن يُعرف بها الله سبحانه . قال : ومن عظيم ما أجمعوا عليه أن دليل خلق الجسم حدثه، فكذلك كل محدث يدفع إلى هذا. وسأل الدليل، وأيد ذلك بما يجوز أن يعرفه محدثًا من لا يعرف خلقا.
Then it reached his statement: 'It is not known by the motion itself that it is created; therefore this applies to every accident such as combination and separation, so it invalidates that there is in any of that a proof of its creation. Then there is no way to know the reality of substances without them. So it is as if God did not establish a proof of His creation by the fact that those are from God without His creation, ever. And that is a statement that Satan did not imagine, perhaps that one of his allies would reach this level through obedience to him. We ask God for protection from that. And according to his saying that a thing does not indicate God, it only indicates—when its cause is known—dropping the indication from bodies that God may be known through them. He said: One of the great things they agreed upon is that the proof of the creation of a body is its temporality; so likewise every originated thing leads to this. And he asked for the proof, and supported that by what allows that one who does not know creation may know it as originated.'
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against those who deny that motion is known to be created. Maturidi reports a claim that it is not known through the motion itself that it is created; if that is accepted, then the same applies to all accidents like combination and separation, thus no accident can serve as a proof of being created. Consequently, the reality of substances cannot be known, and it would seem that God has not provided any proof of creation through these things. Maturidi calls this an extreme statement that even Satan would not conceive. He then mentions the counter-argument: a thing does not directly indicate God; rather, when its cause is known, it directs towards the Creator. He cites the agreed principle that a body's creation is proven by its temporality, and every originated thing points to that. The passage ends with a request for proof and an attempt to support the view that one can know an originated thing without knowing its creator.

Key Terms
حركة (motion)
عرض (accident)
دلالة (proof/indication)
حدوث (temporality/origination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion itself is perceived as created, but the argument here is about the epistemological claim that motion does not inherently reveal its being created.
Correction: The passage is not about whether motion is actually created, but about whether we can know it is created from motion alone. Maturidi argues that denying this knowledge leads to the collapse of all such proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, what happens if it is not known that motion is created?
A) All accidents then prove creation
B) No accident can be a proof of creation
C) Substances become known directly
D) God establishes a different proof

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that rejecting the createdness of motion undermines all creation proofs from accidents, affirming the necessity of recognizing temporality as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any aspect of the world around you that you treat as though it does not point to its Creator? What would be the consequences if you followed that thought to its logical end, as Maturidi warns?
?
This passage shows that denying one small truth (motion's createdness) can unravel the entire fabric of proof. Where in your own faith are you tempted to compromise on a small truth, thinking it harmless?
?
Maturidi asks God for protection from such a statement. Have you ever prayed for protection from a wrong belief before it fully took root? What belief might you need to pray about today?
